The Red Page
Misery, Muck, Cesspool and
Slaughter-house
Of that 500-mile-long slaughter-house and
cesspool combined, which is called “the
Front,” E. Alexander Powell, author of
Fighting -in Flanders, now publishes the best
description yet given. Vive La France!
(Heineutaun, 3s. 6d.) is practically a pic-
ture of a peaceful country-side transformed
by hums* agency into “a garbage heap, a
cesspool and a charnel house,” or, in two
words, “misery and muck.” And the veil
of secrecy that the Allies have di-awn
over their military operations! Localising
Powell’s example, imagine a battle in which
more men were engaged and the results of
which were more momentous than Waterloo,
Gettysburg and Sedan combined being fought
at Eubbo in December, and the people of
Sydney not knowing the details of that
battle, the names of the regiments engaged,
the losses, or, indeed, the actual result, until
the following March! That is what hap-
pened in this-war.
Powell’s first glance at the new French
uniform: “The far end of the valley was
filled with what appeared at first glance to
be a low-hanging cloud of grey-blue mist, but
which, as we drew nearer, I'esolved itself into
dense masses of troops drawn up in review’
formation.” Powell has changed his mind
about the comparative invisibility of the Ger-
man greenish-grey uniform. The French is
still less visible. “At 300 yards 20,000 men
men could scarcely be distinguishable from
the landscape.”
The difficulty of an allied offensive is
frankly stated. “The opposing armies are
not simply entrenched. They have pro-
tected themselves with masses of concrete
and steel armor, so that the so-called trenches
are in reality concrete forts, shielded and
casemated with armor plate, flanked with
rapid-firers and mortars, linked to 'one
another hy mai’vellously concealed communi-
cating trenches, w r hich are protected in turn by
the fire of heavy batteries, guarded by the most
ingenious entanglements.... and defended
by machine guns, in the enormous proportion
of one to every 50 men, mounted behind
steel plates and capable of firing 600 shots a
minute... .That is the sort of wall wririch one
side or the other will have to break throxxgh
in order to win this war; and the only way
to take such a position is by frontal attack.”
J*
This acute observer, given every facility
to see the real thing along the 500-mile fron-
tier cesspool, is not. quite so certain that
we are going to win, or at least with the
ease prophesied by our local politicians.
“The British soldier has at last come to
realise the terrible gravity of the situation
which faces him. You don’t hear him sing-
ing ‘Tipperary’ any more, or boasting about
w’hat he is going to do w’hen he gets to
Berlin. He has come to have a most pro-
found respect for the fighting qualities of
the men in the spiked helmets. He knows
that he, an amateur boxer, is up against the
world’s heavyweight professional champion,
and he perfectly appreciates that he has, to
use his own expression, ‘a hell of a job’ in
front of him....He had alx*eady found out,
to his cost and to his very great disgust, that
his opponent has no intention of being ham-
pered by Marquis of Queensberry rules.
But one of these days, w’hen he gets quite
ready, the British soldier is going to give
that opponent the surprise of his life by
landing on him with both feet, spikes on his
shoes and brass knuckles on his fingers.”
But Powell definitely states that he
doesn’t believe (1) that the British Army
is the most efficient killing machine in exist-
ence, and (2) that its complete and early
triumph is assured. “Neither of which asser-
tions would be true.” The British only
began to build their w’ar-machine when the
Huns had completed theirs. And when we
have finished our machine, after the war, it
will hai-dly resemble at all the machine we
had started to build. “After this war is
over the British Army will no longer be
officered by the British aristocracy. The
wholesale promotions of enlisted men made
necessary by the appalling losses among the
officers will result in completely changing
the complexion of the British military
establishment.” In a word, it will closely
approximate to the Australian Army.
Emphasising the awful effects of high
explosives, Powell asks: “Is it any wonder
that hundreds of officers and men are going
insane from the strain that they are under,
and that hundreds more are in the hospitals
suffering from neuritis and nervous break-
down? Is it any wonder that, when their
term in the trenches is over, they have to
be taken out of sight and sound of battle
and their shattered nerves restored by means
of a carefully-planned routine of sports and
games, as though they w’ere children in a
kindergarten?”
What do the French think of the English?
The replies that Powell got to his frequent
inquiry were not excessively flattering.
“They regard her lack of military prepared-
ness and the deficiency of technique among
the British officers as inexcusable; they con-
sider the deep-seated opposition to conscrip-
tion in England as incomprehensible; they
view the bickerings between British capital
and labor as little short of criminal; they
regard the British officers who needlessly
expose themselves as being not heroic but
insane." Powell heard a Frenchman say to
an Englishman, “To listen to you English
and to read your newspapers, one would
think that there was no one in France ex-
cept the British Army and a few Germans.”
The British soldier of the new army isn’t
at all like the traditional rollicking, reckless
Tommy Atkins. He has not joined for a
lark. He has joined because he felt he ought
to. “He knows that the headlines which
appear regularly in the. English papers
announcing ’another British advance are-
generally buncombe. He knows that it isn't
a question of advancing but of hanging on.
He knows that he will have to fight with
every ounce of fight there is in him if he
is to remain where he is now. He knows
that before the Germans can be driven out
of France and Belgium, much less across
the Rhine, all England will be wearing
crape. He knows that there is no truth in
the reports that the enemy is weakening
He knows that it is going to be a long
war—probably a very long war indeed....
Out along the battle-front they are not sing-
ing much, they are not boasting at all, but
they have settled down to the herculean
task that lies before them with a grim deter-
mination, a bull-dog tenacity of purpose,
which is eventually, I believe, going to
prove the deciding factor in the war.”
And in the Vosges this American corres-
pondent saw what trench life in the cess-
pool was really like. “War is not only
fighting. Bronchitis is more deadly than
bullets. Pneumonia does more harm than
poison-gas. Shells are less dangerous than
lack of sanitation. To be attacked by sti’ange
and terrible diseases ; to stand day after day,
week after week, between walls of oozy mud
and amid seas of slime; to be eaten alive by
vermin; to suffer the intolerable irritation
of the itch; to be caked with mud and filth;
to go for weeks and perhaps months with
no opportunity to bathe; to be so foul of
person that you are an offence to all who
come near—such are the real horrors of the
trench.” Misery and muck !
Jt
Of coui’se, the German does not fight fair.
He used to have a habit, when lying
wounded, of shooting the French soldiers in
the back. Now, when the Foreign Legion
goes into action, each company is followed
by men with axes, whose business it is to
see that such incidents do not happen again.
Powell found no evidence of the British
belief that the Hun regiments are now made
up of young lads and elderly men. The
immense majority of the prisoners he saw
were men between 18 and 40, in the very
pink of physical condition —but they didn’t
seem at all averse to being taken prisoner.
Powell mentions a great number of ingeni-
ous expedients that in this close trench
fighting the combatants have invented for
assault and defence. The Germans are said
to dig shallow pools outside their trenches,
cement the bottom, and fill them with acid,
masked by boughs or straw. A soldier
stumbling into the pool would have his feet
burned off. Where the trenches are close,
the men roll over the tops of their trenches
in front great spirals of heavy telegraph
wire, against which the ordinary artillery
bombardment or the soldier’s wire-cutters
are of little avail. “The French troops now
frequently advance to the assault carrying
huge rolls of thick linoleum, which is un-
rolled and thrown across the entanglements,
thus forming a sort of bridge by means of
which the attacking force is enabled to cross'
the river of barbed wire in front of the
German trenches.” Scene-painters from the
French theatres have been mobilised in order
to paint scenery for the purpose of masking
gun-positions v Fowell has actually seen guns
thus masked —in a sort of tent, which is
painted to harmonise with the scenery. Open
roads are thus cleverly screened, painted to
look like a row of trees; but the precaution
must be taken of watering the road, or the
dust of the traffic would betray the contriv-
ance to the enemy. “Both sides have long
been daubing field-guns and caissons and
other bulky equipment with all the colors
of the rainbow, like a futurist picture, so
that they assume the properties of a chame-
leon and become indistinguishable from the
landscape. Now they are painting the faces
of the snipers, and splashing their uniforms
and rifle-barrels with many colors and tying
to their heads wisps of grass and foliage.”
A new use for Futurists! Why shouldn’t
we mobilise our own artists ? Lister Lister’s
gum-trees would deceive the cutest Hun.
This war always seems to find the Allies
caught napping. First it was big guns, and
then shells. Ploddingly we set to work to
catch up with these indispensable articles;
but by the time we are ready, we shall
probably find that the Huns are tired of
shells and have invented something much
better. . Then we will set to work to catch
up again—only to find that the Huns have
gone one better. The latest method of the
Huns is vitriol throwing. Powell vouches
for this assertion. The French caught one
of the fiends—and didn’t shoot him. “You
see, monsieur, we French are civilised.”
In this vivid volume, Powell writes the
most thrilling account of the most thrilling
struggle in history—the French advance in
Champagne, last September. And after it
was over, he says: “The thing of which the
Champagne battle-field most reminded me
was a garbage-heap. It looked and smelled
as though, all the garbage cans in Europe
and America had been emptied upon it....
The captured German trenches presented the
most horrible sight that I have ever seen or
ever expect to see.” It was 15 miles of
garbage, chiefly human.
Finally he tells us of the most novel of all
the uses to which air-craft have been put in
this war. That is, to drop spies in the
enemy’s territory. Our aviators frequently
fly across the German lines, and drop in
some secluded place an intelligence officer dis-
guised as a farm-hand. A few days later
the aviator returns to the same spot to pick
the spy up. “The exploits of some of these
secret agents thus dropped from the sky
upon enemy soil would make the wildest fic-
tion seem tame.”
So, by the way. does Powell’s faithful
account of the modern misery and muck that
v is called war.
The Anzac Sonnet
There were 71 ways of constructing Anzac
lays, and nearly every one of then! were, more
or less, right. In fact, the ability of the Aus-
tralasian to write sonnets is nearly equal
to his ability to fight. Not more than six
missed the correct sonnet form, though many
entries were fatally flawed by technical errors
of metre, rime, or length of line.
Good, but undistinguished, sonnets were
provided by: R.S.W., R.W.M., A. G. Water-
worth, H.B.Y., Tom Leonard, L. H. Living-
ston, Mary Bayliss, J. F. Jones. . .
Sonnets of a higher quality were received
from: D.G.G., Marcus Gibson, Chas. F. de
Monchaux, A, Finlayson, M. H. Jopling, H.
W. Stewart (2), Ben Sun, E. J. O’C.,
O.N.G., Edward Harrington (who if he is as
youthful as his kindergarten handwriting sug-
gests, has, possibly, a big future before him),
A. Gore Jones and N.G.
H. Stewart obliges with a Turkish point of
view:—•
Seventeen fine sonnets were left in for final
sifting. Y. Tierney is too prosaic, too full
of facts. F. S. Burnell, on the other hand,
might have been writing of any soldier slain
in any war: he failed to localise his well-
expressed theme. “Jimdick” makes the minor
mistake of an inner rime, fatal to the son-
net-reader’s sensitive ear, “And made that
final grand despairing stand,” and also loses
himself in grandiloquent phrases. One of
G. K. Soward’s variants shows itself inferior
to the other, reserved for further adjudica-
tion. George R. Hambidge ends on an
original note, but there are crudities in ex-
pression and a little confusion of time and
locality. A.A.N., with a brilliant, but not
perfect, sestette, makes, too obviously, his
ideas depend on his rimes in his octette.
R. A. Broinowski tells:—
How hero-men came from the Southern seas
With sunlight in their blood, and graved the name
Australia on the scroll of deeds sublime.
but the rest of his imagery is conventional.
“The thorny laurels of Gallipoli,” of which
“AEneas Hayseed” finely sings, is preceded by
some halting lines, such as “To climb and
pant with blood-stained hands to cling,” in
which the interior assonance of “pant*’ and
“hands ’ annoys the ear, and there is some
confusion in the author’s expression of his
meaning. S. Elliott Napier’s sonnet is fine,
but not so fine as his second try, to be con-
sidered later. E. H. Russell spoils her stir-
ring sestette with one padded-out line, in
which, in search of a rime, she informs us
that Australia “teems with wealth of wine
and corn” —a fact intrusive in her theme.
Seven splendid sonnets remain. They all
merit publication, and will appear later on
this page. Lilia Gormhuille McKay just
spoils by a conventional final line her artistic
sonnet. Both Mary A. K. Robb and J.L.B.
deserve the prize, were the level of the entries
not so high. Their sonnets are admirably
wrought, without palpable defect, but also
without the special excellencies of the others.
C. E. Moore's sonnet runs through a .
t ff co “ ventloEal ™t to a noble close t
Elliott. Napier neatly expresses a
which is inherent m many of the InX
sent in; but the rest of his sonnet k * ets
what artificial; his pulse h as nS T*
stirred. not bee »
The three sonnets remaining malm ni, •
extremely difficult. g ke ehoic e
So Bartlett Adamson (Box 1536, G.P.O.
Wellington, M.L.) wins with this example
of fine craftsmanship, lacking the thrill con-
veyed by G. K. Soward’s splendid vision, as
stern and arresting as the cliffs of Anzac.
Bartlett Adamson’s sonnet is too pellucid,
too smooth, almost too complacent. But it
is excellent work.
From the New York Times :—
A.B.M.—The poem, “Where the West Begins,” was
written by Arthur Chapman, a young Western poet,
and was originally published in the Denver Republi-
can. It is as follows: —
Out where the handclasp’s a little stronger,
Out where a smile dwells a little longer,
That’s where the West begins.
Out where the sun’s a little brighter,
Where the snow that falls is a trifle wlmcr.
Where the bonds of home are a wee bit tighter,
That’s where the West begins.
Out where the skies are a trifle bluer,
Out where friendship’s a little truer,
That’s where the West begins.
Out where a fresher breeze is blowing, , t
Where there is laughter in every strt
Where there’s more of reaping and less of sowing,
That’s where the West begins.
Out where the world is in the making.
Where fewer hearts with despair are aching-
That’s where the West begins.. 0 f
Where there is more of singing ana i®»
Where tlferels mare of giving and less of
And a man makes friends without half t.
That’s where the West begins.

Barcroft Boake’s “Where the Dead ,
Lie” and adapted it. That’s what happen
to Bracken’s “Not Understood, B
“Amateur Rider,” etc., in U.S.A.
They came; ’twas Allah’s will that they should land,
And so he turned our bullets in their flight,
And by Mahomet’s sacred the fight
Was a good fight, for these were men: the sand
Ran red with blood; for doubtless Allah planned
That many True Believers should delight
In all the joys of Paradise that night—•
And doubtless many Infidels be damned.
They lied, those German pigs (May dogs defile
Their graves !). They said that it was written we
Should capture many men, and every gun;
And they were Spirited away the while
We"slept; the sun rose on an empty sea.
Kismet! Kismet 1 May Allah’s will be done 1
“Mick Much” just misses with a sonnet
in the language of sentimental blokes. “Bill
the Goose.” though he meets his Gallipoli in
the sonnet form, provides the best line pro-
duced by this competition. His concoction
is x-eprinted, partly as an awful warning to
inefficient sonneteers, but chiefly for his final
line.
The silent ship is sneakin’ through the night—
The parson bloke has give us all the ’ump;
We sees the beach, and in the water jump.
The flamin’ barbed-wire tangles holds us tight.
A shell screams out. Then Hell breaks loose, en’ twict
I stops a pill, but, ’struth, when we gets near,
I drives me bayonet through some poor cow’s ear.
Me cobber laughs, then drops en’ gurgles “Christ!”
I does me block with the warm blood stench,
En’ fights like ’Ell till me eye
Runs down me face, en’ I drops in the trench....
Australians don’t moan nor cry ;
But thinkin’ makes me two ’ands clench.
Anzac 1 Where me cobbers lie!
M. S. Wallace gives us good Australian,
though he does rime “saw” and “war.”
(There was one competitor who revived the
ancient rime of “failure” with “Australia,”
and another who made “war” rime with
“immortal”).
Wot’s this ’ere chat erbout Gallipoli
An’ Anzac ’eroes, an’ ther clarssic shore?
Yer’d think they’d never seen a Man before
Ter ’ear ’em fork. Jist wot it seems ter me
’Oo’s bin, an’ knows it worn’t no victerey,
But jist the lousiest stretch uv bleedin’ war.
Is this—they wuz ther best I ever saw,
But none wuz better than I thort ’e’d bel
Real decent blokes wuz with us, real white men,
So p’raps it ain’t tew bad ter ’ear ther skite
That shows yew. coves ’ow blanky good they were.
An’ now, in fewcher, if yew ’ear uv fight,
Uv flamin’, furst-elarss stoush, well then
Yew tell ’em this: “Orstrylier will be there!”

Here's Anzae’s story to the end of Time:—
Full-fledged there rose a Nation in a
night!
One hour and ever fame! That leap on
shore!
Thou khaki figure rushing up the height
Art note Australia! Voice and bronze and
rhyme
Shall hail and limn thee so for evermore!
G. K. SOWARD.
Guy Innes has an awkward “witnesseth”
necessary for metre and rime, and he has a
fatal love for alliteration; but his conclusion
is splendid.
Baptised hy fire and here confirmed by death
Their travail baffled hy the bloody stair
That led to glory's altar, these made fair
Australia's honor with their dying breath,
And passed with faith fulfilled; as witnesseth
The price that proved their proniise—and for
naught?
Not so—for though they found not what
they sought,
Fate found them fame, and Fame a death-
less wreath.
Though ashes strew their place of sacrifice,
The dead and their undying deeds shall tell—
That those ivho still fight on may understand
And fight on still—of that supreme device
To found eternal on the hills of hell
Australia’s altar in an alien land.
GUY INNES.
ANZAC.
By BARTLETT ADAMSON.
By purple hills and opalescent sea
And sunlit leagues of plain they lived, and
they
Were summery-hearted all, and life teas gay,
And peace was theirs, and love, and liberty.
And when the ' clarion sounded suddenly,
They went, a rollicking hand of hoys at play,
Tilted at doom, and there, at Anzac Bay,
Died....hut they taught the world what
men there he.
And Ansae now is an enchanted shore;
A tragic splendor, and a holy name;
A deed eternity will still acclaim;
A loss that crowns the victories of yore;
A glorious golden dome for evermore
Shining above the minarets of fame.

THE STAR DISC HARROW
THE STAR DISC

on the horses and enab-
ling the operator to turn
his team in their own
length.

THE CANTON DISC PLOUGH has been universally successful for general
use everywhere (in hard, soft, dry, wet, old or new soil).
The Discs are 26in. in diameter, and the Single Disc will turn furrows
from 3 to 12 inches, and the gang ploughs have adjustments of equal dimensions
between each Disc. The Discs have dust-proof boxes, and are set at the proper
angle to revolve freely when in contact with the soil. The soil is turned completely
over and is thoroughly pulverised. Can be adjusted to cut 10 or 12 inches.

all sediment and bad secretions, and makes

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I Had a Garden.
I had a garden high above the town ;
The brave sun smiled on it, the first spring
flowers
Blossomed and shone like jewels in a crown.
I smelled the warm earth moist with
quickening showers.
A tall white lily, like some fair girl saint,
Swayed in an ecstasy of purity ;
And there herbs grew of fragrance sweet
and faint,
While o’er it all laughed breezes from th»
sea.
I had a garden ! God, am I bereft
Of all that sweetness I was wont to
know ?
I stand at gaze—none of its fragrance left!
And still up from the sea the glad winds
blow!
I had a garden high above the town;
You laid it waste, then left it bare and
brown!
N.S.W.
JOHN EGAN.
The Fern-Men.
An you set the new moon in the crotch of a
tree,
An you bide when the day’s at the turn,
On the twist o’ the little grey track you will
see
The little brown men in the fern;
But your eyes must be kind in the front o’
your head
And your heart must be soft for to ken;
And ’tis hush you must keep and ’tis soft
you must tread
If you’d meet with the little brown men.
You must pluck from your ears all o’ bad
they have heard,
You must brush up your coat or your
gown,
For the little brown men will take fright at
a word,
And they hate the drab dust of the town.
You must spread out your sins on the lap o’
the Night
If the brown nimble men you would see,
For your soul must be clean and your heart
must be white
As the moon in the crotch o’ the tree.
If you do all these things and you tarry a
space
And you never give cause for alarm,
You will find a big blossom flung full at your
face
And feel a wee fist on your arm ;
While over the twigs like the steps of the
breeze
From the fronds of the fern and the roots,
The tiny brown men will swarm up on your
knees
Or squat on the ground by your boots.
A score of wee fellows all flowered and
furred
And brown as the bark on the bole,
And each brings a dower of music and w T ord
To weave into songs for the Soul.
And they’ll breathe on your lip the sweet
breath of the earth,
And they’ll fill up your heart as an urn ;
Till it throbs with the warmth and the magic
and mirth
That rustle all day ’mid the fern.
And they’ll bide till the new moon drops
over and down
And the red in the sky shows beyond ;
For they know and take fright at the flare
o’ the town
And they’ll leap for the stalk and the
frond ;
And you’ll know ’twas a wind round the turn
of th’ day,
Or a little grey mouse in the bark,
Or the blithe little men as they rustled away
On their nimble light legs through the
dark.
O the furry fern-men are afraid of the street
And the taint of the Town in the grip,
And the ties of the City which drag at the
feet
And the jargon that lies on the lip!
And ’tis pity the folk in the square and the
mart
Who know not the joys that await;
For the towers of the City crush down on
the heart
And the Soul is nailed up in the crate!
MAORILAND.
BOYCE BOWDEN.
A Memory.
A little tea-room somewhere by the sea,
Glass joybells clashing low and lovingly,
Slight painted things that came from old
Japan,
Dyed pampas grass, a dusty palm-leaf fan ;
Outside the wind blown down a crooked
street,
And sun and ocean whispers, sleepy, sweet.
A wide and shady hotel balcony,
Cape Fingal with a smoke trail on its lee,
A flutt’ring blind some saucy zephyr shook,
A man’s voice reading from an open book;
Or rocky slopes where questing white moths
pass
Above the blue-eyed blossoms in the grass,
And ice-clear breakers thundering below.
O Memory, that sometimes hurts me so,
Upon the throbbing pulse of Loss you lay
The cool white finger of a happy day!
QUEENSLAND.
M. FORREST.
Prayer.
Heaven help poor human folks that are
For discipline by Heaven sent
To be, from dawn to evening^star,
The moralists’ advertisement!
N.S.W.
F. M.

THE TIGER HUNT.
When Dr. Liebknecht, the Socialist leader, was speaking, a number of deputies, greatly excited, demanded his expulsion, and approached hint menacingly.
A deguty mounted the tribone and tore documents of Dr. Liebknect's hand. The President indignantly refused hear Dr. Liehknecht. Several of the
Progressives tried to drag him outside.-Cable.


The Bulletin
The National Australian Newspaper.
•‘Australia for the White Man.”
Printed and published by William Macleod, of
Musgrave and McLeod Streets, Mosman, for The
Bulletin Newspaper Co., Ltd., at the Office of the
Co., 214 George Street, Sydney, N.S.W., Australia.
PUBLISHED ONCE A WEEK.
TERMS TO SUBSCRIBERS.
(In Advance, Only.)
For £1 6s. 6d. per annum you can have The
Bulletin sent regularly to you every week for a
year, including Special Christmas Number, post paid.
Cheques, drafts, etc., which should be crossed, and
all Business Communications to be addressed to The
Manager.
Special Notice.
Insufficiently stamped letters are liable to be re-
fused.
The contents of this paper are copyright by the
publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
part of the contents of this number is hereby ex
pressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor tvill carefully read, and, where prac-
ticable, acknowledge in the “Correspondence” column
all contributions submitted, whether in the form of
Political, Social or other Articles, Verse, Short Tales
or Sketches (those dealing with Australian subjects,
and not exceeding two columns in length, or, say,
3000 words, are specially acceptable), Paragraphs,
Letters or Newspaper Clippings. All communications
will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return ail unsuitable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But
no MS. will be returned, or preserved, unless these
conditions are observed. IFAen MS. is forwarded
without stamps for return it will be understood that
the copy is to be destroyed if not acceptable. Per-
sonal applications for return of MS. cannot receive
attention. The Bulletin desires, further, those
sending MS. to distinctly understand that it is not
responsible for the preservation or transmission of MS.
To Amateurs in Black and White.
Anyone supplying to The Bulletin original
humorous or other topical drawings in line or wash,
of sufficient merit and interest and adapted for
direct reproduction, or any interesting photograph
illustrative of a current event, will be paid for same
on acceptance. Any picture containing a good idea
but unfitted for direct reproduction — i.e., any picture
which has to be re-drawn —will be paid for as a
suggestion only. The conditions under which such
artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS.

THURSDAY, APRIL 27, 1916.
PEACE WITH HARDWARE.
For the 735th time within IS months
someone has been heard explaining that
mechanical appliances are soon to be so
perfect that war will be impossible. On
this occasion it is the British flying-man
Pemberton Billing, lately elected to the
House of Commons as an exhortation to
the Government to get a move on. Thus
the prophetic Billing, whose remarks were
considered worth cabling to Australia :
The development of aeronautics is the forerunner
of universal peace. Britain or another nation will
within 10 or 20 years possess 100,000 airships,
divided into 100 squadrons, capable of devastating
the principal cities of an enemy country in a single
night.
It is an easy vision, this, of a vast covey of
aeroplanes and dirigibles advancing across
the sky to its work of annihilation; but all
sorts of things are missing. For instance,
the calculation appears to be based upon
the theory that, while one lot of people are
accumulating an enormous outfit of aerial
ironmongery, other people will go on living
in cities in the fashion of to-day. It pre-
supposes a world standing still, except in
one particular. If cities can be fortified
against attack sideways, they can also be
made secure against delivery of hostilities
from the perpendicular. The town planners
and engineers have yet to be heard from on
the subject, of course; but even with human
ingenuity as it is, the municipal umbrella
is clearly within the bounds of possibility
and the collapsible city not beyond achieve-
ment. In any case people herd together in
cities only because they have not yet eman-
cipated themselves from bad habits. They
started it mostly because there was safety
in numbers, and continue doing it because
there is cash and convenience of a kind in
numbers now. Once they discover that, in
addition to there being moral and commer-
cial reasons to justify decentralisation, there
is also a physical argument, they will com-
mence to sprinkle themselves over the land-
scape and rub the huge city off the map
under the Social and Military Precautions
Act. When there is only insecurity in
cities there will not be any cities, and that
is all about it. The safest place for man
at one time was up a tree. Later on it was
in a cave. Necessity compelled him to take
up new addresses, and he can do it again.
Cities were under suspicion long before the
warrior thought of attack from the air.
The world is a bit doubtful about where
some of its greatest human ant-heaps were
situated.
* * * *
This idea of a new implement being the
thing that is going to achieve universal
peace on account of its frightfulness is as
old as the memoirs of the man who first
attached a stone to a sinew from the tail of
a dinosaur and brained the unenlightened
savage who came to steal the raw steak
from his pantry. When some forgotten
patriot originally brought an educated
elephant into the game it was reckoned by
his contemporaries that he had settled mat-
ters for all time. But he hadn’t. All he
had done was to make them worse and
encourage the man next door to give his
inventive faculties a chance. The discovery
of gunpowder put what looked like an all-
conquering means of devastation into the
hands of its first proprietors; the repeating
rifle, the bursting shell, the armored war-
ship and all the rest of it represented tre-
mendous advances over the old-fashioned
knife and axe and catapult. But not one
of them made war any less frequent; and
at the end of all the discovery and perfection
of lethal equipment, war at rock-bottom is
still merely the struggle of one man to get
close enough to another to stick a piece of
steel into his innards. From the historical
point of view there is no more reason to
suppose that development of aeronautics
will make fire-eating democracies or
demented autocrats any less inclined to go
out on the warpath with their liigh-sky
fleets and squadrons than the construction
of torpedoes, submarines and Dreadnoughts
has induced them to bolt for the interior.
For every punch there is a block, and while
it may happen that the addition of flying to
the accomplishments of man is destined to
introduce new methods of organised assault
and battery, the betting is at least 100 to 1
that defence against this kind of devasta-
tion will become just as effective as it has
been made against other forms of disorderly
conduct. To this paper it looks far more
likely that expansion of the German’s poison
gas and flame-squirting apparatus will
restrain war than possession of a multitude
of aeroplanes and Zeppelins. If every nation
on earth had a local hell from which by
turning two or three taps a consuming fire
could be projected in any direction required
and for any distance, it is quite probable
that plans for invading the backyard next
door would cease to be fashionable. But
even then a long time would have to pass
before everyone could be convinced that it
was safe to let the taps get out of repair.
* * * *
Once it is firmly established after repeated
trials that to touch any given country means
sudden death to the toucher, war will begin
to become unpopular, and men and women
be at last convinced of its horror and folly.
They are not convinced yet, nor anything
like it. There were more men engaged in
war yesterday than were ever before
gathered for purposes of slaughter since the
world began to cool, and vast numbers of
them are setting about the job with mediaeval
ferocity. The result of it is certainly going
to be what the results of previous wars
have been —impoverishment, demoralisation,
misery and woe. But as physical pain is
something the individual memory quickly
discards, so it would seem that the agonies
of war soon vanish before a revival of the
impulses which lead to war. In times of
peace everyone is ready to agree that war
has always been a terrible curse; and when
it arrives they are mostly eager to lynch
. anyone who was responsible for not being
ready to engage in the curse. If any fore-
runner of peace seemed more reliable than
another it was this more or less com-
mon agreement among men that war is an
abomination; but all experience proves that
the influence of this feeling lasts only until
somebody thinks it a good time to fight.
The mere lifting of armies from the ground
to battle in the clouds can obviously do
no more to JLianish war than the existing
facilities for shifting armies about by sea.
The community with a punch is reasonably
secure from a covetous enemy so long as
the punch is heavier than the assailant cares
about taking. But if he has 50.000 aero-
planes and someone can throw 100.000
against him without creating complications
which will bring 500.000 winging through
the atmosphere, the plight of the first will
be no more secure than if he were depending
on cavalry and-infantry and submarines and
Dreadnoughts. Pending the installation of
the local hells before mentioned the only
guarantee any country has of life is to be
prepared to argue the point, and one of the
surest methods of being unready to do this
would be to start constructing huge numbers
ot implements for the purpose of achieving
universal peace, instead of all the instru-
ments that are necessary for hurting some-
one when there is no peace. To speak of
the aeroplane as a forerunner of eternal
friendliness looks like referring to night as
the forerunner of day. It is—but darkness
arrives again all the same.
A MYSTERY OF HOT-CROSS BUYS.
Nobody above the age of nine years ever really
desired to eat a hot-cross bun on Good Fri-
day, yet by custom and convention an enor-
mous number of people over a long period
of years have conspired to devour the diffi-
cult thing; and the recent refusal of Sydney
bakers to produce or evolve it was in certain
prints discussed with tragic intensity. Many
years ago. when it seemed a fair thing that
people should do a bit of extra penance before
the feast of Easter, it was the custom to
bake dull and sodden buns, freely sprinkled
with grit and ashes and marked with a cross,
through which the weary penitent was wont
to slowly chew his sorrowful way. Later,
with the growth of the community, the bak
ing of the buns was transferred from the
domestic hearth to the big tradesman’s oven,
from which the unpalatable brown things
were turned out in their tens of thousands.
But the public, while it didn’t mind on a
special occasion eating its own dust and cin-
ders. soon objected to paying good money for
such stuff, and a compromise was reached
whereby spices and other flavorings, which
looked sufficiently like dirt to please the
eye of Heaven, were substituted for the
coal-grit in the buns. A well-marked cross
remained to keep the penitential idea to the
fore; and the religious sense was satisfied.
From that time onward nobody except the
maker really knew what a hot-cross bun con-
tained. The spice largely disappeared ; the
heavy, stodgy nature of the cakelet was
changed ; currants dotted the dough as freely
as in other buns, and the mark of the cross
slowly faded until there was nothing left
but a suggestion of something which had
meant a lot to our devotional ancestors. The
hot-cross bun, of all its former attributes,
preserved only its mystery —a mystery as
deep and dark almost as that of the wildest
sausage stuffed at early dawn with the
mortal remains of many beasts. Yet in that
mystery were none of the old elements, for
it was simply a mystery of the bakehouse,
and those who held the key of it had neither
understanding of the meaning of a hot-cross
bun nor sympathy with th.e_ ancient spirit
that desired for its sins to chew dust and
ashes.
* ♦ * *
Modern life is very much a hot-cross bun.
In the process of evolution men agreed upon
certain measures of self-sacrifice for the
common good, and were willing to give up
a lot of natural rights as a sort of return for
the safety and protection that organised
society offered them. They were in a
measure willing to chew the dust and
ashes of self-abasement before kings
and leaders, on condition that those
kings and leaders kept the organisation going
which made the cow safe in the paddock and
insured the periodic filling of the meal-bin.
As time went on. however, the citizens of
most lands became fastidious about the cin-
ders in their social food, and asserted what
they called the rights of man. In the bun
of life certain more-or-less bitter spices were
substituted for the original grit that set the
teeth on edge; and as time went on these
gave place in turn to the currants of alleged
political freedom ; and even the mark of the
cross which stood for the penitential view
of the ordinary citizen's attitude to the State
began to disappear, until only by the closest
scrutiny could any sign of it be seen upon
the bun. But the mystery remained.
Nobody ever quite knew what the funda-
mental ingredients in that bun of Society
were, and in later years the manufacturers
of the article have more and more lost grip
of the essentials in its construction, until
people wonder whether the bun isn’t, after
all, a makeshift for a potato pie, and whether
a plain French roll mightn’t fill the bill
better than the thing as it now exists. The
mystery remains; and the bun continues to
be eaten, though with increasing misgiving.
* * * *
Knowledge is very much a hot-cross bun.
In the most ancient days a few magnificent
and holy people professed to hold the keys
of all that was to be known; and the great
bulk of the world’s rank and file were content
to sit at their feet and eat the grit and ashes
of self-contempt in the sodden dough of
ignorance. Then enlightenment came, with
much boasting, to scatter dirt-colored spice
in the bits of popular education that
authority allowed. And still the game went
on. until to-day authority is a mixed proposi-
tion, with no understanding of its original
place in the scheme, and knowledge is a
mystery with more mystery baked into it
to make it brown, and a few currants of
accuracy here and there in the mass. And
the same sort of analogy applied to a dozen
of the phases of existence and the watch-
words of progress will be found quite work-
able. Always there is the idea of peni-
tential dust and ashes in the early career of
the bun. always the intermediate spice that
looks like dust and ashes but tastes differ-
ently, and at last there are the sparse cur-
rants in- the white mass, from the brown-
baked top of which the mark of the original
cross of sacrifice is beginning to disappear
or has already vanished. But nothing among
all the ideas or forces to which the parable
of the bun can be applied is so close to the
original in the story of its evolution as the
thing we call patriotism. It began in self-
sacrificial dirt-eating, it passed through the
period of spice substitution, it developed on
into the stage of currants and the disappear-
ing cross : and with its final change it became
a thing of ever-deepening mystery that had
lost all touch with original motives and aims.
Of course, . unlike some of the other buns,
patriotism is in all stages of development at
one and the same time. There are good
patriots to-day gritting their teeth hard
on the sacrificial bun; there are middling
patriots eating the spiced thing that is still
obscurely linked with the original thought;
and there are flag-flapping zealots of much
noise eating the sort with the currants scat-
tered under a cross-less crust. And it all
goes by the name of patriotism, even as a
bun is always a bun, whether it contains
street-sweepings or ambrosia.
WOOL—THE HYPOCRITE!
‘Sea Island” takes a vegetable view:—
“ltiMU’s” yarn about cotton (B. 13/4/’l6) is an elu-
sive ana deceptive curse with which I am forced partly
>0 agree, though in the main I repudiate and-abhor it
Imported cotton may be the liar he says it is, and
the cotton-importing crowd may deserve all he
says about them ; but when it comes to a plain con-
troversy between Australian wool and Australian
cotton, all the decency, honor and gentlemanly con-
duct is on the side of the vegetable. What is im-
ported cotton, anyhow, but the lackey and parasite
of that smug, oily hypocrite, wool? It was wool
that grabbed the great bulk of Australian land and
scattered 10 miserable sheep over a landscape which
might have been a settler's homestead; it was wool
that stood always in the road of progressive reform,
and that, while it didn t use brown brother to elia«e
or barber its sheep, lifted a solid sneer against the
White Australia policy: it was wool that saw no
use in Federation or Defence, and that in its dull
merino fashion helped the advocates of congestion
and Freetrade and every other abomination
Cotton, on the other hand—the genuine Australian
cotton—as far as it has got, has been an industrv
of the small farmer, conducted by the farmer s own
family, tending to closer settlement of the countrv
and giving decent returns for the moderate labor of
white men. On the face of things, it is Australian
cotton that is the honest proposition, and Amo v
wool that is the smug, hypocritical liar ’ t a ian
* * * '
Take this question that “Rimu” raises ab
kid’s singlet and pants. The general idea that 1
is the proper wear next the skin has some nointl°°
its favor in the colder districts, though the ln
wearing Australian doesn’t seem to have degenem!',
to any extent through his habit of necle f
grandma’s advice on the subject; but even h
cotton has an advantage of its own. Flannel t
which “Rimo” despises, may attract pneumonia aln
may be dangerous in case of fire; but wool’’
perpetual danger because it can’t be thoroughly
cleaned. Germs of many diseases can only be "
moved from clothing by boiling, and cotton
regularly boiled ; but the fibre of wool is destroy rt
if in washing you raise it to a temperature a bov
that at which the giddy microbe thrives and multi
plies. In the case of the kid, the mother has
better chance of keeping him from the cold which
she can feel and the flame which she can see th
from the secret death hidden in the warm but
uncleansable wool. A silk industry for Australi
may be most desirable; linen, which involves
strenuous work on the part of the flax-growers and
is particularly exhausting to the soil on which the
fibre is raised, may get a footing some day in the
colder parts of the continent; but over a huge
portion of the Commonwealth cotton offers the
surest crop for the least outlay and labor. If on
merely sentimental grounds or from some anti-
quated notion that wool is, under all circumstances
a better wear we should turn down this source of
potential wealth, we deserve all that may be coming
to us.
* * * *
In Queensland, where Daniel Jones has been
fighting for support for the cotton industry, the
weather conditions which often make wheat-growing
a bitter failure exactly suit cotton. The rainfall
is sufficient for the wheat, but it comes at the
wrong time of year. With a dry winter and a wet
summer cotton makes marvellous growth. More
than this, while in America and elsewhere cotton
has to be planted annually, many of the best varieties
are perennial in Queensland, and give fine returns
of the highest-quality fibre after standing several
years with a minimum of cultivation. To the
cow-farmer who has broken his heart and enslaved
his children over Strawberry, cotton-growing would
be only- a pleasant pastime; and for the returned
soldier who wishes to go on the land it offers the
safest and easiest road. In the Northern Territory,
where the conditions are similar or superior to
those of Queensland, cotton is the direct track to
successful settlement. Cnee the politicians are
brought to book and compelled to keep their pledges
with regard to the direct North and South railway,
there is no reason why cotton of the finest staple
grown by white men on their own holdings should
not bulk as one of Australia's biggest industries.
* * * *
The real curse of cotton narrows itself down to the
curse of importing it. Cotton can be grown here
and manufactured here; and we have no sort of use
for the product of nigger lands or nigger looms.
In America the nigger is largely out of the business,
but all sorts of discolored persons are at it else-
where. Build up the tariff by all possible means,
keep Australia's jobs for Australia's people, but don't
heap curses on a harmless vegetable whose intentions
are of the best. Wool, on the other hand, has a
lot to answer for, since it is in the very nature of
the greasy stuff that it makes for land-monopoly and
every anti-Australian sentiment.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Passing Round the Hat for Billjim.
An appeal is made by the Commonwealth
and State Governments for subscriptions to
a fund “to assist necessitous soldiers to gain
a permanent livelihood after the war”; and
The Bulletin hopes that it will be liber-
ally responded to. This paper isn't going to
pretend a real liking for it. Taking up sub-
scriptions in aid of the distressed Pole and
Belgian may be all very well. Holding out
the hat for coin to discharge the public obli-
gations to Billjim is a different proposi-
tion. As it has apparently to be either this
or worse, the time seems ripe to prevent the
worse thing happening. But while the poli-
ticians are beating up money for the fund,
one more appeal may well be made to them
to consider what is happening to the cashbox
generally. All over Australia to-day there
is a carnival of waste in progress, ranging
from the good solid cash that is being
blewed on 14 Houses of Parliament and
seven Cocked Hats to the equally valuable
coin disappearing down the drains at the
Post Office and throughout the Defence
Department. Money taken directly from
the taxpayers’ pockets or acquired with a
p.n. is being squandered with blind disre-
gard of the past, present and future, lha
the country will be faced with heavy respon-
sibilities to soldiers long before the war is
over, and still heavier when it ends, is not,
apparently, given even passing consideration.
Money that should be saved is fntteie
away; credit that should be protected i
heavily discounted. It is not too much
say that in two years Australia will ha
literally wasted more cash than it will
for this repatriation fund. And it all com
out of the citizens’ purse. The job or P
viding for the soldiers is one which sbou
be discharged by Parliament at o°®j.
the taxpayer, and if the politicians did t
duty they would see that this was d ■
What is contemplated now is to shore U P>
the cost of the taxpayer, a demented sup
structure of extravagance and allow
voluntary principle to attend to the res
candle alight at both ends is being ,
to melt by sticking a match into the m

The Crime and the Punishment.
When Roman citizens, not content with
breaking the Carthaginian power, i - - ,
that Carthage should be destroyed rt j
branch, they were acting with a
more common sense than most <»
modern critics will give them credit •
Phoenician had a bad name all am
Mediterranean : and in the rise of •
the Old Word saw the unpleasantness « g
Phoenician crystalising into enl P ir V som e
stood for free institutions and ir
measure for fair play: C arthag - terS
plutocracy, for the rule of neb wasn ’t
over communities of slaves. . * viatic
gentle-handed; but Carthage was


in the matter of cruelty. Of course, the
destruction of the big African city arid the
complete breaking up of the race that held
it looks a pretty inhuman business; but it
might have been still more inhuman to leave
there the elements of fresh trouble and
"more widespread bloodshed in the years to
follow. The old story is worth looking into
in the light of the curious cry for gentle
treatment for the German at the close of the
war. For centuries Germany has been the
centre of the European war-plague. The
barbarian forces which destroyed the Roman
Empire —all, except one or two horrible
things which trekked over from Asia —were
made in Germany. For a thousand years,
wherever you found a bitter, savage mob
breaking a quiet and well-ordered country-
side, you could lay a king’s ransom to a
wheelbarrow that the mob was of Teutonic
origin. The outbreak of Wilhelm is merely
the old running amok of the Goth and Van-
dal, phis organisation; and the story of
Belgium and Servia is a repetition of things
which occurred in an earlier age, seen in the
limelight which the modern press supplies.
If Germany were to be judged on the part
which first and last the German race has
played in Europe, the verdict might quite
reasonably be national destruction; but such
a punishment would so shock and debase
those who took on the job of executioners
that it is not to be thought of. On the
other hand, anybody who talks about kind-
ness to Germany when the war stops is
simply preaching unkindness to Frenchmen
and Englishmen and Belgians. Given the
chance, the Teuton will come at it again;
and if England is spending £5,000,000 a day
merely to give him that chance, it is pretty
poor finance. Clearly the gentle people who
have made Belgium a suburb of Hell can’t
be trusted with firearms, or with the trade
that enables them to buy firearms, or with
the friendship that enables them to create a
trade to enable them to buy firearms. If
Europe decides that the German is for ever-
more to feed pigs and grow cabbages and
report at the nearest police station if he
wants to get a pass to see a picture-show,
Europe will be only taking a wise precaution
for her own good. The war is not won yet;
but when it is won the arrest of the criminal
nation of homicides who made it will have
cost such a price that penal servitude for
the term of its natural life will not be too
heavy a price for that nation to pay. What
recruit other than a lunatic would offer his
services if it were understood that the end
was to be a gentle request to the German not
to do it again?
The N.S.W. Public Service.
Tiie N. S. Wales Public Service has been
given another Superannuation Fund; and,
as usual, the fattest get the most fat. In
a matter of this kind the men who are
approaching the retiring age are always the
snag in the river. To provide himself with
an annuity that would be worth having, the
public servant of 55 who is doomed to the
block at 60 would need to pay a premium
that is beyond the power of the ordinary
man. The new scheme makes a compro-
mise : it is not compulsory for public ser-
vants who are over 30, but every servant who
is over 30 may contribute to a pension of
£lO4 a year as though he were only of that
beautiful age. So to obtain a pension of
£lO4 a year a man of 55 will pay in alto-
gether £77, whereas the public servant who
actually is 30 will pay in £465 —assuming
that in both cases 60 is the retiring age.
The servant of advanced years, and thus
usually of advanced salary, will in this way
benefit very greatly at the expense of the
younger man of more moderate means. The
younger man certainly gets some advantage :
if he dies before he is 60 his widow is to
draw half the amount of the pension he has
been contributing for, with £l3 a year for each
of his children under 16. A start had to be
made, however, and whenever it was made
the ; old men would present the same diffi-
culty. The one crumb of consolation is
that as the veterans drop out the thing will
right itself. No doubt it would be possible
to devise a scheme that would not do an
injustice to either the younger public ser-
vants or the taxpayers, by whjch the older
servants could get their pension without
drawing something to which they were
not entitled. Three-fourths of the ser-
vants who retire at 60 are, taking
one consideration with another, as valu-
able as they were at 55. Nominally,
then, the officer might retire on his pension
at 60, but, while drawing only his pension
pay, might be required to put in six or 12
or 24 months’ service, as the case might be,
in order to thus make good his contributions.
The Government in that event would pay
into the fund the difference between the
man’s salary and the pension. The majority
of officers would be just as happily em-
ployed in their old posts for a few extra
months as in wandering about looking for
somebody to help them kill time. But,
naturally, the men of advanced years are
the men who mostly guide Ministers; and
if they can get a pension without paying for
it, they are not likely to insist upon pay-
ment. Apart from this feature of the
scheme there isn’t much wrong wi.h it.
And when it is all boiled down there isn’t
much of any rbmarkable value in it, either.
It is the proper thing, no doubt, to require
the public service to make provision for its
old age; but to a great extent that had
been already done through the regulation
which requires all officers on joining the ser-
vice to take out an insurance policy, increas-
ing it as their pay rises. These policies
may now be either continued or dis-
continued. If an officer is wise and
wants to continue his policy, though he
feels he cannot keep up the payment of
premiums, he may hand the policy over
to the Board of the Superannuation Fund,
which will pay the premiums, and, when the
policy matures, will collect the insurance
and hand it over less the amount of the
premiums paid by the Board, plus Si per
cent, compound interest. As it is a rotten
life office which doesn’t earn for its policy-
holders a good deal more than per cent.,
the public servant will presumably rush this
offer. It means, in effect, that the good old
State is going to borrow money at anything
up to 5 per cent, to lend to him at per
cent. But this paper doesn’t expect to see
any kick coming from the taxpayers on that
score. They are too used to it: they stand
up to be milked as placidly as any Straw-
berry in the country. The few who might
be disposed to throw out a leg, chew, instead,
the usual old cud : the bulk of the new pro-
visions will only come in after the war.
And obviously what is only going to happen
to-morrow doesn’t matter.

Fish Oh!
When the N. S. Wales Government decided
that it wouldn’t be honest to its party or its
principles it resolved that it would engage
in industries which the daily papers might
call “Socialistic,” and which, with that label,
might convince the rank and file of the
Laborites that Holman and Co. were still
the -despised and rejected of Fat, and as
such to be supported at election time. The
confidence trick practised on the public was
pretty transparent, but the results of the
various enterprises are in the nature of
calamity. As these things were the Gov-
ernment’s big bluff, it might reasonably have
been expected that a great deal of effort
would have been expended to keep them up
to the mark and force them to show the
administrative ability of those in charge.
But apparently the Government didn’t care
a hang for anything except the advertise-
ment to be gained by starting them. Some
have been successful; more have been
failures; and the net result is much “lost
money.” Black, arriving late in the Minis-
try, had a promising scheme. He would
catch fish and sell it in Sydney. Every-
body wanted fish; and to keep the scaly
things well in the public eye would count a
lot of votes at election time. Besides, who
would think of the forgotten non-borrowing
plank or the failure to abolish the Legisla-
tive Council while he was actually eating
cheap Government fish? So the trawlers
went out and the shops were opened and
Black made many fishy speeches in a solemn
tone. Now the result begins to be known.
Up to date, according to the Auditor-General,
there has been a loss of fSOOO. Certainly, as
“lost money” goes, £BOOO isn’t much. Another
Government might quite easily have lost
£50,000 in the same way; but the Auditor-
General knocks out every excuse before it is
uttered. The accounts in connection with
the business were muddled in a fashion that
called for special and particular notice.
The position is not a cheerful one. The
Holman Government was placed in office to
carry out certain reforms. It side-stepped
the things it was appointed to do and elected
to sell fish instead. It has sold fish so badly
that it is doubtful whether even Wade and
Fitzpatrick combined could have done it
worse.
+.
Besides inferentially offering an insult to
Australian working men, by suggesting that
they in particular escaped service under the
present system, Navy Minister Jensen was
guilty of what, in the nation’s crisis, is an
unpatriotic and degrading appeal to narrow
class prejudices. His suggestion, made to
an Eight Hour audience, was that Conscrip-
tion compels only the poor to shed their
blood, while the rich are not compelled to
shed even their money. Stand the grotesque
statement on its head, and you get some-
thing near the truth. The physically fit
capitalist may have to give his money and
his life. The working man employed indis-
pensably in any war industry gives neither
life nor money. He is the conscript who
stays at home and draws good wages. To-
day in conscripted Germany, Austria and
France, hundreds of thousands of “poor”
men, who have had military training, are
employed in heavy work on the home rail-
ways, or in cannon foundries, etc., far from
the roar of battle, whilst all the wealthy
men of military age and fitness are on mili-
tary service at the Front, not being exempted
by any other qualification of usefulness.
Among the dead or maimed conscripts of
Germany are thousands of spectacled “pro-
fessional” men, whilst armies of lusty
workers are engaged in munition-making.
The able-bodied expert toiler at Krupp’s is
never asked to give his life’s blood. But
the dandy son of the millionaire merchant
or banker is the first to be required to go
and fight. Jensen’s “argument” is not an
argument at all. It is just an audacious
mis-statement offered in excuse for a vote-
bunting Government that is under the thumb
of a small section whose ingrained class
prejudices keep them unteachable.
♦
General Monro’s despatch on the evacua-
tion of Gallipoli is an exceptional document,
embracing considerations usually not taken
into account in such reports. According to
a summary : —•
He found the troops suffering through various
causes from strain and continuous exposure, to shell-
fire, from which it was impossible to withdraw them,
because every corner of the peninsula was exposed to
fire. They were also very much enervated by
endemic summer diseases, and were gravely under-
officered owing to losses in earlier battles. The make-
shift mingling of foot and mounted troops necessary
for holding the front did not tend to efficiency.
In short, the position had every imaginable
defect. It was too narrow and too shallow ;
consequently it was impossible to keep the
reserves and supports out of range of the
enemy’s artillery, which meant that they
could never get proper rest. What that
means to men engaged in the prolonged
defence of an entrenched position only those
who have experienced it can fully under-
stand. The range and rapidity of fire of
artillery have never been greater than in
this war, and not only the range and rapidity
of fire, but also the effectiveness of the
ammunition. An added risk is read in
the serious charge of General Monro against
those who were concerned in the organisa-
tion of the force sent to the Peninsula, and
it no doubt applies as much to the British
war chiefs as to the Australasian, because
all of them depended on the unreliable
voluntary system for supplying leaders.
What sort of officers can a nation expect
when in ordinary circumstances they never
have to handle more than a hundred or two
men, and are then suddenly called upon to
dispose of tens of thousands under the most
difficult conditions?

The new Liberal Government in Tasmania
got a smack in the eye to start with. Premier
Lee achieved the support of Whitsitt, the
Independent Dictator, by a promise that
neither Mulcaiiy nor Payne, both ex-Min-
isters, should have a place in his Ministry ;
and with Whitsitt he would have 16 mem-
bers, facing 14 on the Labor side. But now
W. H. Burgess, ex-Treasurer and Liberal
member for Denison, has cut loose from the
Liberal Caucus. He intends to sit on the
cross-benches and has told Lee that he does
not recognise him as leader, will attend
no more meetings of the Liberal party under
present conditions, and will refuse to be
bound by the decision of the majority of the
party. This, all things considered, is good
news. When Burgess, and Whitsitt, and
Mulcaiiy, and Payne, and a dozen others
form themselves into parties of one, there
will be a fair chance of an Elective Ministry,
with an honest expression of opinion in the
House for the first time. At present the
majority doesn’t rule. Under the party sys-
tem the majority of a majority does the
ruling. In Tasmania this means that nine
men, forming a majority of the Liberal
crowd, pass laws to which the other 21 may
be violently opposed. The party system,
reinforced by the Caucus, has not a thing in
common with democracy.
The Commonwealth has decided, at last, to
stop the wastage of Australian shipping in-
duced by the tremendous prices that people
all over the world are prepared to pay for
anything that can float. It let the Peregrine,
which had been sold by the Australian
Steamship Co., clear for Shanghai; then it
woke up, and, after the Adelaide S. S. Co.
had agreed to sell the Innamineka to North-
ern buyers, refused the vessel’s clearance.
Both parties—buyers and sellers —made a
song about the fact that the held-up vessel
was going to Hongkong, a British port,
whereas the Peregrine was allowed to clear
for a mere treaty port. But that is not the
point, of course. Australia has her own
shipping requirements to consider; and a
stop to the export of water-vehicles must be
made somewhere. That an entire stranger
has stolen Brown’s doormat while he was
asleep is no reason why Brown, having
wakened, should allow a relative to get away
with the morning milk.

A Victorian bank, which had successfully
experimented with women in other branches
of the service, has lately appointed its first
ledgerkeeper, vice a man enlisted. Before
men began to come back from the
Front in numbers, it was in many
business houses found advisable to
replace the absent fighting-men with
young women. Presumably the Vic-
torian bank is now employing female labor
because, incredible as it may seem, the
supply of returned soldier isn’t equal to
the employers’ demands. Still, it would be
interesting to learn for certain if the right
kind of inquiry had been made in the right
place, a bank billet being so peculiarly suit-
able for the returned soldier with the right
qualifications and the right kind of disable-
ment.

“The Old Soldier” :
There seems to be a good deal of agitation in cer-
tain quarters about the way to get a commission in
the Commonwealth army, and, to allay it, Head-
quarters has issued instructions consistent with
commonsense but for one thing:—
Persons who have never held a commission must
enlist in the ordinary way in the A.I.F. for general
service. On arrival at Liverpool or any other dep6t
an application can be made at once for admission
to an N.C.O. school. Candidates must have a
knowledge of platoon drill.
Quite right. But why the school? A recruit would
be of no use as an N.C.O. till he had been dismissed
recruit’s drill, and he would learn his duty a great
deal better while doing it in the ranks. If he was
a really clever youngster he would soon find his level
as an N.C.0., and would learn more in a week doing
duty than in six months in a theoretical school.
The school, it is alleged, is a necessity on account
of the changes in drill. Nonsense! The British
army changes its drill about every six months, and
the regimental officer has to accustom himself with
very little help to the new book when it comes
out. Anyone who remembers the old light-infantry
drill of anytime back to 50 years ago, with its
words of command, could handle a company or a
battalion to-day, after studying the book for an
evening. Always a great many things have been
practised that have never found their way into the
drill-books. For instance, the captain of a leading
company, on coming to thick timber, would put
himself at the head of his men, and say: “Follow
me,” and they would follow him in single-file, if
they could do no better. Yet where is that laid
down? Where is the regulation for the old-
fashioned escort for guns—three files in extended
order to each gun, and the rest of the company
extended on the flanks, not looking to their own
officers for orders, but conforming to the movements
of the guns ? Such things are not to be found in
any drill-book, yet they have been practised by
British infantry and artillery for IQjQ years and
more.

The Sunday-school superintendent might
take Whitsitt as the text of an
address on the. importance of little things.
Whitsitt is the man who has been making a
see-saw of Tasmanian politics for two or
three years past. And it’s all because a
couple of Liberal Ministers forgot their
manners. At the time Whitsitt was a good
Liberal; and (this is the story as lately told
by the Argus) : —•
An inspector discovered moths in Mr. Whitsitt’s
potatoes and prohibited their export. Mr. Whitsitt
appealed to the Minister for Agriculture—a Liberal
—to have the embargo removed; but the Minister
declined to distinguish between Liberal and Labor
potatoes. This testing of Mr. Whitsitt’s Liberalism
was too much for him, and from getting moths in his
potatoes he got moths in his politics.
But it wasn’t so much what Liberalism didn’t
do as the rude way in which it didn’t do it.
Whitsitt, on behalf of the farmers of his
electorate, including himself, wired the Pre-
mier asking a question about a certain sec-
tion of the Potato Diseases Act (1909), and
the Premier didn’t reply. Then Whitsitt
wired the Minister for Agriculture another
question concerning the Act, and requested a
Yes or a No. He got what he considered an
evasive Yes-No. Then, when neither Premier
nor Minister would give him the information
he and his fellow spud-cockies wanted,
Whitsitt bumped them with a far from
evasive bump and listened to their expiring
squeals with calm approval. His subsequent
offences against good politics don’t alter the
fact that Liberalism received a bitter lesson
in manners as a result of its characteristic
attempt to sidestep an issue.

When golden Niagaras of the Empire’s cash
are being poured out every day, it is inter-
esting to read a disclosure made by General
O’Moore Creagh, late Commander-in-Chief
in India, while explaining the uses and func-
tions of a general staff^
It has always seemed to me that there should be a
commercial intelligence branch in the Ministry of War
which would advise the spending departments of the
General Staff as to the best and most suitable market
to make purchases in, and also as to the reliability of
contractors. If this were done much greater economy
would be ensured in military expenditure. Some six
years ago, when organising a General Staff for the
army in India, I tried to establish within the fold
of the Ministry of War a commercial intelligence
branch, but I could not get the necessary funds,
although a very trifling initial expenditure for the
purpose would have secured an enormous future
saving of Government money.
What is the matter? Is it because the whole
surroundings of the British military machine
are so corrupt that suggestions for prevent-
ing waste and doing the vast business of the
army in a business manner are always turned
down? It is difficult to suggest any other
reason that will hold water —except plain
idiocy.

“Watcher” from the Front Door: —
The Commonwealth’s demonstration farms and 1
sheep stations in the Northern Territory make the
practical man feel ill. The only thing they demon-
strate is how much money they can spend without
getting any return for it. For that sort of thing
they could earn Y.C.’s all through the year. If
the Government would give a bonus for experts’
scalps there might be some hope for the Territory.
What between incompetent officials and experts who
know nothing of the conditions of the country they
are sent at large expense to report on and run, the
poor old Territory has not much chance of coming
out on the credit side in any of her experiments.
We are told we can grow anything in the agricul-
tural line; but then the question arises what to do
with the stuff when it is grown. The only thing
that has a chance here at present is stock-raising,
and that is pretty well a dead letter, as the best of
the country is held by foreign capital. We are
told that Vestey Bros., the big people who have
been given facilities in return for putting up freezing
works, etc., are all-British. But are they? I see
questions are being asked in the House of Commons
as to why this all-British company is removing its
headquarters to the United States. It is said by
some that the idea is to avoid war taxes —which
would be a patriotic way of helping good old
Britain, eh? Anyway, it is a well-known fact that
all through Queensland and the Tableland the
American octopus is reaching out in all directions
to secure a monopoly of the meat trade; and there
are cattle stations on the Tableland whose output
of fats for the next four years has been secured by
this benevolent Trust. This country ought to be a
reservoir from which the South could draw the meat
that makes Anzacs, and draw it at a reasonable
price. But, instead, it has no rail connection, and
is used merely as the playground of “experts” and a
doubtful monopolist or two.

“Ex South African,” apropos of the
Empire’s obligation to uphold the White-
Australia policy in return for Australia's
help in the war: —
During the last two years I have been instru-
mental in getting a number of people from South
Africa to take up orchard land in Tasmania. Their
one common reason for making the change has been
that they wanted to get away from the neighborhood
of the blacks. Throughout South Africa the kaffir
is displacing the white man in all branches of
manual work, and in Capetown, Durban, Pretoria
and elsewhere the Indian is gradually ousting the
white trader. It is only a matter of a little time
when S’Africa will be another India—a country
populated by colored people ruled by a compara-
tively few white administrators and wealthy men
whose descendants will, in the end, meet the fate
of the other white men. Professor Broom, the South
African scientist, in a lecture in Johannesburg,
expressed the opinion that before another hundred
years all the whites would have retreated to Aus-
tralia. All these S’African immigrants who have come
to Tasmania send the same message to Australians:
Hold fast to your resolve to reserve the island con-
tinent for the white man.
Not Yet.
[FOR THE BULLETIN]
We still have not all the troops in Western. France
which should be there, though our strategy has im-
proved since the General Staff regained its function
in October. Our output of guns and munitions is
still below that of France and Germany.
— Col. Repington, in London Times.
We were not ready many months ago
When the storm broke,
And over ruined lands the headlong foe
Rushed, stroke on stroke,
Trampling our honor in the bloody dust —-
The honor in which nations set their trust.
Earth may forget;
But while the chorus of loud death is pealing
The haggard ghosts with fear about us
stealing
Ask are we ready yet!
A great offensive some day may be ours
When, loud and strong,
The awful concert of our gathered powers
Breaks into song ;
When, with tremendous might on land and
sea,
We teach stern lessons to the kings-to-be ;
And rolling down,
A wild, red harvest of destruction reaping.
With blow on blow across new fields is
sweeping
The Teuton to his tomb.
But now days grow to weeks and weeks to
years,
Trust faints and dies.
The skeletons of nations cold with fears
In horror rise.
They point to us, to ours, to them who led,
To the long front of battle grim and red.
Can these forget?
They lift once more their trembling hands
appealing;
And Famine, in the tracks of misery reeling,
Pleads “Are you ready yet?"
N.S.W.
D.M.W.

Billjim on His Travels.
Woodrow Wilson seems to have got
writer’s cramp, and unless there have to be
more Notes explaining the last Note it looks
as if Brother Jonathan will at last knock
the Hun off his list of acquaintances and
refuse to take delivery of any more corres-
pondence. What this would lead to can he
left to the future and the submarine to
decide; but it seems about time that the
Yank at least stopped having official rela-
tions with a murderer whose hands arc red
with the blood of American citizens and
who refuses to stop hurrying more of them
into Kingdom Come if be gets a chance.
This all-in torpedo policy, pursued by the
German, outrages every principle of war for
which the United States stands, and there
is nothing for the President to gain except
contempt by resisting it with mere ink. To
Australia the exchange of documents
between Berlin and Washington is of less
concern just at the moment than the address
of Billjim, who mostly seems to have been
swallowed up by an animal called Official
Precaution. Lately some of our lads were
reported to have had a little desert fighting
away to the north-east of Suez, and there is
a story about General Birdwood telling
some of the wounded Anzacs who are attend-
ing frequent divine service with A. Fisher
in London to get well and hurry up to
France. This suggests that Bill and Jim
may be in the thick of things again directly:
and as the tilings which happen in the region
of Gaul are the ones which really matter in
this disturbance, William and James arc
likely to have various fresh experiences to
write home about. Meanwhile the gory
struggle around Verdun goes on, there is
apparently a big scrap pending in Flanders,
on the Russian Front both sides are crouch-
ing ready to spring, and the end is still a
long way round the corner. The other day
the landscape in Mesopotamia carried a
brighter appearance: but since then the
clouds have gathered again and the only
thing that can be definitely made out through
the murk is more muddle. Even the unintel-
ligent Censorship can’t quite hide that. In
the midst of it all John Bull is holding a
secret sitting of Parliament to decide whether
he is going to put every man under arms:
and there are 500 reasons why he should
hurry up and do what he should have done
long ago. The risk of a stalemate is very
considerable and is not going 1o be avoided
by hanging back in the traces because the
collar pinches.
Melbourne Herald lias come to the con-
clusion that the Jews are “a far-seeing
people.” They have to be. Otherwise they
would always be bumping themselves.
Billy Kuhnel —everybody called him
Billy—died very suddenly in Adelaide last
week. After playing a game of billiards at
the Stock Exchange Gluh he walked into
the street, and heart-trouble carried him off
within a few minutes, lie w T as head of a
big piano-business, and one of S.A.’s many
third-generation good citizens of the German
stock of 70 years ago. Neither looking, nor
speaking, nor feeling like anything but a
good Australian, he could - afford to joke
about his descent. “Oh, I’m dead,” he told
a pressman last year, cheerfully. “Didn’t
you bear? I was sending guns disguised as
pianos, and, when a ease got burst open on
the railway platform and gave the plot
away, I committed suicide! Ani/body will
tell you that.” Kuhnel was a great racing-
cyclist in the high-machine days 30 years
ago, one of the first men in South Airs. to
steer a motor-car. and an enthusiastic owner
of racehorses. He was only 53 when the
end came. The old racing against Busst
and Davis and the other champions had told
at last.
From chairman .T. Reid, of “Pineapple”
Huttons, Ltd.: —
“0.K.” (B. 20/4/’l6) is neither quite fair nor
quite correct when he states that the late D. Mit-
chell was responsible for the greatly-improverl quality
and mildness of hams and bacon generally in recent
years. The fact to be remembered in comparing 28
years ago with the present is that refrigeration was
not in many cases applied to bacon factories in the
former period, and the work had to be done in
cellars, the temperature of which could not be con-
trolled ; lienee heavily salted and unsuitable bacon.
These last 15 years have seen refrigeration used in
almost every modern curing house, with the result
that bacon and hams are being treated in a manner
quite impossible prior to the introduction of freezers.
So far as my co. is concerned, we were one of the
first to make use of refrigeration, and 25 years ago
we were turning out mildly-cured bacon and hams
both at Melbourne and Brisbane, to be as much
appreciated in Australia as in the markets of the so-
called “East.” Australia has not, in my opinion,
sufficiently honored the men from whose brains sprang
the modern freezing machine, without which the vast
business in perishable products could not be con-
ducted.
