A youth in the agonies of calf-love is the
cause of “The Boomerang” at Melbourne
Royal. The poor lad. Budd Woodbridge,
has conceived a deathless passion for a dam-
sel named Grace, and growing limp and
loony in consequence is thrust by his mamma
into the inexpert hands of Dr. Sumner for
inspection and diagnosis. The medico,
readily identifying Budd’s complaint, pre-
scribes a course of treatment combining hypo-
dermic injections with physical exercise, and
including the attentions of a nice amateur
nurse. Budd patiently submits to the per-
secution, and even allows Sumner to directly
“O N TRIAL ,” AT SYDNEY CRITERION.
Frank Elliott addresses the Court.
imperil his attachment by freely editing his
correspondence to his donah and substituting
stealthy insults for the feverish imagery of
love. After a month of this treatment the
boy gives such a marvellous imitation of a
cooling regard that the doctor considers it
safe to throw' Grace in his way. just to
let her see how thoroughly he has been cured
of his fancy. But the doc. reckons without the
lady. Her guile is superior to his science.
Result is that at an evening party she con-
fronts Budd with the last throb of her
breaking heart, and he is soon bestowing
copious endearments on her powdered neck.
Apparently the only point in the comedy of
the doctor’s failure is that his sententious
scoffs at Budd’s emotion become verbal
boomerangs later on, when he suddenly sur-
renders to the aforesaid nurse’s glad eyes
and finishes up the show as badly rattled as
his patient.
“The Boomerang” is stronger in its by-
play than in its argument or dialogue, and
its humor on the whole is conventional and
artless. The scenery, however, is likeable
in its handsome simplicity, and the acting,
if not remarkable, is almost sufficient unto
the characters and the frivolity thereof.
Hale Hamilton, perhaps, leaves the greatest
sense of injury by filling Dr. Sumner with
his own loud, combative good nature. Hale
is rarely soft or soothing. In moments call-
ing for subtle intrigue an obtruded chin and
a roar are the better part of his diplomacy.
Miss Tannehill as Nurse Virginia Xelva is
relentless in her unruffled repose. She never
spares the audience a jot of the serene pro-
fessional air necessary for the part. In the
role of Budd Woodbridge Donald Bowles
practically reduces his actor’s art to an exhi-
bition of well-creased clothes and melancholy
profiles. Nevertheless he is as quaint as
maybe, his broken-hearted voice being a
delicious idiocy. Miss Beatrice Nichols as
Budd’s tart is a meagre excuse for the young
man’s foolishness. Honeyed innocence is
not quite her forte. Miss Ghiloni as Budd’s
fond mamma also seems a bit short of the
requisite gentleness. In fact, her nearest
approach to that agreeable quality is a sort
of playful pugnacity. Nancye Stewart,
Mattee Brown, Leslie Victor; Edwin Lester,
Charles Wheeler and John Ardizoni figure
inconspicuously in minor parts, the Italian
gentleman making a better job of a French
servant than he did of the demented Dago
in “Twin Beds.”
At Sydney Adelphi they don’t forget that
there is a laugh in Shakspeare. And why
should they, especially when “Twelfth
Night” is on? Why restrict the play to the
severe boundaries of the literary text, wffien
if the yarn the story hinges on has anv
meaning it is that Malvolio was an ass and
a wowser whom the author wanted to hold
up to ridicule? Malvolio is a pompous, self-
righteous sort of Bumble, whose type sur-
vives the passage of the centuries and the
scorn of satirists. His hide is as tough as
an alligator’s, his vanity of that order which
almost reaches the sublime, his condescend-
ing bumptiousness exquisite in its offensive-
ness. When the vixenish Maria hooked him,
she used the methods her sex has employed
on such fish ever since the world began.
Allan Wilkie plays the part with intelli-
gence and skill ; and if at times the tiling-
looks like comic-opera, wffiat is there to
grumble at when Malvolio is being so effec-
tively stripped of all his humbug and ex-
hibited as a thoroughbred donkey? Wilkie
sees to it that the laugh is on Malvolio all
the time, and right through his work is en-
titled to commendation. Australia has seen
many Malvolios, but few r have been better
than this. As for the rest of the cast, there
are the usual bright and the usual dismal
spots. Miss Hunter-Watts gets through as
Viola very well indeed, and the pining
melancholy of a robust Duke of Illyria re-
ceives adequate elocutionary treatment from
Walter Hunt. John Cosgrove found the
garb of Toby much more comfortable than
the royal robes of Denmark, and as Sir Jack
Belch held up that end of the performance
with a firm hand and an emphatic hie. He
had need to, for Andrew Aguecheek sparkled
with extreme difficulty and the Jester had a
hard job. Maria was a cat. Miss Elwyn
Harvey made the lady one. Hers was a
really excellent performance.
On Saturday Allan Wilkie Shyloek will
lose his ducats and his daughter and his
pound of flesh. It w T as hard luck for the
Jew ? ; and as all the favorites have been
going down at Rand wick, and the price of
meat is a serious business in Sydney just
now, George Marlow is preparing for a
frantic rush to the only place where the
Chosen can be seen getting it in the neck.
Arriving with the reputation of a so long
run in Sydney it says much for “So Long.
Letty,” that the show came almost up to
expectation in Melbourne, at Her Majesty’s.
The entertainment seems heartless and soul-
less, like many other things made in
America, and the music has only a rag-time
swing, but the effects have a certain smart-
ness of their own. The touch of novelty is
there. The two scenes and the many dresses
are gladdening to the eye, and numerous
ridiculous incidents are absurdly beautiful.

of girl pours into Tommy’s trellis garden.
Anything happens in “So Long, Letty,” and
it mostly happens for the very best.
The two married couples who change

Ten years ago “The Squaw Man” boomed
in Melbourne at Her Majesty’s, nor is there
any reason why it shouldn’t now renew its
profitable youth at the King’s, except that
British military officahs. who wore scarlet
jackets and fussed about their precious Reei
mental Fund in 1906, are mostly clothed in
khaki or fine-linen bandages to-day Jfili
tary times have changed. The first’ act u
“The Squaw Man” is a very ’Murkan con
ception of an English ’lgh Society gathering
at the Earl of Kerhill's country house or
rather, in the garden thereof—where the
brothah officahs of the noble Earl present him
with a loving cup in recognition of his s. M-
vices as trustee of the Regimental Fund
But the noble Earl has embezzled the fund
and the chief concern of himself and his
noble mother is to save the Family Title
from disgrace. So mother persuades her
heroic nephew Jim to pretend to he the
embezzler by bolting to Utah (U.SA i
under shadow of suspicion, Jim being "the
limit in self-sacrificial quixoticism as well
as the soul of honor. He humps the burden
of his noble cousin’s shame for the sake of
that high-horn blighter’s pretty wife Diana
who is dead struck on him. ' There’s no
aristocratic dissimulation about Diana.
When alone with heroic James in the gar-
den she tells him that no matter what ill-
fortune his Family I’ride may lead him into
he will always have the love of w T omen—-
which is what vulgar, people would call giv-
ing him the straight griffin. Nevertheless
Jim goes away without kissing Diana, and
the love of woman breaks out in a new place.
When he takes to cow-punching in ’Murka
(cows being meek quadrupeds that rarely
punch back i a little Indian girl gets so
struck on Jim that his chivalrous nature
can’t help itself. The red-skin lady acquires
the habit of saving his life. Incidentally,
she kills Cash Hawkins, a lurid had man who
is about to put a bullet iiito Jim’s soul of
honor. He marries the persistent, unspeak-
able little woman, and their half-breed
papoose is a few years old wffien the Family
solicitor and Diana come from England to
tell him that the blighter Earl is dead and
himself is the lawful successor to the title,
the estates and Diana, if he wants ’em. This
is where the faithful little squaw becomes a
pathetic inconvenience. Her decease is
essential to the happiness of Jim, Diana and
the Family Name; so she shoots herself.
Her father, the boss rooster of the Ute tribe,
produces her corpse as evidence of good
faith.

[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Sing bev for a wife who loves to gad,
Though not at heart a sinner,
Who makes her doting husband sad
By quite forgetting dinner!
Sing hey for a wife who feeds the brute—
She’s a matron stout and dowdy—-
Whilst he flies round in a smart sac suit
And sighs for raptures rowdy.
Sing hey for two contrasted hubs,
Ore fond of home, one fond of clubs,
Who give or take domestic snubs
And suffer sorrows petty ;
And here’s to the girls,
The stylish chorus girls,
The girls in “So Long, Letty.”
Sing hey for a maid that’s said to be
A-drowning in the ocean.
Her rescue from a mystic sea
Is quite a Yankee notion.
Sing hey for an Aunt and a slim young Phil,
Who cut less ice in acting
Than the forty girls, not on the bill,
Whose charms are so distracting.
God bless the whirlwind dancing pair,
The nimble man and the lady fair !
And bless those legs beyond compare,
So gracefully coquettey,
Of the eight nice girls,
The merry, merry girls—
The girls in “So Long, Letty.”

;he Family Honor. The scenes of cow-
punching and bar-room life are convincing
enough. Cash Hawkins, played by Geo.
Bryant, the stage manager of the show, is
about the baddest rustler that ever fingered
a gun with malice aforethought. His career
is a crowded half-hour of evil intentions, and
his sudden death is a note of tragic realism.
Also Miss Gleeson (the squaw) and her
feathered parent are strangely human items.
The Bailey-Grant firm runs this thickly-
populated drama at the King’s, and if it runs
up to its old reputation “The Squaw Man”
should be a winner.
The upside-down melodrama, “On Trial,”
at Sydney Criterion, provides an exciting
evening for amateur detectives. These, on
thinking the play over, discover some
difficulties in the plot that are not men-
tioned in the evidence. For instance, how
did the murderer manage to get into, the
millionaire’s house? The burglar wouldn’t
be likely to let another man in, would he?
After listening to an artist and a woman
arguing about the splendor of “Mother
Goose” and last year’s outbreak at Her
Majesty’s (Sydney) present scribe is con-
tent to leave it to the swarming Easter
crowds.
“Peg o’ My Heart,” with Sara Allgood in
the lead, is broguing prosperously at Sydney
Palace. Always in the holiday season this
compact little theatre is liable to feel like
an Oriental lady who tiglit-laces shame-
lessly ; and with so good a show as “Peg o’
My Heart” it is, this Easter, feeling that
way more than usual.
* * *
When the elephants tiptoe silently amidst
the palatial appointments at Wirths’ Sydney
hippodrome, and after standing on their
hindlegs, dancing a tango and otherwise
behaving after the manner of all well-con-
ducted monsters of the forest, slink out
again one by one, they have to be revived
with smelling-salts and peanuts, the atmo-
sphere of grandeur about the place having a
serious effect upon their nerves. But it is
quite different with the seals. Considerable
trouble has been experienced in restraining
these cultivated cousins of the mermaid
from taking front seats in the dress-circle
and applauding the other performers. By a
compromise George Wirtli has provided the
hairy musicians and tightrope walkers with
a private box, and from this they give three
cheers when May Wirth somersaults and
the Japs, leap from nowhere with unerring-
precision to the tops of their heads and back
again.
Like the contemplative adjutant bird,
Sydney Tivoli seems to be very satisfied with
its long bill. The managerial advt. repeats
one name four times and five others twice.
The artists who are only mentioned once
have probably kicked up a row about it
before this. Marie King Scott is the artist
of whom the house is proudest; but there
would be trouble if some rival drove up with
a closed furniture van and tried to abduct
Menetti and Sidelli, Cardo and Noll, the
Three Ivundles and Jack Bornhold.
Scouts of winter are warning the open-air
shows to stand from under. The Dandies
are preparing to vacate their entrenchment
at Branscombe’s Garden Theatre (Sydney),
and a couple of weeks will see the close of
the season. Meanwhile special old-favorite
programmes are maintaining the cheerfulness
of the show, and friends of the co. are
invited to nominate any item that is linked
with fond memories of a visit to the garden.
* * * *
King Carnival still jingles his bells at
the White City, and the young Gumleaves
from the bush have been having the time of
their lives with him. Beauty competitions
are the rage at the big playground just now.
and after Rider Haggard has seen what
Sydney can produce in this department he’ll
have no difficulty in inducing British
Tommies to come to Australia after the war,
land or no land.
The Ideals of 1916, at Melbourne Audi-
torium, continue to suggest starlit summer
skies. Also an odor of brine from the sea-
side. They are a fairly versatile company,
with the exception of the gentle pianist
whose name is Fred. Fred is much too
lovely to be versatile, but his glance across
the keyboard is absolutely killing.
❖ * *
Twelve farewells of the Follies are being
crowded into this week at the Melbourne
Tivoli. A protracted season will come to a
well-earned conclusion on Saturday, by
which time the names of everybody required
to fill the gap will be duly announced to a
listening world. Meanwhile all things at the
Tiv. are as they were in last Bulletin.
Fred Villiers, who has been talking war
at Melbourne Athenaeum Hall since Satur-
day last, was advertised to say no more
about it after to-night (Thursday). This
arrangement, however, may not receive the
sanction of F.Y.’s dear old friend, General
Desire. If the General orders him to stand
and deliver another lecture, Villiers will
have to obey. Protests from Carlyle Smythe
will be useless.

fines him sd. for slander, with costs. Paris
Green, w T ho is in the monologue business,
is an equally satisfactory entertainer. He
has a heap of bright new T stories, and one
at least loses nothing in piquancy for being

The panto, at Melbourne Princess’s is
taking full advantage of the Easter season,
when autumnal-tinted frivolity takes a final
flutter and panto, is in the fashion of the
times. Dan Thomas is still the livest wire
of “Whittington,” and the Ballet of Bare
Legs continues to emphasise the fact that
beauty unadorned is oft admired the most.

Adelaide shows have now’ got into their
usual winter clothes. The Williamsonian
pictures have vanished from the Royal. They
were a fine set, introducing the brilliant
Theda Bara, and showing that an old friend,
Nance O’Neil, is still on deck. The trouble
was they began with their best thing, “Car-
men,” instead of working up to it. For
Easter “The Dancing Mistress” has come
in. At the Tivoli last year’s Con-
way and Fitzherbert, have had a successful
week as Raffles and Sherlock Holmes in an
American play that brings those two heroes
together. The King’s has the juggler Testro,
who should be a star if he only had some
new feats; also Coleman whistles, and
Cumby and Brown are conventional coons.
At the Queen’s, the deft little Napoleonic
conjurer Malini is giving a one-man show
—an unusual idea these times.
While fond Australian mothers have been
sending their youngsters to England so that
they mightn’t have to mix with the unedu-
cated, unmannerly Jimbills they’d be sure
to find in the Australian army, Dr. Brissen-
den, one of the most learned, bookish law-
yers in Australia, and a man with a very big
practice, has dropped into the line in Sydney
as a private, and is well content to leave in
a few weeks as such.
Scores of civil servants in Tasmania
have been making it understood that they
burn to fight, but that a hard-hearted
Government declines to give them leave.
Well, according to a soul-less official return,
only 19 out of the men eligible have
been refused leave, and the names
of a few of those who have been
pleading the alleged barrier cannot be
discovered in the document. Out of
1041 officers eligible for service only
224 have enlisted. This is fairly disreput-
able seeing that the Government has made
definite promise that those who go shall
get their old jobs when they return, or some-
thing equivalent.
AT POVERTY POINT.
“H. 0.”: Why “So Long, Letty”? Not so
long as broad.
“Norbell” : I went to the panto. “Mother
Goose” here (Brisbane) the other night, and
sat with all the other baldheaded, happy
children in the stalls. During the show a
burly person in some sort of make-believe
uniform squeezed along the row of _ bony
knees and fatted calves and thrust a picture
postcard into the hands of every stallite.
Presently he squeezed back again and sug-
gested a “silver coin” from each individual.
Thinking it was a patriotic collection, as
usual, everybody sparred up, and when the
lights flared we read, “Three Dutchmen on
their journey round the World. Started
22nd March, 1914, from Brussels (Bel-
gium) without money. They may not beg
and must earn their living by the sale of
postcards. Please buy one and pay what
you like.” The same old fake! But what
a cast-iron nerve these three “Dutchmen”
must have to try it on at a time like this !
Also, who let ’em do it at the panto.? Fur-
ther, why don’t the police swap some hard
and useful work for the swindle?
“Screen” : Jane Shore, on the movies, has
been sending a fiost of people to their history
books. As the fourth Edward’s left-handed
wife she exercised all her power for good-
just like Lola Montez with the dotard King
of Bavaria till the Jesuits snuffed her out,
and sent her acting “Lola Montez in
Bavaria” in Australia. Although historians
conspire to smother Jane Shore, she is the
most famous woman in English history next
to the queens. There is Rowe’s old tragedy
of Jane Shore. Also one by Wills, in which
Miss Heath, Wilson Barrett’s wife, acted
with him. The last-mentioned play was
produced in Melbourne by Mrs. G. B. W.
Lewis.
“Vocalist”: What “O K.” says about
Theophilus Roberts (B. 13/4/T6), the Mel-
bourne voice-restorer, applies equally to
Roland Foster in Sydney. In my own case,
after being debarred from singing by nose
and throat troubles for nearly eight years,
my voice has developed so greatly that I
am now at work professionally with success,
and the throat specialist I was under is
surprised at the difference in the voice. Like
Roberts, Foster believes that, generally
speaking, the vocal organs are as normal in
construction as our hands, and that any intel-
ligent person with an ear for music can
acquire the power to sing without causing
the neighbors to send for the police.
“Private Boxer”: Only last year both
lan Maclaren and Allan Wilkie were appear-
ing at Sydney Criterion in the J. C. William-
son version of a United States drammer
called “Inside the Lines.” The work of
both mummers was voted commonplace in
the factory-made piece. This year one is
starring in Shakspeare and making a suc-
cess of it, and the other is compelling admi-
ration by his high-class work in modern
comedy. All of which seems to prove that
United States drammer is deadly, and that,
given the good stuff, there are plenty of
brainy actors available to play it.
Irene Browne recently skipped into
musical comedy in London. She was filling
a minor part in “Who is He?” at the Hay-
market, when Robert Courtneidge coaxed
her away for the lead in “My Lady Frayle,”
for which Arthur Wimperis and Max Pem-
berton constructed lyrics. They called it
“Vivien” for the trial provincial tour.
“Kaniva”: A man’s fame dies with him.
Jim Bell, who had been on the Rickards
circuit for nearly 30 years, and for
the last 10 had been one of its stage
managers, attracted an audience of two to
his funeral the other day. J. R. Greville,
the comedian who amused countless thou-
sands, was followed on his farewell by his
old partners Harwood, Stewart and Hen-
nings. Not one “pro.” followed Johnny
Cowan, the old-time minstrel, although he
had appeared at benefits for his fellow-man
times without number. John Bryan, another
well-known theatrical, who was in the first
cast of the “Silver King” in Melbourne, also
raised but a couple of followers.
From London : “The Tiger’s Cub,” at the
Garrick, is about the rowdiest and roaringest
mining play on record —a sort of Bret Harte
nightmare. Madge Titheradge is the dog-
whipped heroine of this extraordinary piece,
and whatever else she may think of it she
will agree that it is the most strenuous
knockabout part she has ever played.
She does it in a fine, full-blooded, melo-
dramatic style which has captured all the
critics.
Minnie Tittell Brune was in the cast, of
“The Love Thief.” a Canadian melodrama
which moved the Maple Leaf soldiers in
London to derision and metaphoric cabbage.
What she said is not recorded, but she must
have said something. It was an ungovern-
able habit of hers to say something between
her pantings.
“U.S.”: Stevenson’s novels were for a long
time regarded as impossible for the stage.
Then Mansfield astonished the Yanks with
“Jekyl and Hyde.” Now “Treasure Island”
has been produced as a play at the Punch and
Judy, in New York. Great success. Four
acts and nine scenes, adapted by J. E. Good-
man. The piece was first tried on the dog at;
Albany, and failed, but manager Hopkins
insisted on giving it a run on the Great White
Way. Edward Emery is fine as John Silver.
There is only one female and no love interest.
This the public finds mighty refreshing. Ed-
ward Emery is brother of Winifred, who
visited Australia with her father, Sam
Emery. Sam’s father, John, was a celebrated
comedian, and John’s father, too, was a
theatrical. The thespian history of the family
is unbroken since 1712. It is remarkable
that two other eurrently-successful New York
plays have no love interest —“Young
America” and an adaptation of Hauptmann’s
“Weavers.”
“Stargazer” gets an extra turn : —•
I experienced an unusual diversion at a motion-
picture show in St. Kilda (Vic.) the other evening.
An ordinary, machine-made drama from one of those
American factories where they turn them out, true
to scale, at the rate of a million a week, was in
progress, when the audience was thrilled by an omin-
ous growling like that of a famished Zoo tiger
attacked by a rabbit. The growling gave place to
a deep-throated barking, and a huge dog—of the
mastiff sect, I am informed —bounded up the aisle,
roaring with emotion, ' and went straight for the
party on the film. The screen was solid, and the
dog shut up into a lump where he hit, and recoiled
in a ball, amazed and somewhat confused, but wholly
unconvinced. He tried again with every demonstra-
tion of canine emotion, and once more he o’erleapt
himself. He tried again, and failed again, while
the audience shrieked in chorus, with squeals that
were half rapture and half holy terror. Then the
small, barbered French poodle in the picture that was
the real occasion of the mastiff’s demonstration
trotted off the film. The big tyke went after him,
and ran all in a heap off the platform. He picked
himself up, now thoroughly roused, and went tearing
round and round the picture screen, barking,, yelping
and sniffing, seeking the mysterious poodle, and
promising him a merciless death should they meet.
The dog’s owner and a large attendant captured
Crusoe after a long chase and threw him out; but
for half an hour the exasperated animal could be
heard, coursing round the building, still in quest
of that contemptible but bewild'eringly elusive
French poodle.
“Harrison O.”: After having endured a
series of blatant Yankee plays performed
by mostly indifferent mimes, I learn, on the
authority of the J. C. Williamson press
agent, that, when here, Yankee performers
are at pains to “broaden” their style, so as
to appeal to the crude perceptions of Aus-
tralian theatregoers. A paragraph circu-
lated in Melbourne quotes the dictum of a
member of the noisy “Twin Beds” show
that “a remark which in America would
evoke roars of laughter if merely uttered in
a quiet way, without throwing it at the
audience, would in Australia be passed over,
so here we color it up a bit.” It is perhaps
natural thus to make a virtue of incapacity,
but to attempt to excuse crudity on the
ground that artistry would not be recog-
nised is merely rubbing the salt of insult
into injuries already done to the artistic
conscience of the Australian playgoing
public.
(Continued on page 26.)
Chief Justice Murray, of South Aus.,
passed on an interesting decision the other
day. The Federal Probate Act provides that
duty shall be levied and paid upon the value
of estates of persons dying after the com-
mencement of the Act. Frederick Flavel
died at 7.45 on the morning of December 21,
1914, and the Bill did not receive the
Governor-General’s assent, and thus become
law, until after 10 a.m. on that day. It
was consequently argued that Flavel was not
a person dying after the commencement of
the Act, and that his estate was not liable
for duty. To the layman the point seems
good, but the Commissioner for Taxes
decided against it. The executors ' then
appealed to the Supreme Court; but Justice
Murray, following the authority of English
cases, held that it was a recognised and well-
known rule that the Law took no notice of
fractions of days, and that an Act which
came into operation on a given day became
law as soon as the day commenced. So the
executors passed sadly out of court to meet
that hungry animal, Costs, licking its lips on
the doorstep.
A WASTED PERFORMANCE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
From the recruiting depot strode
Jules Greasepaint; on his chest
His chin was sunk, and on his brow
Sat gloom. I greeted him: “How now?
And did you pass the test?”
He nodded. As his hand I grasped
He showed no sign of joy,
But merely frowned and heaved a sigh,
Then murmured, sorrow in his eye:
“A wretched house, my boy l”

SOCIETY
PENFOLD'S
WINES
Australia has a Labor Government and
an Arbitration law, and a unionist in Syd-
ney the other day told how the two between
them were helping the nation through its
greatest crisis. He looked for an engineer-
ing job at Garden Island, but as there
was a strike on there was nothing doing
for him. So he went over to Melbourne
and secured an engagement, but before he
could take his coat off there was a sudden
crash of tools being downed. Hearing a
rumor that work was going on at the Clyde
shops (Sydney), he rushed back to find
another strike commencing there. Then,
with the aid of a black tracker and a
bloodhound he ran down a billet at Eveleigh,
but the day he reported the same old curse
settled on that place. Whereupon in bit-
terness of soul and the fear of lifelong idle-
ness, he wrote to the newspapers to say in
effect (1) that he was a good unionist; (2)
that his object in becoming a unionist was
to improve his prospects; (3) that his pros-
pects were on offer to the first comer at
seven for 6d.; (4) that his luck was so
rotten bad that he believed if he died
the grave-sinkers’ union would go on strike
and his body would have to be hung over the
limb of a tree.
WOMEN ARE SO SELFISH.
“You said that, when I married you, you would gratify my every wish.”
“Well, what are you wishing for notv?”
“ I’m wishing that I’d never seen you."
At Sydney Quarter Sessions Judge
Scholes has been earning a mixed reputa-
tion. On the average, his Honor has shown
more leniency than this Court has known
for an age, and in the cases of young offen-
ders sentences have been suspended in a
record number of cases. Yet occasionally
the learned Judge imposes quite unexpected
sentences, married offenders being singularly
unfortunate. His Honor’s idea was ex-
plained in a recent case, in which it was
mentioned on behalf of a defendant, charged
with stealing furniture from a firm which
employed him at £2 10s. per week, that he
was married. On this the Judge gave the
opinion that the man with a wife should
be more severely punished than a single
man, because he knew in committing an
offence that he would bring trouble upon
others. Solomon believes, in short, that if
a man is driven to crime by the pressure of
necessity, such as might easily fall upon a
married man with £2 10s. a week, he is a
worse criminal, than the man who, having
only himself to think of, adds to his punting
money (say) by felony. This paper is no
advocate of thieving; still it hopes that the
good-hearted Scholes may live long enough
to find out that many a man with a hungry
wife and children has stolen because the
best thing in his whole composition told him
to do it.

Major Billjim found it waiting for him on
the mess-table at Tel-el-Kebir the other
morning : —•
His Excellency The Major,
I am called Abdou Mohammed who was a helping-
man for the Australian man who is called Shaly the
cooker of your Excellency.
I tell your Excellency that Shaly the same brought
me to help him in the cook-room, and promised me
that he gives me daily six P.T. (piastres) to consider
of mj’ work with him ; for that, I worked with him a
month only without I did not take from him money;
and when 1 said to him that he gives me my charge
for a month he refused that.
Now I presented this letter to your Excellency after
my begs; and thanks to order him to give me my
wage; because your Excellency the biggest director
of him ; and I have my old father, mother, and my
family; and all these I supply them with food to
eat.
God bless your Excellency.
Your Servant,
Abdou Mohammed.
The Major paid.
Justice Rooth, one of Westralia’s im-
ported wigs, added at the last Criminal
sittings to his already remarkable record.
A man of 25 years committed a loathsome
offence upon the infant daughter of the
family he was living with. The monster,
or lunatic, communicated a venereal disease
to the five-year-old child, and pleaded in
defence that his passions had mastered him.
Rooth, J., made some noises and handed
out a sentence of 18 months. On the
following day an aborigine who —according
to the charge —attempted to rape a girl of
16 years was given two years’ hard. But petty
pilferers and a horse-stealer received sen-
tences of from three to five years. Horrible
offences against children are shockingly com-
mon in Perth, and a little more of Booth's
style of dealing with them will lead the
married citizen to take a gun into his own
hands.

THE LESSER OF TWO EVILS.
“I suppose you have a lot of trouble keeping your wife dressed in the height of fashion?”
“Yes; but it’s nothing to the trouble I’d have if I didn’t."
That a doctor’s prescription written in
a dog version of a dead language may be
mis-read with deadly effect was shown at
Melbourne the other day. A Ivew medico
wrote on a prescription: “One tablespoonful
to be taken hora somni ,” which is the un-
English way they have in the Doctors’
Union of writing “bed-time.” The chemist
who made up the mixture —a sleeping
draught—read the words “hora omni”
(every hour), and wrote the label for the
bottle accordingly. When the patient had
been given three doses of the draught, which
contained morphia, the family did not like
the look of him, and he died soon after the
arrival of the hastily-summoned doctor. As
the patient had been in a very low state
and w r as about due to leave the world, and as
several members of the Doctors’ Union gave
evidence that the morphia was not enough
to kill a man, the Coroner merely remarked
that the chemist had been careless, and
returned a verdict of misadventure. He
might have made a few observations anent
the absurdity of writing medical directions
in a dead language, but he did not —perhaps
because he is a member of the Doctors’
Union himself.
* * * *
“Whizzer”: Melbourne is at present
harassed by great clouds of children who
rush hither and thither on what are locally
known as “scooters” —things like a capital
L on wheels. The scootist places one foot
on the horizontal part of the L, and, grasp-
ing the top of the perpendicular section,
propels himself into the distant and oft-times
sensational future by a succession of back-
kicks with his free foot. The noise made
by a battalion of these engines resembles
that of curtain-fire at Verdun. They are
in use by boys and girls from 7 a.m. to 10
p.m., and collisions with citizens have been
so numerous that ratepayers are protesting
in a bruised voice to the local councils. The
police view is that the kiddies are too young
to be prosecuted for driving vehicles on the
footpath, so the law merely warns them to
go elsewhere. They promptly do so at 12
miles an hour, with results similar to those
set forth in Neil Lyons’s story of the little
girl and the Belgian officer. What with
motors, scooters, electric cars and uproar-
ious nippers in box-trucks on every down
grade, the Melbourne citizen nowadays is as
full of dints as last year’s jam-tin.
The tale of a Melbourne tragedy of last
week had an unusual sort of postscript. The
man was Gerard Fondabury, a railway em-
ployee of 50; the woman, his wife, was 40;
the serpent was a sailor-man. The last-
mentioned refusing to be choked off, and the
wife being unduly fond of him, the harassed
husband shot her and then himself. As the
bodies were on the way to the morgue a
letter arrived from the cause of the trouble
asking the woman to meet him that night
and make arrangements for eloping. Police
search of the house, a small dwelling in
West Melbourne, gave some queer results.
Fondabury was really Van der Berg, of
Dutch birth, naturalised in T 2, though in
the naturhlisation papers he called himself
German ; and amongst his papers was found
a plan of trenches, roads and gullies between
Ilerbertsholie and Bitappa, at Rabaul, the
how and why of its existence being a mystery
that the Defence authorities ought to know
something about, but probably never will.
* * * *
An Adelaide man is getting an expensive
lesson in the law of divorce. He said things
about his wife. She denied them, and said
the same about him, besides accusing him of
cruelty. This brought on the scene two
other women whom she had named, and who
insisted on coming in to defend their reputa-
tion. There was a leisurely trial, 29 days
of it, and then the jury returned a bunch
of negatives —nothing wrong with the hus-
band ; nothing wrong with the “interven-
ing” ladies; no cruelty; and about the
wife’s conduct they simply couldn’t agree.
All this was last year; and now the winter
campaign is opening again. The husband
naturally wants a fresh trial on the one
point that was not settled. She that
the whole case must be re-tried, if any of it
is ; and the Court agrees with her. By way
of clearing off some of the magnificent accu-
mulation of Costs, it has ordered that she
shall pay those of the other ladies whom she
dragged in. But, as for her own, “the rule
is that a wife is entitled to be provided by
her husband with the means of defending
herself in a matrimonial suit, and her soli-
citor cannot be deprived of the right to
receive his Costs at the husband’s expense,
even though the defence should he unsuccess
ful.” A lawyer with lady clients seems o
be on a good wicket.
UNINFRINGED NEUTRALITY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A mighty Zeppelin soaring high,
Immersed in the celestial sky,
Exploded bombs—some six or seven—
And played the very deuce in Heaven.
The murderous Huns’ destructive fire
Killed more than half the heavenly choir,
Destroyed the golden streets, and tore
Serene archangels by the score.
The news clutched Wilson by the throat.
He armed. But Kaiser William wrote,
And soothed him with this sweet advice:
“There are no Yanks in Paradise!”
VICTORIA.

Musty.
“Longy” : Owing to the excessive remune-
ration attached to the job of cleaning country
schools in N. S. Wales, prospective cleaners
generally take fright when the subject is
mentioned, and the work falls to the
teachers, who toil as a rule for less pay than
a piek-and-shovel man. With a view, there-
fore, of having the work done on sound
principles the Education Dept, has issued
instructions on the subject of how to do it.
Here they are: (1) All floors must be swept
daily with sawdust dampened in disinfect-
ant; (2) all walls and ceilings must be
cleaned once a quarter; (3) all desks, seats,
tables, etc., should be dusted every morning
with a moist cloth; (4) all rooms to be
scrubbed out once a quarter; (5) all presses
and cupboards to be cleaned out periodically
and washed with soap and soda; (6) floors
of all w.c.’s to be scrubbed with soap and
water once a week. The pay for this is
9s. 3d. a month, payable half-yearly all at
once!

“B.B.” : They run in, all shapes. It was
Ali Hussein, of the Indian Army Transport
in Mesopotamia, who reported himself with
a bullet in his shoulder. Said the doctor:
“When did you get this?” “In the battle,
sahib,” answered our mule-walloping
brother. “But that was three days ago.
Why didn’t you report yourself before?”
“Sahib, I had no time. The wounded were
too many.”

“How old do you think I am?"
“Not nearly as old as you look.”
"Well, how old do you think I look?''
“Not nearly as old as you are.”
“Musketoon” : W. A. Murray, a constable
at Forbes (N.S.W.), with 17 years’ service
behind him, decided to change uniforms last
week. To get from blue to khaki he tells
me he had to resign from the force, and so
his long service goes for nothing. At least
he’s assured that it will go for nothing. But
I guess the Department will change its mind
if the right sort of row is made. No doubt
it is most important that the country should
be policed; but if George Black can’t get
the job done temporarily by men who are
unfit for war service, then he isn’t nearly as
ingenious as he used to be when he patched
my Bulletin paragraphs 25 years ago.
* * * *
Probably the oldest specimen of human
handiwork extant in Australasia has just
been unearthed in the tin-bearing gravel near
Gladstone (Tas.). It is a beautifully chipped
little cutting or scraping tool liin. long and
the same in breadth, and it was found at a
depth of 30 feet from the surface and well
below the marine deposits which cover this
part of the country. The material is a
semi-transparent chalcedony—not the chert
generally used by the aborigines for their
tools. Black Brother of Tasmania must
have been a very unprogressive
individual, for this ancient tool,
which probably dates back many
hundreds of thousands of years,
shows better work than the stone
implements used by the aborigines
within historic times, though they
are all of the same general type.
The theory is that some black-
fellow dropped his pocket-knife in
the gravel away back before Bass
Straits began, that later this part
of the country sank under water,
that a thick deposit of marine
sediment, in places over 100 feet
thick, was laid down, and that
finally the marine deposits were heaved
out of the water again. And now
at last a miner has stumbled on the old-
time implement when he was trying to sluice
out alluvial tin. The tool is of exactly the
same type as the palaeolithic flints from
very old deposits in Spain and elsewhere.
Probably there weren’t any patents that long
B.C.
* * *
The latest egg-laying competition in Mel-
bourne has resulted in the breaking of three
world’s records. This has inspired a patriot
to hang on the fence at Burnley a notice:
“Our Hens Have Done Their Duty. Do
Yours.”
"Do not kiss on the lips,” is portion of
what Melbourne Herald describes as the
“platonic prophylaxis” laid down by Dr.
B. J. Bull, University bacteriologist, in his
Victorian anti-meningitis campaign. Very
good. But when a Hawthorn engineer came
home on April 7, after having omitted to
kiss his wife at his departure, he found her
dying, with a rifle beside her and a bullet
wound in her breast. Both she and her
husband were members of rifle clubs, and
the method she adopted was to lie down on
the bed and press the trigger with a poker.
At the inquest the Coroner, having been
informed that before she died the woman
had sobbed “He did not kiss me or say
good-bye,” took the view that she had mag-
nified a domestic quarrel. But he gave no
guidance to husbands in the matter of choos-
ing between tragedies and meningitis.
THE WINTER GIRL.
She shivers in furs by day, yet a smile will
warm her at night.
In the art of sitting down on a thing and
. . ... . , TT , , ,
not moving till it is dead, Hobart hasn’t
much to learn. Back m 1912 an inter-State
Conference was held to make uniform regu-
lations under the Foods and Drugs Acts, and
amongst other things agreed on were cere
tain provisions for improving butchers’
shops. In Tas. the turning on of the new
machinery was left to the local bodies, and
about the end of 1914 Hobart City Council
had got to the stage of giving the butchers
(two of whom happeu to be aldermen)
notice to bar out the too-frcqueut fly and
keep their shops more or less clean. On
August 12, 1915, 45 butchers were sum-
moned for breaches of the regulations, but
they proved that the regulations had not
been tabled in Parliament within the proper
time and legally didn’t exist. So the Gov-
ernment re-gazetted them on September 14,
but made them purport to come into force
on January 13, 1915, which rendered them
retrospective and void. The Government
then hatched another effort on December 28,
but mislaid it until January 25, 1.916, when
the regulations were properly gazetted. Two
months later Hobart City Council solemnly
decided to give the butchers six months'
more notice that the regulations were coming
into force. By this time the Chief Health
Officer was getting a little weary of the
farce, so he stepped in and told the Council
that he would take over the administration
of the thing, and that if the butchers didn’t
comply with the regulations right away
there would be something doing. The Coun-
cil replied that he was welcome to the job, as
the speed required to keep up with it was
an undue interference with the liberty of the
subject, or something like that.

The other day the cable reported that
upward of 1,000,000 Armenians had been
massacred by the Turks. “The English,”
the message concluded, “are endeavoring to
arouse popular feeling in Armenia against
the Turks.” There’s the Bulldog Breed for
you—ready to tackle anything, even the
task of making you unfriendly towards the
man who has been in the habit of dropping
in before breakfast and massacring a few of
your family.

The reflections of a Beast:—
There’s a lot of human nature among the suffra-
gettes. Mrs. Pankhurst, who is still the leader of
the married and elderly parties, became a whole-
hearted anti-German as soon as the true inwardness
of the war was made clear. They exhort mankind to
keep on fighting until German militarism is knocked
out and Freedom is herself again. The awful
destruction of husbands and marriageable men doesn’t
turn them from the main principle of suffragettism,
which is directly opposed to German man-made des-
potism. But the spinster suffragettes! The strong-
minded girls who have not tasted matrimony and
are mostly old enough to be afraid of dying in igno
ranee of its flavor! They don’t want any further
destruction of possible husbands in any Cause what-
soever. Here we have little Adela Pankhurst (who is
still quite huggable) and the Virgin Vida, Saint
Cecilia Johns and others braving the suspicion of pro-
Germanism rather than desist from calling on the
war to stop. They complain of nothing save the
slaughter of men —the men most of them affected to
despise as long as they remained in the flesh, but
whom they are so screamingly sorry to lose. Miss
Sylvia Pankhurst, in England, appears to be doing
her little bit to stop the war by championing the
cause of any strikers who block the way to success.
She has formed a sort of Labor Unrest Federation,
which would have held a meeting in Trafalgar
Square the other day if a party of stand-no-nonsense
Anzacs hadn’t intervened. Still that will only stop
Sylvia for a week or two. Why doesn’t some brave
Anzac marry her and keep her quiet for good?
One of the results of the war is the filling
up of the Australian mercantile marine with
foreign sailors. The Australian service is
a paradise to the overseas sailor, and he is
a constant new arrival. Long Bay Gaol
always has a batch of
deep - sea teailormen
“doing a month” as a
penalty for ship deser-
tion, their object
being, mainly, to get
aboard Australian
vessels. Since the
Kaiser went mad
quite a number of
Australian seamen
have taken a shore job
in khaki, and foreign
A.B.’s have climbed
into their bunks. Now
the Federated Sea-
men’s Union of Maori-
land is demanding a
promise that returned
sailor-soldiers will be
given preference when they get their oil-
skins out again, and the Australian Union
will probably fall into line.

A soldier-man in an Adelaide police court
was charged with flourishing a revolver and
making blood-curdling threats to his wife.
He expressed a wish to get away into camp
as soon as possible; on which a dull Court
told him to go to war, but solemnly bound
him over to keep the peace for 12 months.
. “The Tenth”: Opposite the Continental
in Cairo is a huge statue of Sulieman Pasha
or some other Gippy on horseback. One
of our coves thought the nag wanted a feed,
so overnight he climbed up and put a nose-
bag on. How he got there beats me; but
Jimbill will go to a bit of trouble for the
sake of a ’orse he’s taken a fancy to.
MINERVA.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Chill Age with furtive talons claws
My blood, though fain would I be merry;
Would I were young to-day, because,
Minerva’s with me on the terry.
That is the name I’ve given her
For reference, since I do not know her;
Her own, maybe, I’d much prefer;
And I've a heart I’d love to show her.
Minerva’s slender and most sweet—
V mouth provocative but shy;
A something variable and fleet
Just scintillating in her eye.
But should I speak to her she’d scorn
With tilted nose my simple word,
And make me wish myself unborn.
These stale conventions are absurd.
Her ankles slim ! Her dimpled neck !....
A grim regret is o’er me stealing
That I must hold myself in check
And coolly glance at her unfeeling.
I’d like to catch her by the chin
And kiss away her mouth’s rich sorrow ;
But that would be a grit .oils sin—
And I’ll be forty-eight to-morrow!
So there she sits, and here sit I,
And never shall we kiss and cuddle.
Though Beauty holds her sovereignty,
This life’s a curious sort of muddle.
But still Minerva, sitting there
Demurely, glorifies the ferry.
And life is sweet, and maids are fair....
Thank God ! say I, and let's be merry!
N.S.W.
JOSEPHUS Q. HARRIS.

PERSONAL
ITEMS
Took the last trench at Dunedin (M.L.)
the other day, Lieut. 11. It. Martineau,
Y.C. He got his bit of bronze at Game
Tree during the Boer AA 7 ar, and was
wounded three times and lost one arm while
earning it. Prior to that he had been
through the Matabele campaign and the
Natal rebellion. When the present scrap
started he took his one arm into it as
transport officer of the Otago Infantry Bat-
talion. Recently he was invalided from
Egypt, and was 42 when the end came.
AVinthrop Duckett, of the AA’est Aus-
tralian, remembered some of the old em-
ployees in his will, but not to the extent that
was generally expected. The young wife —
she is still in the 20’s—gets about £3OOO a
year. Her five children, four girls
and a boy, are bequeathed £lO,OOO each.
The Perth University gets £40.000 (when
the sum to be invested is swollen to that
amount) to build a AVinthrop Hall ; and
among many others the Anglican Church
and the Zoo also benefit. But it is not yet
certain whether all the legatees will be able
to get as much as Haekett wanted them to.
A late recruit is Desmond Duffy, son of
Justice Frank Gavan Duffy, of the High
Court and grandson of Charles Gavan
Duffy, now dead.
Two sons of ex-Senator Arthur Rae
(who is one of the Senate candidates with
the Labor endorsement, and therefore may
be Senator again) are off to the Front. Ma
State’s selected Labor bunch are doing well;
another of them, Peter Bowling, has sent
off almost a regiment.
Joseph Parr, who heard the Last Post in
Wileannia (N.S.W.) Hospital at the age
of 90, was a sergeant-major in the llth
Hussars and a survivor of the Charge of
the Light Brigade at Balaclava. This dash
was looked upon as the last word in terrible
valor, but that was in the days before the
war-devils stoked-up in earnest. His old regi-
ment fought for the Turks against the Rus-
sians, which was a howling pity. If it had
been the other way about those graves of
our beloved dead would not be in Gallipoli.
Eric Unwin, who can wear a Military
Cross nowadays, is a Sydney boy, born in
the Ma capital in 1894. His father, now
deceased, was a one-time secretary to Gover-
nor Loftus in Sydney, and was afterwards
iu the State military forces. Young Unwin,
who went to England with his mother some
years ago, graduated from the Military
College at Sandhurst just before Bill broke
loose, and was part of the first British expe-
ditionary force sent to France, going as a
second lieutenant in the Army Service Corps.
He has now climbed to a captaincy, and
figured in despatches not long ago.
Governor Barron, after serving a year
over the normal period, will leave Westralia
iu a few months’ time. There will be no
moaning at the bar when Sir Harry and his
dame put out to sea. His Ex. ra,n up against
public opinion two or three years ago by
breaking away from the precedent which
allowed the S.P.C.A. the use of the vice-regal
ballroom for its annual subscription dance.
Later on he made himself ridiculous by but-
ting in as a war prophet. After the Marne
affair he could not open a kindergarten or a
bottle without assuring his hearers, “as an
old soldier,” that the war would be over
before Christmas. Punctured as a
prophet, Barron finally came out as an advo-
cate of olive culture —not of the branch, but
of the berry.
J. AY. AA r ood, just dead at Brighton (Eng.),
was a follower of “Elijah” Dowie in Colling-
wood, Melbourne, and cheekily pretended to
the same gifts of healing. In the English
seaside resort he found many adherents, par-
ticularly a rich old maid, who built him a
gorgeous edifice in Edward-street, the so-
called “Temple of Jehovah.” AA 7 ood pro-
claimed himself a re-incarnation of Solomon
—a more difficult character to sustain than
Elijah—but his sayings went down amongst
the unwise. The “Army of the Lord” was
formed and Solomon, in robes of purple and
gold, would sit on a throne, with men,
women and children dancing like Dervishes
before him in “The Golden Circle.” Poor
Sol. and his religious ballet burst up long
ago and the Salvation Army got the Temple.

Mostly the artist has been pictured as a
man with long hair and no muscle to speak
of, a high strung person who loathed dirt
and smoke and untidy ways of dying. Yet
no little group of men in Australia has
yielded so large a number of recruits. Of
The Bulletin's black-and-white contribu-
tors Colics and Sommers left in the first
days of the war. Booth and Hartt followed,
then Townshend ; next Mattinson went into
camp in the West, now Hugh McCrae
has enlisted in Victoria, and Fred Brown
goes in this week with the idea of giving
the Huns some new ideas about Topliet. The
veteran “Hop,” by the way, is understood
to be the last private left from the American
Civil War. The few other survivors are all

The Drury statue of C. C. Kingston has
been in Adelaide some time, but the erec-
tion of it has been delayed by a squabble
over the pedestal and steps. These have
now been fixed, and the statue is to be
erected and unveiled about the first week in
May. The site is at one side of Victoria
Square, looking on to West Adelaide, King-
ston’s old and faithful district for the S.
Aus. Assembly.
All the good ’uns aren’t in khaki, though,
like those who are, this one doesn’t think
lie’s doing anything out of the ordinary.
He is Mervin Birkensleigh, of Bairnsdale
(Vic.). Dad sailed for Egypt lately, and
while he was in camp the youngster ploughed
a paddock and planted peas, etc. Every
morning he goes round his rabbit-
traps, collects the bunnies for market,
and then attends school. He is fully 11.
and just a typical Australian kid. And
still they wonder how it is Billjim is full of
initiative and resource.
That Marquis of Clanricarde who died in
London the other day had one great distinc-
tion: he was the best-hated Irishman of his
time. Everybody loathed him, and no one
more cordially than A. J. Balfour. The
second son of an Earl who was British Am-
bassador at St. Petersburg and Lord Privy
Seal, and of a mother whose brother was the
first Earl Canning, Hubert George de Burgh,
then 42 years old, fluked the title through
the death of his elder brother, a distinguished
Indian soldier. That was in 1574. He
went over to Ireland to attend the old man’s
funeral, stopped the work of rebuilding
Portumna Castle, and only once set foot on
the old sod again—when he appeared as de-
fendant in a suit for damages brought in the
Four Courts at Dublin by his agent, Joyce,
the successor of Blake, who had been shot
dead at Loughrea. Something like war
was for a long time waged on the Clanri-
carde estates in Galway. The county was
deluged with police and military; every
homestead was levelled to the ground. The
poor wretches who had wrested a meagre
living from the soil —they were descend-
ants of the retainers of a long line of feudal
landlords —drifted away into space and the
pool-house. After one lot of’ evictions a
few were accommodated with shelter in the
chapel yard at Cloncoe, near Woodford.
Clanricarde brought some old charter into
action, secured an injunction against Father
Costelloe, the parish priest, and they were
hunted further on. The estate became a
profitless waste, but the Marquis was a
man of great wealth and could afford to
enjoy his frightfulness. In the end the
Crown gave him a quarter of a million for
the place and told him to go> to Hell. He,
however, delayed until he was 84.

Swiss Hermann Ritz.
lecturer on languages
at the Tasmanian
University, and of late
interpreter to the
Censors department
could pick up a lan-
guage in the casual
way ordinary folk read
a novel. At the time
at his death last month
he wielded 15 different
languages with ease,
and was adding Mal-
tese to the collection.
In his lighter moments
he toyed with Chinese
and the fragmentary
aboriginal dialects of
Tasmania. He left a
host of friends and a
few enemies—the lat-
ter the product of an
uncompromising out-
spokenness which im-
pelled him at times to
“speak faithfully” to
his adversary.

Maoriland’s prophet Rua, whose surnames
being interpreted are Canaan Hepzibah; ran
up pretty well the whole gamut of tapu
Scriptural names in his bush-Mormon career.
He began unnretentiously by comparing
himself to Solomon, which was warranted
by his penchant for fat young wahines and
plenty of them. Next he was John the
Baptist, because, as he explained, John and
he both lifted up their voices in the wilder-
ness and lived on scrub turkeys and damper.
Then he gave the prophet Isaiah a turn,
and encouraged by the applause of the
Urewera brethren he grabbed the halo of
the Wairua tapu, the Holy Ghost. A little
later he explained to a scared constable that
he was Jesus Christ. The policeman retired
to break the news to the Government, and
when the Government sent another Hop to
verify the information he found that Rua had
gained a step in rank in the interval. “I
am a god,” said the holy man. His next
move was into plain, ordinary quod.

Burnet Gray, an occasional Bulletin
contributor, is off to the war in the capacity
of company sergeant-major. Gray, who
still looks as young as he feels, is a coun-
cillor of St. Ivilda (A 7 ic.) these times —the
third St. Ivilda councillor to go where duty
calls and danger waits them. Brigadier-
General Hughes and Major Duigan are the
other two.
CLEON.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Cleon lias gone to gaping Syracuse—
Onr Cleon, safely smuggled, to diffuse
The dreams of Labor and Democracy;
Across a greedy, trade-embittered sea,
Safely lie's stewed to TTarmswortli’s friendly realm,
Youth at the prow, and Pleasure at the helm,
.roll ns on the poop to save him from the foe,
With handy basins round the bunk below.
An eager crowd awaits him on the pier,
The high priests snigger, and the small boys cheer.
Great Cleon! ’Tis the time for festival,
'The flutes are playing in the banquet hall,
'The meats are smoking, and the tables spread,
Where wait Sicilian damsels garlanded ;
Bald-headed elder statesmen, grave and bland,
Plunged in deep thought, extend a withered hand ;
And as the lemonade and buns go round,
Secrets of State and stratagems profound
Are whispered, as a prelude, all the while
The pompous matrons gossip, gush and smile.
Ah! this is better than a mess of beans!
He learns at last, learns what an Empire means,
Eyeing the table with fat turkeys spread.
(The hungry sheep look up and are not fed.)
Doubts are resolved, his speech is ready now.
Why should lie plod behind a four-ox plough
In far Boetia? For lie’s darkly thinking
Diplomacy’s devised by eating, drinking,
And how a nation at a feast divine
Is sometimes bartered o’er a jug of wine.
Cleon arises, and his wild eyes gleam ;
His rhetoric bursts forth, a lava stream,
Grander than Etna ; fierce gesticulation
Electrifies the half-awakened nation.
lie claws the air, then like a tiger springs,
Turns somersaults about the whirling rings,
Rides on the lightning, and directs the gale,
To point a moral or adorn a tale;
From sphere to sphere in splendid frenzy hurled
Lest one good purpose should corrupt the world,
Rumbles, spits fire, in voice of thunder calls,
Dazzles some venerable generals,
And frets and struts his hour upon the stage
Greedy to shine in the next morning's page,
Startles the Harmsworth press to cables fine,
Feels at each par., and lives along the line.
But when the blazing peroration’s o’er
They find he's said what they’ve all said before.
What of Boetia? Let Boetia keep
Her humble place, and clip the woolly sheep,
In pastoral peace, nor strive aught else to know
But how the lucerne springs, the pumpkins grow.
The tables roar. 'The eldest statesman wakes,
Rises on crutches, gives his hand, and shakes.
'The people shout: “Hail, bail to Cleon’s name!”
And: “God's demesne, Boetia!” they exclaim.
Not once or twice in our colonial stoiy
The path to Iloundsditeh was the path to glory.
Perchance he feels some vague presentiment
His blushing honors may be permanent.
But what of Labor? Let the workers frown—■
Have they yet met him with a parsley crown?
And what of Nationhood? A tiresome theme!
Draw close the silken bonds, prolong the dream!
VICTORIA.

This looks better. But things are really not
quite as good as that. The true amount
of capital not represented by tangible assets
is more than £5592, because the total
amount received as premiums has been taken
credit for, and no charge has been made
against it in respect of unexpired risks. The
net premiums for the year were £93,516,
and it is probable that, as an average, the
policies have at least four months to run —
they may have more. Plainly, then, those
premiums have been only two-thirds earned.
Deduct from them the commissions and
expenses of management, say £36,(X>O. and
divide the remainder by three, and you have
about £20.000 which the co. ought to have
put by in an Unearned Premiums Fund.
That would bring the'deficit to over £25,000
—the amount which the co. must make
before it can say that the capital is really
intact. On the other hand, there is the
goodwill of the business: and though good-
will has no place in a fire office’s balance-
sheet Tiie Bulletin certainly isn't pre-
pared to say that the 1.0.A.’s connection
isn’t now worth a good deal of money. The
directors say it was valued “for Federal
purposes' recently at £40.000. This paper
isn’t prepared to name any figure. But
with something like 1200 or 1300 share-
holders scattered, over the continent and
Tasmania, the co. is wonderfully well placed
to get a very big and cheap and profitable
business. So far it hasn’t had a very profit-
able business. The losses have been much
too large. Most of the 1200 or 1300 share-
holders no doubt have good business to place
—the private residence risk, for instance.
But it doesn’t look as if it was that sort
which* has flowed to the co. Anyway, the
loss rate last year was 55.6 p.c.. and that
is too high, especially in view of the large
number of new policies issued during the
year, many of them no doubt late in the
term. There is no good reason why share-
holders shouldn’t give the co. their business
—and there is plainly an excellent reason
why they should. The co. certainly hasn’t
the largest amount of funds in proportion
to risks of any co. in Australia; but its
position, on the other hand, isn’t weak. It
has £50,000 on deposit (including £lO,OOO
lodged with the Government of Victoria)
and £9OOO in public loans, besides mortgages
and freeholds: and though it has £28.828
of liabilities (which seems excessive), it
also has £20.285 owing by agents and sundry
debtors (which seems excessive too) and
£4192 of cash balances.
The progress of the business is further
shown in this comparison:—

The share capital is £74.245. So an amount
in excess of the capital and the ordinary
reserves is represented by “shares in other
cos.”; and that being so the co.’s own
accounts don’t give its shareholders much of
an idea as to what their investment is worth.
For instance, these other cos. may be accu-
mulating large reserves —or, again, they
may be doing the other thing. The assets
of the “other cos.” consist very largely of
unpaid balances on purchases; and these
other cos. may have contingency reserves,
or this co.'s own reserves may have to pro-
vide for the contingencies. It is obviously,
then, not a concern to be bought into on
the strength of what its own balance-sheet
tells you. If everything is worth just what
the balance-sheet says it is worth —including
the shares in other cos. —each £1 share is
represented by about 335. of assets W hat-
ever it may be now it was no doubt a fine
purchase when the Baillieus got their hands
on it. W. L., J. L. Wharton and Hyndman
are the directors, with a London board
which originally represented the old boom
banks’ creditors, but is now of not much
consequence.

This is the first time for many a year that
no profit on sales has been shown. Most

manner of speaking. The society, having
been left with a large mass of securities on
its hands 20-odd years ago. wrote them
down and reduced its capital by half: and
the ‘'profit" simply means that things turned
out less badly than had been thus provided
for. Now the chances are that property-

“A large amount of business has been done
with the Commonwealth and State Govern-
ments during the half-year.” the directors
say; and that no doubt accounts for the
abundance of milk in the half-year’s cocoa-
nut. The time must of course come when
the Governments won’t be able to borrow;
X. S. Wales, for instance, has in the past
eight years raised half as much as it did
in all its previous 110 years, and it is quite
absurd to suppose that that sort of thing
can continue. How insane the policy is
may be judged from the fact that, after
lately spending £8,000,000 a year, allegedly
in developing the country, the Government
now declares that it must go on borrowing
in order to prevent widespread unemploy-
ment. Even if the country could go on
borrowing—which it won’t be able to do —
nothing is more certain than that the people
will presently see what a fraud the whole
thing has been, and will stop it. And then
there is going to be unemployment. The
X. S. Wales Government says truly that that
is' what the stoppage of borrowing would
mean now. But it will mean that whenever it
is stopped: and the longer it goes on the
worse the position will be when it is stopped.
The cry of distress will go up, and there is
nothing like that cry for inciting private
people to follow the State and shut down
on expenditure, especially in building ana
construction. The Bulletin therefore sees
a slack time coming for those in the timbei
trade. This co. will, however, be in a better
position to meet it than some others, because
its stocks are comparatively small and its
liabilities exceptionally light. Stock runs
to onlv £29.618 and total liabilities are
£lB 073, against which the co. has book
debts of £11,333. The rest of its assets con-
sist chiefly of mills, vessels and punts, a
£15.000 mill, plant and wharf at lihrs
Point (Sydney), and suburban yards, lhe
share capital is £75,000.

In addition to that £26.951 of reserves there
is £19,000 to the credit of a “special reserve
for doubtful debts.” Ordinarily such a fund
wouldn't be regarded as a reserve at all: the
common practice is to write down
the book-debts asset by the amount
of the fund. In this case, however,
it may be a more or less genuine
reserve, because the profits are shown "•after
providing for bad and doubtful debts, and
there is £152 brought into profit from the
recovery of bad debts previously written off.
If the practice is to write down all debts
considered bad or doubtful, then the specific
reserve might better be called a reserve for
contingencies. But The Bulletin views
the figures of this co. with some suspicion.
For example, in an official “circular letter
to shareholders” explaining the position, it
is said: “The figures show that the assets
of the co. are sufficient to pay all the present
liabilities (£123.473) and all the capital
paid up by shareholders (£164.7351 and
then leave a sum .of £75.701 as a surplus.”
If that were soothe total of the assets
should be £366.909, and they are nothing of
the sort: they are. according to the balance-
sheet. only £354,823 —and that is giving all
the book debts (against which there is a
reserve) the credit for being worth 20s. in
the £.
Excluding the reserve against doubiful
debts, the position has moved as follows
(the figures are as at Jan. 31. before making
the annual appropriations) :
1911. 1915. 1916.
Capital and reserves .. £86,338 £199,262 £214,849
Liabilities . . . . 72,872 92,103 123,474
Propertv accounts .. 19,702 26,519 32,398
Book debts .. .. 136,688 260.971 298,836
Stock 5.092 17,469 23.037
Cash, etc. .. . . 231 406 552
The liabilities include moneys owing on cur-
rent account (£66.023). on fixed deposits and
on overdraft and mortgage. The book lebts
are nearly all "amounts due to co. on current
account.” The co. thus appears to be doing
something very like a banking business; and
what sort of a fist it .will make of it depends
chiefly on its management. The weak point
is that, with £66.023 owing by the co. on
current account, the cash on hand amounts
to only £4Bl : and a bank director who saw
his bank issue such figures would wonder
what sort of a time he was in for. Yet.
provided there is no untidy rush for money,
the concern is thoroughly sound, assuming
that ordinary prudence has been used in
making advances or giving credit. The co.

are £354.823 of assets: and no ordinary bank
can show figures like that.

* * *
The recent bubble was nicely blown but
the expected has happened—the*inside crowd
and brokers have the cash, and Perth and
Westonia punters the scrip. One dav the
Central management mailed a list of assav<
running up to 10oz., on the mine-office door
"When Westonia punters saw this they fell
over themselves in their rush to the' tele-
graph office with buying orders up to £5 Ilk
The publication of assays in this wav
was a simple move, and those who knew
enough to come in out of the wet saw
through it; but Westonia punters did not
even begin to think till the lOoz. assays
were followed by -a 13dwt. clean-up. I t
has been suggested that there is a bullion
reserve at the mine, but nothing definite is
known about this except that assays such
as have been published from the Central
certainly ought, in view of last month’s yield
to be able to provide two bullion reserves.
Edna May Deeps is “still cementing: hope
resume sinking soon.’* So run the official
reports. The truth is the Deeps has no
earthly chance of striking the Edna May
reef in the main shaft with present water
appliances, and most of those intimately con-
cerned with the show know it. Sydney
bought this stock pretty solidly at two pounds
and can now console itself with the fact
that it possesses something dear at much
less than that.
Consolidated has exposed a few tons of
low-grade ore. but there is nothing exposed
in the mine at present to justify the hope
that the stock is yet worth more than Is.
Golden Points had a flutter also, but as
regards this show it can be said that the
manager has been distinctly moderate in his
official representations as to the value of
developments. Westonia punters boomed
this stock on their own account, and if they
have been left with some wallpaper they
have only themselves to blame.
The Battler is erecting plant to deal with
a vein exposed in the Battler workings. The
vein is certainly small: nevertheless, judged
from the point of view of ore reserves. Bat-
tlers are worth Deeps, Consolidated and
Golden Points combined.
* * * *
Edna May has paid £175.000 in dividends
and is now bagging specimen ore from lower
workings: but the public hears little of this
sort of thing. Edna May people appear to
permit yields to do the talking. But what
a rattle there would be if some of the
adjoining shows could get a few bags or
even a few ounces of such ore as is now
being broken in Edna May!
* * * *
The Westralian Board appointed a year
ago to attend to miners’ complaints has
given the first annual account of itself. All
sorts of cases have been handled. One man
crippled with rheumatism was sent away on
a stretcher and returned full of gratitude
without the aid of even a stick. Another
man with falling eyesight was sent to an
eastern specialist and underwent a success-
ful operation. Still another, with heart dis-
ease. was found lighter and more suitable
occupation, while the number of tubercular
cases were far too numerous to particularise.
In every instance, the Board paid 1
patient’s expenses, cost of treatment, etc.
and made a living allowance to dependen t
during his absence. In one case it
more. A miner borrowed £lO from a mon .
lender six years ago, and alter P t
ing £4O in interest he still 0
£l7. The Board paid the debt. an“
is allowing the beneficiary to refund i
small instalments without interest.

“Why should we use it?’’ said a well-known mine-
manager. “What’s the advantage?’’ This gave us
another oppovtunity of emphasising the sterling
qualities of “Chromol” belting—its durability, its
pliability, its dependability under all conditions.
“Chromol” is mineral tanned, which gives the leather
a longer fibre, consequently it is more elastic and
more durable. It resists heat and moisture and does
not readily deteriorate. Used exclusively on many
of the stiffest drives in the largest mining plants in
the Commonwealth, it has proved its merit under
most severe conditions. J. C. Ludowici & Son, Ltd.,
“Pioneer” Leather Works, 117 York Street, Sydney.

ALUMINOFERRIC is used by the Sydney Water
Board, New South Wales Public Water Supply Depart-
ment, and many country Water Boards and graziers
for precipitating impurities and clarifying water in
reservoirs, cattle dams, etc. It is simply thrown
into the reservoir in the form of a solid cake, or
dissolved and added as a liquid. In three hours’
time the most discolored water will be as clear as
crystal. One pound will clear over 2000 gallons of
water. It is exceedingly cheap and quite harmless.
Sole agents: William Adams & Company Limited,
Sydney, Melbourne, Brisbane and Perth. — Advt.
For rapid and accurate sizing of samples, the
Hoover Rapid Laboratory Sizer is unsurpassed. An
ordinary hand test over 12 different mesh screens
takes about 10 hours to complete ; the Hoover Sizer
will make the same test in about half an hour. By
the use of one of these Sizers the saving in time
would, in a very short while, cover the small initial
cost of the machine. The machine can be dressed
with any series of screens. The Austral Otis Eng.
Co., Ltd., of Melbourne, will be pleased to furnish
particulars.— Advt. [3

Agents and Correspondents throughout the World,
the relief continues for life; if they are
killed their children are maintained till they
are self-supporting, and their widows during
their widowhood. As soon as the accumu-
lations and contributions are in excess of
the .requirements, as ascertained by the
Government Actuary, the scale is altered.
Two alterations, both in favor of the con-
tributors—who, by the way, supply only
half the funds —have been made already.
Altogether, it is the finest insurance scheme
in existence in Australia; and that the
accumulation of funds should be regarded in
the West as a failure is one of the weirdest
jokes this paper has heard for some time.
An almost amazing body of ore has been
run into by the diamond drill which is prob-
ifig at the 12170 ft. (bottom) level in the
Broken Hill South. The bore was put out
so as to cut as nearly as possible across the
lode at right angles to the walls ; and on a
recent measurement 94ft. of ore had been
disclosed, running up to most extraordinary
values: —
Lead. Zinc.
