So for 38ft. this body contains (besides
unmentioned silver) 58.2 p.c. of metal. The
managing director doesn’t seem to over-state
the case when he adds to the figures the
note: “This is a very important develop-
ment.” Previous to this the ore reserves
were estimated at 3,350,000 tons; and on
past performances The Bulletin (9/3/T6)
reckoned there was an “ultimate value” of
upwards of £lO per share in that lot and in
the liquid assets in hand.
« * « ft
. Vegetable Creek (N.S.W.) hands out a
sixpenny, and the Board has added
£l5OO to the reserve of £lOOO. Enuna-
ville produced 1045 tons of high-grade and
easily-smelted stream tin last year; and this
in' its journey to the seaboard was railed
over the most extensive coal-field in Aus-
tralia. With cheap coal and coke we have
unrivalled advantages for smelting tin ore
and the production of tin plate; still we
continue to export ore and import plate.
Just now this madness comes home to us,
because we have to pay unheard-of freight
on shipments both ways.
Newcastle (N.S.W.) exported only 777,359
tons of coal during the first three months of
this year; it sent off 1,013,001 tons for a
similar period of 1915. Those mines that
depend on the export trade are having a
bad year. But it is only temporary —there
is- a good time coming with the development
of metallurgical and secondary industries.
* * * *
New Chum Goldfields, Bendigo, having
lost the shoot of gold that brought it such a
patch of dividends 12 months ago, has been
coveting its neighbor’s vineyard. This
desire to grab related to a slab of 600 feet
held by the big Victoria Proprietary pro John
Bull on the Nell Gwynne line. London has
put £lOO,OOO into this Bendigo gamble and
got in return a couple of divs., a deep shaft
and a spick-and-span plant. As more cash
cannot be raised in England just now, and as
three years have passed since the tributors
gave the mine best, New Chum Goldfields
had a fair claim to follow its reef over the
boundary into the Victoria Prop.’s ground,
provided it was ready to man the area.
Now it gets the block from the Warden on
that understanding, leaving the Victoria
Prop, with shaft and plant to expand in the
other direction if it cares to blossom out
again.
Home Reef, at Wandiligong (Vic.),
started with a shoot 30ft. long. At 130 ft.
depth it had developed to 300 ft. in length.
A section 70ft. south kept about the lOdwt.
mark; then came 50ft. of poor stuff, next
50ft. of Bdwt. ground ; finally 130 ft. of 4dwt.
reef. Costs are fairly cheap in the district,
but a new shaft is needed, with the testing
of other reefs on the claims. The stone
broken so far has averaged about Bdwt., and
with a sft. reef showing at 135 ft. it is worth
while seeing whether better values make in
the deep ground.
* * * *
The Victorian gold yield is shrinking
visibly owing to the tired feeling of call-
payers and the few really good divs. that
are being produced. March saw only
24,5000 z. won, a decline of 93500 z. compared
with March, 1915. This tailing-off has gone
on all this year, so that for the past quarter
the gold won (63,3000 z.) was 15,4000 z. less
than in the first term of 1915.
Justice Powers’s dictum that if the gold-
mining industry couldn’t pay better wages it
had better die is knocking the stuffing out of
Ballarat East shows. The other day Vic-
toria United decided to give life best. New
Normanby would have next shut down if the
miners hadn’t practically contracted them-
selves out of the Arbitration Court’s award.
Now Llanberris No. 1 is begging alms of
the State Treasurer. This is to enable it to
prospect at 1200 ft. for the big make of
quartz that kept the co. alive and gave tri-
butors a crust when jogging along at 1100 ft.
Only £2OOO is needed, and the directors don’t
mind giving the State a lien over the plant
as security for a loan. A mine that has
turned out £400,000 of gold oughtn’t to have
to do such a crawl for that bit of cash for
development work. However, there it is;
and the situation is all the more perilous
because the closing-down of the Llanberris
would mean putting the extinguisher on
Llanberris South and Last Chance, with a
consequent throwing out of pretty w r ell 500
workers.
Something will have to liven Walhalla
(Vic.), or it will soon pass out as a mining
town. A few parties keep picking out
patches of good stone in the upper levels of
the old Long Tunnel, and there is talk of a
run of ounce yields, but a lot more ginger will
be necessary before the show will buzz even
at that point. Down south, Cohen’s Long
Tunnel has ceased flirting with greasy heads
and slippery ground above the battery site, so
there may be a chance of the stampers
rattling before the winter snows are over.
The co. has at last got its paw firmly on
the old South Long Tunnel lease. Hopes
run high that the spur reef out of which the
good assays came will be traced east of the
south shaft, as there will then be an induce-
ment to drive out to test it at depth. The
south end of Cohen’s reef can also be picked
over and a crosscut can be got out west to
test the reef under Goodwin’s shaft. All this
makes up an attractive little programme-—if
it were not for the uneasy feeling that that
interfering fellow, the old miner, may have
been there already.
* * * *
There is plenty of competition in Westra-
lia for the new billets of workmen in-
spectors of mines. Nominations are pouring
in, and upwards of a hundred threaten to go
to the ballot for the two full-time jobs at
Kalgoorlie. The union is urging the appoint-
ment of three full-time inspectors for the
other fields in place of half- and part-time
men. It says that if this plan is not adopted
some inspectors will get only one day’s
work a fortnight from the Department and
for the balance of the time will be merely
employees in the mines where probably they
have insisted on certain alterations being
made. This, as the union points out, won’t
do at all.
* * * *
Corinthian North (W.A.) reckons it has
turned the corner. Prospects in the sul-
phide area are good, and returns from the
plant now being installed are, from all
accounts, going to earn dividends almost
right away. To date the mine has pro-
duced about £40,000, but plant, develop-
ment, treatment troubles and transport
charges have swallowed it up, and a bit
more besides. Some 20,000 shares were
placed by the Board last year, and the co.’s
nominal capital is now £32,000 in 10s.
shares, of which Dorrie Doolette and friends
hold the bulk.
* * * *
Meekatharra (W.A.) is in trouble. For
weeks it has been paralysed by a miners’
strike at the Fenian, and now the Queen
of the Hills has added to its misery by
closing down. The stoppage of this show is
due to the want of a suitable mill to treat
refractory sulphides; and its present owner,
the Lake View and Oroya, can’t just now
finance a purchase.
Wilfrid Rickard, who ran the New Chum
Goldfields, Bendigo, for a couple of genera-
tions on 3dwt. stuff, is shifting camp to Mt.
Magnet (W.A.). There he is to try to make
a mine out of Hartrick’s Empress, which,
so far, has duffered out wherever worked.
That a man with Rickard’s reputation for
cheap handling should be sent to the job
makes it look as if the Empress wasn’t
reckoned an ounce show any more.
8., M. and Co. are gradually getting out
of the West. At one time they bossed
nearly all the best mines there, and with
the aid of Hoover, who was then a partner
in the firm, looked like getting the rest.
Then the Perseverance and Horseshoe, over
which they never had a very strong hold,
slipped through their fingers, and the South
Ivalgurli and several of the smaller fry
parted from them some years later. To-day
they are general managers of the Lake
View and Star, Oroya Links, Yuanmi,
Great Fingall and Sons of Gwalia; but they
are only nominally in control of the first
three, and are liable to be bumped out
any moment by Hoover, Govett and Co.,
who boss the Boards. In fact, the only
mine where their word is really law is the
Gwalia. The strange part of the business
is that their management is probably better
than ever it was.
Chaffers’ London liquidator is trying to
raise the dead. He proposes to form a new
free-from-debt co. to acquire any portion of
the old mine that can be got “on reasonable
terms” and to take bver two leases known
as the Auckland and Fortuna on the Mt.
Margaret field. From these latter, it is
stated, £20,000 worth of gold has already
been obtained. The new concern’s nominal
capital is £02,500, in 1,000,000 shares of
Is. 3d. ; but as the float is being engineered
by a London syndicate which wants 100,000
paid shares and £5OOO in cash for its ser-
vices, the dead is likely to remain where it is.
Black Range’s old mine at Sandstone
(W.A.) has passed away. Its life was not
a long one, but its performances were good.
Altogether some 200,000 odd tons were
extracted and treated for nearly three-quar-
ters of a million sterling, and shareholders
got about a third of it in dividends. The
Board spent a good deal of time and money
in searching for a new property, and after
turning down several fastened on to the
Pinnacles, near Cue. The plant has been
removed to this show, and crushing will
be started very shortly. According to official
reports the Pinnacles is on a fairly good
wicket. It has £lB,OOO in hand, an easily-
worked lode formation of £2 to £3 value,
and over £lOO,OOO profit in sight.
* * * *
Capital is being sought in London to
resurrect Randall’s (W.A.), but it would
be far better to let the dead rest. The
Santa Claus syndicate, which started two
years ago to bring the field back to life, has
given up hope after spending £13,000 of its
own and about £6OOO of Government money.
With others Santa Claus is now turning its
attention to a line-deposit concession at Lake
Clifton, in the South-West (W.A.), out of
which it expects to make a recovery.
* * * *
Yilgarn Consols (W.A.) has come to
light after what a Kalgoorlie paper terms
“a long seclusion” and is busy window-dress-
ing the Karridale at Burtville. The Consols
was floated two years ago with a slender
capital to make money out of an alleged
mine near Southern Cross. It failed, and
nothing was heard of it until it reappeared
on the Karridale. The latter is a 24-acre
block with a narrow reef from which 4000
tons have been extracted for a return of
£50,000. Water beat the prospectors at
80ft. and the show was abandoned. Opinions
are conflicting in regard to it, but The
Bulletin believes it would turn out all
right if worked on a small scale by a crowd
which knew the meaning of economy.
At the annual meeting of the Chamber of
Mines (W.A.) in Kalgoorlie the other day
Dick Hamilton, of the Gt. Boulder, presided
for the nineteenth time in succession.
* * * *
Leisner’s Wolfram Block (Q.) has been
placed on the list of the Sydney Stock
Exchange. There are 75,000 shares of 6s.
each; 50,000 of these are paid up, and a
recently issued 10,000 contribs. still leave
15,000 in reserve. Leisner’s is one of the
two richest little wolfram claims in N.Q. The
ore is a mixture of that metal with molyb-
denite and bismuth, occurring in pipes near
the contact of granite and slate. The patchy
nature of the ore is proved by the small
output of only 1800 tons of high-grade stuff
from Wolfram Camp in 17 years. Leisner’s
is down 450 ft. and is the deepest claim on
these rare metals in Queensland, and one
of the deepest in the world. In six months
290 tons were mined and crushed for £3780.
The co. owes £lOOO to the Queensland Gov-
ernment, and recently increased its capital
to equip the claim properly and push on
development.
* * * *
Bonnie Dundee, Charters Towers, has
crushed 1011 tons for £5153, roughly £5 2s.
per ton, compared with £7 4s. 9d. from the
previous crushing. Wages, stores, etc., are
all on the rise, so Bonnie will take breathing
time between dividends. News from the
mine is rather depressing.
G. D. Meudell says he is not going to let
Treasurer Higgs beat him when it comes
to chasing oil in Tas. A veto having been
put on the raising of the capital in Australia,
Meudell observes that he is going to get the
needful outside Australia. To which, of
course, Higgs will have no objection what-
ever.
* * * »
Tasmanian tin claims are recovering their
lost strength. Briseis from the main work-
ings got 43 tons of black tin in March, which
compares with 36 tons in February. Mutual
Hill gave two tons last month. Mt.
Bischoff reported 35 tons black tin for the
month. Bischoff carries 12.000 shares, while
Briseis has 600,000. Briseis is entirely allu-
vial and has paid £402,000, compared with
Bischoff’s £2,436.000; but the latter has had
a much longer life and had the advantage
of securing a virgin claim and the most
remarkable tin deposit in Australia. There
are still 1,600,000 tons of gossan, porphyry,
slate and detrital matter, but it is very low
grade.
There is a nice bonus brewing for Queens-
land Insurance shareholders. Present paid
capital is £150,000, in £1 shares. The formal
reserve fund was last year carried up to
£125,000; and, besides, there is a reserve of
over £lOO,OOO to cover unexpired risks. How
much of the £125,000 will be transferred to
capital hasn’t been disclosed; but probably,
in view of the corpulence of the unearned
premium fund, £25,000 will be thought suffi-
cient to leave as a formal reserve. The
important question, of course, is What will
they pay on that? On past performances
10 p.c. would appear to be quite possible.
Speakman’s (W.A.) is going to have
another try. A supply of fresh water is
being laid on to the battery, and the co.
hopes thus to reconcile extraction with assay
values. About 50 tons, estimated to average
between 9dwt. and lOdwt., is awaiting treat-
ment, and a fair quantity of similar stuff is
said to be in sight. Whether there is enough
to give shareholders a dividend is, however,
extremely doubtful.
From Meekatharra :—•
Fenian has a labor dispute on hand over the
question of a minimum wage of 13s. 4d. a shift.
The mine has been closed two months now. The
syndicate is prepared to pay the wages, but refuses
to sign an agreement, and Bill refuses to work with-
out an agreement. Queen of the Hills has shut up
its treatment plant, and only a few men are em-
ployed. They are prospecting at No. 6 level. The
local yarn is that the tandem gas-engine didn't
prove a success, hence the closure. The Com-
modore has given it best, and hauled up rails, air-
pipes, etc. Treatment of rccumulated slimes is
expected to return a profit. Ingliston, adjoining, is
crosscutting for the reef at the bottom level, with
a hard bar in the face, which is not improving the
prospects. Ingliston Consols is opening up well at
No. 8 level, and locally is hailed as another Fenian,
which mine it adjoins. Ingliston Extended (gener-
ally under a cloud) is crosscutting and driving for
the West lode at the 550 ft. level. The mill is
going on ore from the East lode, which has length-
ened and improved in value at this depth.

BOTTLED IN FRANCE.
A GOOD REMEDY FOR
SALLOW COMPLEXIONS.
Everyone may have a clear and bright
complexion, free from furrows or wrinkles, if
they will only take a little daily trouble in
the following manner : Buy some Puritol wax
from the chemist or draper, and apply every
night, lap a little cold water on the face ten
times with the hands every morning, allow-
ing it to get thoroughly round the closed
eyes, and pinch the skin very gently across
all wrinkles. Puritol Wax Soap, which
leaves a fine film of wax on the skin, should
be used ; this acts as a protective during the
day. This treatment arouses the dormant
nerves and blood vessels, and undoubtedly
accounts for many of the clear complexions
so much admired by those who do not yet
know the secret.— Advt. [3

SCOTS WHISKY
A WOMAN'S
LETTER
Sydney, Afrit. 24, 1916.
My Dear Arinj.—
The G.-G. took a preliminary canter round
the Show before it was opened with a cork-
screw. Clad in his Quaker-grey _ rigout,
with his favorite pudding-basin millinery,
he interviewed the uttermost pig on Tues-
day. On Thursday the grounds teemed with
Excellencies. The Victorian Stanleys had
fine weather for their excursion. But
the poor old public got more or less drenched
in Friday’s .downpour, and stormed the
trams in dripping battalions.
A genuine blue-and-gold day—made only in
Australia —arched the formal opening of the
Show. The Strickland chaise drove into the
ring with Lady Edeline, his Ex. and three
daughters. The eldest treasure’s white-cloth
coat —one of the long pleated species—and
trim skirt were amongst the best exhibits.
President Sam Hordern —a proud man the
day, with his first Show breaking a string of
records —met the party. Then the prize bone
and beef made a stately progress round the
sward. The haughty tread of the beef—
notably the Shorthorns, who are It this year
—showed an instinctive sense of the price of
steak. Later those present saw his Ex. open
the carnival. Hearing was out of the ques-
tion. The orchestra in the ring simply
drowned the soloist.
The Women’s Industries were worse even
than usual. One section is devoted to crochet
quilts—hideous cross-stitch of the old carpet-
slipper pattern—and knobby cushions ; and
it interests nobody but the exhibitors.
Needlecraft, except in the lingerie cases,
apparently reached the “worst ever” this
show. Red Cross activity in the country is
responsible for the feeble response to the
call of an always'-weak pavilion.
In the pumpkin shed the mere house-
keeper could only gaze in stunned amaze-
ment at the produce from the Promised
Land. Pyramids of fruit and vegetables,
cured pig, and mountains of cheese and
butter showed that Australia can still grow
more than it consumes. The dark-eyed lady
or gentleman in the city protests that these
things are “ver’ scarce” and sells doubtful
apples at 2d. apiece. Yet Batlow alone,
judging by its exhibit, could supply the
whole of our Best Suburbs.
The Women’s Horticultural Guild has a
tea and jam tent, but it only got its sign
up after the opening day. Visitors previ-
ously unable to find this retreat then rushed
the provender. Proceeds go towards a
depot in the city which hopes to inciden-
tally benefit the wives of returned soldiers.
Mrs. Bertram Stevens, the president, did the
star turn as scone-maker. She was occa-
sionally sent for from her fastness in front
of the stove to explain that domestic science
would be included in the spade-ladies’ cur-
riculum. Mrs. Lindsay, formerly well
known at Forbes, was general manager of
the forage department.
In spite of a gorgeous Easter we couldn’t
get away from the war. The will of the
late Major Jenkins, killed at Gallipoli, was
proved during the week. The estate is
worth just under £14,000. The popular
ox-artilleryman was a brother of Mrs.
J. H. P. Murray, who comes this way for
the winter.
At the big Show lunch Rider Haggard’s
easy impromptu remarks were vociferously
encored. Printer Sam Lees, in his other-
wise spirited address on things in general,
came a literary cropper. He alluded “to
that delightful novel, ‘Far from the Madding
Crowd,’ by our distinguished visitor.” Then
there was a great peace.
After Friday’s storm Easter Saturday
bounced in with cloudless skies and a nip
in the air—just the weather the race-goers
would have ordered. Cool enough for the
new winter garb, not too cold for the last
ninons of summer. Velvet, with a fur
edge, is the primary fancy of the season.
Made in one piece, or with the long, wide
coat falling over the short skirts, it is put
together with sufficient breadth to house a
crinoline. With this rig-out the Philip
Morton girls wore the latest tartlet milli-
nery. The latest touch, a tinsel belt, cheap-
ened the look of one otherwise handsome
frock.
In spite of its habit of travelling to the
cleaners every few week-ends, white cloth
is In. A creamy princess robe (a thing we
haven’t seen in winter stuff for years),
edged with brown fur, was worn by a fas-
cinating stranger. Nigger brown—it’s a
few shades off ebony—is having a great
innings. The Tom Hughes lady was clothed
in silk of this Uncle Tom tint. Mrs.
Arthur Griffith, in a cosy fur coat, was
spotted with ermine here and there. Mrs.
Spencer Brunton took out from her Sara-
toga in the recent unpacking the latest gasp
in striped black silk. It’s so new that it looks
mid-Victorian, for that's where the tight-
fitting jacket and wide, plain skirt (the
latter suggested a wired understudy) came
from.
Paradise plumes tossed on many a race
hat, in spite of infuriated bird-lovers. The
ostrich is taking a comparative rest, though
he’s still plucked for neckwear. A grass-green
silk with a high storm frill, worn by Mary
Grimley, was on the summery side. Here
and there a daring damsel stepped out in
white muslin. Leopard-skin mats trailed
round other shoulders, and ears were rugged
in Antarctic collars.
Ex-Mayoress Richards, quietly gowned,
was in the members’ reserve for a while.
But the G.-G.’s ukase, “No races till after
the war,” either kept most of our Second-
best away or incited them to make a stealthy-
rush at the shrouded official stand. What
the vice-regal young gentlemen have done
with themselves, besides inspecting mangel-
wurzels, goodness knows! 'What they have
said on each race morn —but no! Let’s try
not to forget we’re ladies.
Dr. Roger Hughes, in khaki, paced the
lawn with his Eileen. Her other name was
Maher a few weeks ago. Mr. Arthur Gor-
don had a daughter or two under his wing,
but they were in the semi-disguise of ultra-
plain frocks. A stack of country McMasters
and Campbells and a woolly Merewether
or two were in the station contingent.
In town for race week is Mrs. Kenneth
Mackay, the tall, picturesque lady from Wall-
endbeen homestead. The Pine Ridge Buck-
lands, as usual, followed the geegee to Rand-
wick. Mrs. Lex Craig—she was a
Queensland belle not so many years ago—
finishes a summer here with the races before
booking her sleeper for Brisbane. Mrs. Yald-
wyn, mother of Beatrice, came from Queens-
land to meet her just arrived-from-London
girl, and she, too, took an outing at Rand-
wick.
This is Cicely
Courtneidge, who
was married in
London the other
day - dressed for
her part in “The
Arcadians.” She’s
one of the daintiest
little things on the
English stage —in
father’s companies,
of course. Cicely
was born in Aus-
tralia, and, a dozen
years later, when
Dad was “produe-
in g” Shakspeare
for his old friend
Musgrove, she
made her first
stage appearance
here as Pease-
blossom. She might
at least have had her wedding in Australia,
too.
The G.-G. and his lady went up to St.
Ignatius’s College, on “the River,” the other
day. A watery guard of honor, the renowed
college eights, splashed to meet them. The shy-
est person on the premises, the learned Father
Pigot, who knows nearly all the world
knows about earthquakes, has to be lassoed
on such occasions. Besides having his
vast knowledge of seismic thrills, this
modest cleric is quite a brilliant
pianist. Sydneysiders who travelled to
England in the same mailboat used
to wonder who the retiring musician was who
fled into silence when footsteps approached.
Finally the earthquake authority was run to
ground as the surreptitious performer. But
he could rarely be persuaded to oblige an
audience.
Colonel Sandford, of the Permanent
Artillery, goes into “A” Quarters in Vic-
toria Barracks. He has been State Com-
mandant in S. Australia, so it’s like promo-
tion backwards to take a second place here
under somebody whose life’s job hasn’t been
soldiering. But that's the kind of thing the
professional soldier is used to nowadays. He
has made the stupid blunder of only studying
military tactics.
Do you remember the tall Queensland sub.,
young Kelly? For a while he fretted on
the Permanent chain in Victoria. Now he’s
off to join a Flying Corps at the Front. His
orderly, by the way, who enlisted as a full
private, has an income of £5OOO or so a
year. No wonder poor old Tommy Atkins
wonders how the Australian does himself
so well on 355. a week!
Jack An till, who has been through the
Gallipoli turmoil, is now Brigadier-General
somewhere at the Front. That’s all the
damages he has sustained up to date.
Lieut. Clive Backhouse, of a Strathfield
family, had a date with his best girl, Vera
Rogers, of Chatswood, the other day. Clive,
by the way, is the fourth to get into khaki
in that household. The other three brothers
are already in the thick of the uproar. The
bride’s plumage was white shimmer with a
snowfall of tulle, garlanded with rosebuds.
A couple of warriors in khaki looked after
the bridesmaids, and a few dozen Back-
houses, including his Honor, were present to
show that there was no ill-feeling.
Town and Country Matrons have put their
purses together as of yore. Their pre-war
Easter hop is resuscitated this week as a
mixed show. After the concert wilts away
our Second-best People are once more pre-
pared to dance till the early milkman ham-
mers at the back gate. The Very Best are
themselves hammering at a gate. Tickets are
7s. 6d. Proceeds are to be cut up between
the Manly hospital and the French’s Forest
fund. Mrs. Jack Campbell and Mrs. Hotham
are moving spirits in the affair. At the
Pacific (Manly) on April 28.
A good-bye sing-song to Lieut. Carmichael,
the recent Minister for Education who bad
most to do with turning Government House
stables into a Conservatorium, drew a fair
audience to the old mews. After a tempestu-
ous blare down Macquarie-street the Rifle-
men’s battalion band stamped up with a
tail of casual followers and stray dogs. The
programme had the Verbrugghen quartette
to tone up the first half. Lottie Dearn ham-
mered conscientiously at Chopin’s study in
C Minor after ambling through a Grieg noc-
turne. When a long list of performers
snatched one bar rest the Conservatorium
staff, per Verbrugghen, handed the departing
sojer a silver photo-frame shrining the Bel-
gian conductor’s pensive countenance. Mrs.
Carmichael, in a becoming white frock, got a
presentation posy and took it into a front
seat, with Chief Secretary Black and his
wife near by.
Dr. and Mrs. McGill’s only daughter Nell
has agreed to be Best Queen to Pex-cy King,
of Tulloona station, out Moree way. The
McGills used to mend the broken bones of
that woolly district, but they’re Bingara
folks of late.
Mrs. Pelloe, the Mildura lady who sat
in her saddle all the way to Sydney—it’s
nearly 700 miles—is a tall, slender young
woman with a soft voice, a velvet manner
and a steel wrist. She rides either side-
saddle or astride, but prefers the old way when
she’s taking the jumps. One of Victoria’s lead-
ing amateur horsewomen, she’s been collecting
show-ring prizes for years. “Our horses
were tired when we reached Sydney,” she
says, “but my friend Miss Cox and I were so
fresh that —so we were told afterwards —we
looked as if we’d just ridden into the city
from Centennial Park. Some of the
back hotels —we couldn’t always reach a
homestead —were fairly rough, though we
enjoyed the experience. The landlord would
come out to greet us. After trying to
‘place’ us, he would say: ‘Hello, Missus!
First or second class?’ If what we had
here and there was first class, we’re glad we
weren’t crowded into the other.”
This year the Maddrell family came to
town from Braidwood for more reasons than
the Show. Father gives his daughter May
to one of the Downes brothers of Brownlow
Hill early this week. Brother Edgar mar-
ried Isabel, the elder Maddrell girl, some
time ago. I fancy that one has been in khaki
for months.
“Peg o’ My Heart,” at the Palace, gives
the jaded playgoer a taste of the real
thing. I mean the Dublin brogue which
falls from the lips of Sara Allgood like
clotted cream. The “Irish” hero and
heroine generally own up to Putney or
’Ampstead as their native heath. Sarapeg’s
touzled yellow curls are a good many shades
lighter than the coat of her wild Irish ter-
rier, but they both fit the part. Tait’s lead-
ing lady is a plump little dumpling with
the beauty of rounded arm and dimpled
hand that Erin’s daughters generally pre-
sent to an admiring world.
A granddaughter of the late Francis
Suttor, M.L.C., walked down St. James’s
aisle on the arm of Lieut. Alan Audsley, of
the R.F.A. The bride, Margaret Collam, is
the Sandeman Collams’ only girl. Mother
was a Suttor.
Ruby, a daughter of the late David Rob-
ertson, of Betliunga Park and Rockview
station, beyond old Junee, held out her finger
when Hugh Aytoun-Young. a Scot from
Perthshire,_ produced the wedding-ring.. The
Mosman kirk was trimmed with flowers for
the occasion. The bride’s snow-white drapery
was veiled in Honiton lace. The white
heather in her posy travelled from Scotland
to be amongst those present. The brides-
maids, Grace Robertson and wee Effie
Fraser, were our home-grown blooms.
One Ruby reminds me of another. Ruby
Winckler, the artist-girl with the wonderful
imagination, came home the other day. She
has, as you know, been illustrating" fairy-

Surgeon Herbert Moran—he who used to
specialise in the muddied football field-
goes to N’York in June. lie has been more
or less at war work for some time.
George Vivers, the tall King’s Plains
squatter, has left for the other side to go
into khaki, though there’s quite a good
chance of doing it in his native State. The
big Cornstalk learned medicine, but prefers
wool —for many golden reasons—and never
encourages any allusion to “Dr.” Still his
knowledge may be mighty useful in the firing
line.
The Youngsters’ Welfare Exhibition in
May has a list of patrons as long almost as
the road, to Berlin. The exhibits already
entered include nearly everything—even a
Reformed Sunday School. You can’t think
what a reprobate an unregenerate Sunday
School is.
The Sydney Clerical Women’s Association
wisely cut off its new “Patriotic Club” tail.
The tag led to confusion with the old origi-
nal firm in Bligh-street. Now it brandishes
the impressive name, “Clerical Women War
Workers.” Already out of its modest
monthly members’ subscription of Is. it
has handed £lO 10s. to the Y.M.C.A. for
its work at the Front. It buys wool to knit,
and it finds the wherewithal to help feed
the hungry mattock-swingers at French’s
Forest. Something of a record on a thrum-
mer a week !
Mayoresses of country towns —a formid-
able body when the city calls for help—have
already forwarded their sympathy with the
Edith Cavell memorial fund. Later they will
organise the usual brigandage. The State-
school kiddie is just getting a few marbles
for himself after prodigious self-denial for
the patriotic hat. But there’s a suggestion
that the girls should again indulge in a
famine in hair-bows and chocolate for the
nurses’ home. And ho doubt they will rise
to the occasion.
Most of our war news may be smoked out
by the censors, but through the photographer
and the cartoonist we at least know what the
generals of the world look like. A former
generation had to depend on art when art
was really something else. In the Sydney
Gallery hangs an old print entitled “Re
Heroes of Waterloo.” It shows one side of a
room crammed with chubby, middle-aged
gentlemen in full uniform and various styles
of whisker.' They seem as unconscious o
one another as so many lunatics, and evei.'
single one of them has a Cupid’s-bow moutu.
If that artist were alive and working: to-day
I suppose he would be busy fitting a Cupids
bow to Hindenburg—or Kitchener.
Yours affectionately’,
VANDORIAN.

the underlying cause of most forms *

MELBOURNE CHATTER
Melbourne, April 24, 1916.
My Dear Yarrie, —
The vice-regals have lately been chasing
round like the characters in a comic film.
The moving Stanleys have just slipped over
the State frontier to visit the G.-G. pair in
Sydney. Just before they started they did
a return from Tasmania, and rushed around
opening shows till it made me dizzy to look
at them.
A vice-regal affair up country the other
day was given a touch of unexpected vivacity
by the conduct of the sweet child, seven-year-
old daughter of a local nabob, who had been
decked in pink to pass the usual bouquet to
her Ex. The darling little thing handed up
the dahlias all right; then, after hanging
round for five minutes, made a rush, and
recovered the floral tribute. “Give em
back, go on,” said the innocent. “You ve
had ’em long enough !”
Dr. Jack Gordon is having a ten-iuinute
rest from Egypt. He was Red Grossing
under fire for eight months on Gallipoli, and
was originally in charge of the First General
Hospital. In those days he put up at
Mena House. The hospitals, by the way,
are all being rearranged. So, it seems, are
most other things. It is reckoned that in
six months’ time there won’t be more than
90 Generals in one another’s way in Egypt,
and it is then hoped that, as an average, an
order won’t be countermanded and restored
and countermanded again more than 11
times.
When Colonel Springthorpe made his
recent hurried visit to Melbourne he spent
most of his spare time giving workers advice
as to the best things to send to the soldiers
in Egypt. He strongly recommended great
quantities of apples (the consignees to lure
part of a sailer if cargo space were not
available in a steamer) and any other fruits
that would stand the voyage. But he
crossed oranges off the list. People in
Egypt can get plenty of splendid oranges.
Guy Madden is doing his share for the
Empire, as usual. He has been lining a fair,
round uniform since early in the war;
but not much has been heard of
him lately, with the exception of a
few rumors as to his possible departure
for parts unknown at an early date,
or later. The other night, however,
he again rose to the surface and received
honorable mention in the newspapers. He
had faced several hundreds of soldiers and
come through the ordeal without losing an
ounce of flesh. The soldiers were at a
Y.M.C.A.-hall concert in the Domain camp,
and Captain Guy took the chair wuth great
dash and unbounded intrepidity.
Andrew Millett has given up his honorary
job as secretary to the Australian Literature
Society, which exists to encourage the local
author by reading and reciting his works,
and is not to be confounded with the Litera-
ture Society of Melbourne. That concern
makes a hobby of dead ones like Shakspeare,
and usually ignores the very existence of
Australian writers can spell much
better. Millett’s time is now entirely taken
up with military-camp activities.
A. L. Gunning, who came here from
S’Africa (w’here his dad is director of the
Transvaal Zoological Gardens and the Pre-
toria museum) to join Marshall Hall a
couple of years ago, and is knowm to concert-
goers as a quiet, convincing pianist, has
joined the Army Medical Corps.
One of the Collins-street rest rooms for
soldiers started a post office, where the war-
riors might receive their letters and despatch
replies —after compiling same with the sta-
tionery, pens, ink, typewriter and stamps
provided by staunch patriots. I understand
a great locker full of envelopes empties itself
in a twinkling, and the other supplies melt
like icecream in a stokehold. Mrs. Ewflng
is voluntarily acting as chief postmistress.
Essendon is feeling very sore about the
discovery of its two presentation motor-
ambulances standing idle and neglected in
a warehouse near Victoria Docks, London.
They had been there for six months or
more. Essendon will now take up another
collection and send England an alarm clock.
The Sliakspearians have decided on _ a
miniature Anne Hathaway cottage—lattice
w T indow T s and all —as their Anzac stand in
the city on Friday, 28th. They want holly
berries and autumn foliage to sell, and are
invoking the aid of the Macedonians and
those that do dwell at Creswick, Daylesford
and Ballarat.
Sister Sissie Stephens, daughter of the
Age’s long-established sub-editor, went off
in one of the latest hospital ships bound for
ports unknown. She is one of the nurses
sent to relieve the Red Capes who are due
for a rest.
The Carse widow, straight from Egypt
and London, w 7 here son Frank has been
invalided, is off to the Riverina to put in
the fireside months on a Falkiner brother’s
sheep station. She will take the baby grand-
daughter, the daughter-in-law having gone
to nurse the soldier-son.
Dunlop, the tennis-player, is back in
Melbourne after doing patriotic patrol work
in London for many months. A brief eight
weeks before the war he played with the
burglarious Crown Prince and took food at
the same table with Little Willie Sikes and
four French and Dutch court sportsmen,
none of whom guessed what bad company
they were in. Before the guns began Dun-
lop' and his wife travelled through France.
Spain, Switzerland and Germany, and had
the time of their lives, the tennis champion
being felted so frequently that they appeared
to glide along a river of champagne. Every
time they went out at night somebody’s
opera-box yawned to receive them, and
they were entertained so lavishly that they
scarcely ever had a hotel bill to pay. Then
war came and it was all over like a beautiful
dream filled with food.
Adelaide rings up again :
Unley—which I hope you remember is technically
a city and on the same level as London and New
York —selected Saturday as Gallipoli Day, and added
£6OO to the Red Cross and the Trench Comforts Funds.
The Galways divided their time between that and
the Inter-College Sports. There were also races at
Victoria Park, but we girls don’t go to races now.
Mrs. Carew Reynell, whose husband was killed at
Gallipoli, returned by the Kashgar from Egypt,
where she spent about four months. Before she left
she helped to establish a reading-room and canteen
at the 3rd Light Horse Brigade Camp at Heliopolis.
The Wattle Day League and the State Government
are planning an Apple Day for May 5, in the hope
of collecting some of the surplus crop and putting
it on transports for the men on their w’ay to the
Front. That’s a good idea. The Government will
provide cases and rail carriage for donations of
fruit. As most of the Hills gardens are carpeted with
windfalls, the armies should be munching hard
presently.
Lieut. F. Cutlack, who was beginning to make his
name as a writer when he got a commission in the
British artillery at the beginning of the war, is in
England, convalescing after 12 months at the Front
with his battery. He has to thank Bill’s poison-gas
for a first attack of bronchitis.
Charles Reade —the one who didn’t write “It’s
Never Too Late to Mend’’ —is busy polishing up
the Government’s Town Planning Bill. Apparently,
we are to profit by the mistakes of the older States
and make due provision for expansion so that we
won’t find ourselves suddenly compelled to pay
heavily for land and buildings required for railway
accommodation and so forth. It sounds lovely, and
it’s a pity we didn’t think of it before.
The Government is showing further signs of tackling
one difficulty, and Premier Vaughan has appealed
to the public not to burn its old papers, but keep
them for re-pulping in the mill we’re going to get
by-and-bye.
Frederic Villiers has just told us in a couple of
war lectures what he thinks of the military pros-
pects. But I won’t divulge what he said. He was
lunched by the Commonwealth Club, and had com-
plimentary speeches poured all over him.
Major E. N. Johnston has left for the U.S.A., after
putting the Government on the right track in regard
to Murray River matters. He has completed the
whole of his job, except for the report on the
opening of the port at the mouth. This will be sent
from America and read with keen delight by all
good novel eaters. I love reports on rivers.
Woman is coming to her own —or her husband’s.
The appointment of a third woman “bobby” has been
decided upon, and the Premier babbles of more in
the near future. Of course you mustn’t call them
Roberts. They are, officially, patrols.
The Lady Galway Belgium Book—2ooo copies of
which are to help the Belgian Fund at 7s. 6d. each —
is soon to be on the counters.
Died at North Adelaide, on Wednesday morning,
Mrs. Peterswald, widow of one of our best-known
Commissioners of the Police. She came to the State
with her husband in ’52.
The H. P. Gills were amongst the passengers by
the Mooltan, booked for England, which is to be
their home in the future.
Chief Justice Way’s death leaves all sorts of
prominent positions vacant. One of them has been
filled by Eustace Grundy, K.C., who becomes Grand'
Master of Freemasons after being D.G.M. and P.G.M.
and as much more as you like during Way’s 32-year
tenure of the chair. Piper, another K.C., is Deputy-
Grundy.
There is one happy town in S. Australia, at any
rate. Cockburn, on the railway between Broken Hill
and Adelaide, straddles across the line between the
two States, and has one pub. in each stirrup. The
migration between the two States has increased re-
markably since the S. Aus. stirrup went dry every
evening at 6 p.m.
Erskine Parker, of Launceston, who
handicaps for the Tasmanian Turf Club,
will some day, barring accidents, have a
lord as a son-in-law. His daughter Leonora
Mary has said “I will” in London to the
Hon. Francis Ormond Eaton, eldest son of
Lord Cheylesmore. The bridegroom is
a lieutenant in the Grenadier Guards, and
cut the honeymoon very short to get back
to the firing-line.
Major Dare —the youngster who rushed
up from a lieutenancy like a skyrocket—
lately cabled Rita Moss that he could find
time to marry her if she would pack up and
meet him half-way. The b lfr sister laJt

Wednesday. Mamma was one of Bijou
Wilson’s amazon Misses before she
and went to live at Brighton Beach

Brewer McCracken, who departed this
life last year, left a few barrels of cash
behind for his wife and family. I beliete

The widow of the late politician William
Sliiels has single-handed brought her family
safely through the Ilun’s submarines, after
wandering about Europe for nearly tluee
years. #
A statue of the late James Cuming has
just been planted in the allegedly chemically-
pure but sometimes over-perfumed Melbourne
suburb of Footscray. Margaret Baskerviile
did the sculping. Apart from the debatable
propriety of erecting a statue in memory ot
a good citizen who would probably have pre-
ferred to see the cash go to the Children’s
Hospital, the only visible blot on the busi-
ness is that Italian marble was used instead
of Australian. Better and more durable
marble than ever Carrara produced is avail-
able in Gippsland.
The Stanleys visited Edward Officer’s
patriotic picture show just before they
whirled away to Sydney. Before they left
a red spot came out on a canvas full ot
Darling River gpms.
The Arts and Crafters sat at the boots of
the Stanleys one day last week, and all
went well enough until Professor Spencer
hurled a shower of barbed criticisms into
the happy circle. The most terrible moment
was when he denounced the kookaburra, emu,
kangaroo and wattle flower as being unsuited
for decorative art. and recommended a study
of the Black Australian’s shield patterns
and so forth. Then his Ex. followed on and
shocked all the girls by saying he was in-
clined to agree with Spencer. But he had
a good word to say for the wattle when, as
a tower of blossom, it leaned lovingly over
the Yarra at Warrandyte. He suggested
that some effort be made to get a loan
exhibit of furniture, jewellery and other
crafts from South Kensington Museum. All
this was served up with some jam about the
capacity of the Australian to do excellent
work; but the gathering broke up looking
very thoughtful. You see it was hardly
what the workers expected.
Amy Castles, soon off to the U.S.A. if
Wilson doesn’t reach for his gun, gave a con-
cert in the Town Hall on Saturday night
and got a fine house, considering the rush
of Easter shows. Summed up it was a
packed floor and a thin balcony. Amy sang
from inside an amber-colored gown—a dream
of a thing netted over with ribbon-threaded
trellis and decorated with some cunning
touches of cobalt, blue and black. Encores
were greedily demanded, and she seemed
tired when she got to the last item on the
bill —a glorious thing from “Louise.” Of
course the music-drinkers absolutely refused
to go until she returned and shouted
another round. Soldier-brother George
was not available, and Hector Gold-
spink warbled in his stead. Amadio
communed a bit with liis flute, and
Laurie Kennedy made his ’cello sing. Wil-
liam Burrell was at the piano. As the
concert was not clearly labelled “Farewell”
we may hear Amy again before she goes.
Rocco di Gilio, the Italian who came to
Melbourne a couple of decades ago with
little more than a flute, a toothbrush and an
extra pair of boots, has found Australia kind.
Part of his cash success is due to the fact
that, in following his profession of string-
band provider, he has always been prepared
to sandwich an engagement at the Market
Gardeners’ Bay Picnic between' a Govern-
ment House programme and a little music
for a Gaud Mayoral soiree. The di Gilio
night-travelling outfit, in which his weary
bandsmen find their way back to barracks
when the dancing’s done, is a marvel of
antiquity. It is_a cut between an 1887 vic-
toria and an 1877 omnibus. At suburban
dances, when the concern is propped up
against the landscape for the evening, it is
occupied by a succession of amorous couples,
while within the festive hall Rocco is point-
ing the way to a perfectly heavenly time with
his trills and tootles. In the breath-taking
intervals of his flutings, Rocco bestows a
long series of white-toothed smiles upon the
fairest of the flock whirling by. They seem
to like it, too. Di Gilio, by the way, has a
nephew doing “real” music at the University
Conservatorium—August di Gilio, a very
bright and promising student under fiddler
Carmen Delprat.
Stanley Adams, whose fiddle is nowadays
often heard in good company, commenced
with the banjo. His father was known,
more than 20 years ago, as Melbourne’s one
and only topnoteh twanger. He ran a banjo-
works in Queen’s Walk and taught Toorak
all it knew of the instrument. Young Adams
followed in father’s footsteps, and presently
went ahead on a new track. ll e used to
appear on a variety of platforms, but
nowhere attracted such notice as when he
appeared between acts during a theatrical
season and produced a remarkable burst of
melody from his limited vehicle. p ress
critics took him seriously as a musician and
urged him to study an instrument more
worthy of his talent. So he began fiddling
at the Marshall Hall Conservatorium, and
after a few years became that concern’s most
press-noticed pupil. Afterwards he took to
playing for a crust in theatrical orchestras
a calling in which from time to time a good
deal of sound Australian talent is buried
alive, and so disappeared awhile from public
notice. But lie had the goods.
The father of Carrie Haase tried putting
up another name on his baker’s carts, but it
was disallowed and the original surname
had to be painted on again. The man of
dough must think it hard luck when daughter
Carrie can change her stage name to Hallett
and go unchallenged. Are recitations less
important than bread?
Carrie Moore, whose principal-boyishness
is a main support of the Dick Whittington
pantomime, at the Princess’s, will shortly
enter a private hospital to he operated oil
for an affection of the throat. But there is
nothing to indicate that Carrie is down-
hearted.
Mooroopna, a quiet little town among the
orchards of the Goulburn Valley, is in the
throes of an industrial struggle. The local
fruitpickers went on strike for higher wages,
and, stirred to emulation, the schoolboys
formed a union to secure shorter hours and
the abolition of ’’keeping in.” The entrance
fee was four marbles, and any youngster who
had been “skun,” and was therefore unable
to buy in. was in the cold. On the principle
of preference to unionists, no boy who
was a member of the union would
play alleys with a non-member. The
unionists even elected officers, includ-
ing an organiser and a “rep.” They
then asked J. McPherson, an A.W.U. organ-
iser who was in the district in connection
with the fruitpickers’ strike, to put their case
before the teacher. He wisely refused, and
the nippers are fighting it out among them-
selves, to the accompaniment of black eyes
and bruised faces on both sides.
Frank Tudor, Minister of Customs, com-
plimented the Austral Clock Co. on the birth
of a new Australian industry at the co.’s
special exhibition of clock-frames made from
beautiful Australian hardwood. Blackwood
from Warburton, oak from Tasmania and
Queensland, and mountain ash from X. S.
Wales and Victoria were a few of the timbers
used. The prices, so the yarn went, are about
30 per cent, less than those of the imported
goods which tumble in here by the thousand
from the U.S.A. now that the German is
barred. A fine specimen clock with an Aus-
tral frame was presented to the Empire Rest
Rooms for soldiers, and the Minister ap-
plauded the finish of the local work as “ex-
cellent.” But he didn’t promise an excellent
increase of duty on the wooden clock cases
from oversea. One trouble, I expect, is that
the stupid people who run such things
always, as in this case, skite about being
able to make the article 30 per cent, or some-
thing cheaper than the foreigner is selling
his stuff for. It is mostly an absurd tarra-
diddle. but it gives the politician an excuse
for doing nothing. Why should he do any-
thing when the market can be so easily con-
trolled without a duty? Then the importer,
who has been making a sinful profit, winks
liis eve, suddenly drops his prices, lives on
his fat for a while, and there is another
infant Australian industry being buried in a
lonely liftle grave.
War-correspondent Frederic V illiers
stormed Melbourne Athenaeum on Saturday
night like an artist and a showman.
Sprucely groomed from whiskers to shoe-
tip, he bowed gracefully, in front or a pic-
ture screen flanked by crimson curtains, an
handy to a reading-desk shrouded in blacK
and iit with a concealed electric light. Lae
lecturer was armed with a stick of tne
ordinary walking length, but decorated wi
a tassel; and a steel extension sprang tr
the innards of this cudgel when maps
appeared on the screen. As usual a num
of the slides arrived upside down; 1 nev
go to a show of this sort without teen g
how good God is to lanternists to keep tie
right away from the lecturers. Besides maps
and slides, Villiers also has moving pictures
and his own sketches. And above all he <s
his Manner, which, I am sure, came do
from Heaven.
Yours sincerely,
JOHANNA.

ABORIGIN-
ALITIES
‘‘l2 x 12” : Concerning “Mulga’s” bullock-
tying act (B. 30/3/’I6), I claim the following to be
a place-getter in the competition. When J. S. Drew
managed Mt. Mitchell (N.S.W.) for Amos and
Bitchie he had some smart blackbovs. Notable among
these were “Oban Tommy” and “Paddy Malone.”
ild cattle were then, in the ’9o’s, very numerous in
New England, especially on the rough country about
the head waters of the Orara, Mitchell, Oban, Aber-
fovle and Guy Fawkes. One day Jack Drew and the
blacks started a mob of wilds. Each threw a beast
and each boy tied his according to fancy.
“Malone’s” capture was a cock-horned Hereford-type
bullock. Paddy had tied him by placing his off fore-
leg behind the off horn. We had Old Nick’s own job
to let him loose.
* * * *
“Quidam” : The old residents of the Blue
Mountains (N.S.W.) are for the most part a primi-
tive, unenterprising crowd, relics of the spacious
days when fences were few and any settler on a
little bit of land had the run of many square miles
round it for his stock. They represent the period
when brush fences and bark roofs were the order of
the day, and in many cases they have not pro-
gressed beyond it yet; wherefore their general
poverty and inevitable future extinction. But they
possess one charming characteristic, and that is their
hospitality. No matter how
poor and no matter how rough
the conditions under which
they live, the casual visitor is
always assured of a share of
whatever is going. Whatever
time he drops in, whether a
former acquaintance or not,
preparations for a
“cup of tea” at
once begin, the
“cup of tea” being
a substantial meal.
Generally there is
no tempting his
politeness to a re-
fusal by asking
would he like any
refreshment; the
setting out of the
repast starts auto-
matically with his
entrance. The floor
may be of earth,
and the seating
accommodation the
ancient hardwood
stools that grandfather made out of slabs
many decades ago, but the hospitality is
there as a matter of course. For genuine,
spontaneous hospitality that takes no
heed of social stutus, or whether a feed in
time will return nine, they can give
several points and a bad beating to many
richer and higher-toned communities.
There is no better place to be lost in,
caught in a snowstorm, left by your
horses or otherwise in dire straits than
these Blue Mountains, if only you can
accidentally happen upon the home of one of the old
settlers. It is unfortunately necessary to add that
the characteristic is not epidemic among the new
settlers from afar. Some of them are less addicted
to dispensing hospitality than to dispensing with it.
“Mulga” : The white ant of the Northern
Territory is probably the largest of its kind in the
Commonwealth, being from lin. to ljin. in
length. This termite plays havoc with the
mango cocoanut and pawpaw plants. It enters a tree
below the ground, and makes its way through the
roots into the trunk. Should the insects by any
chance get too near the outside of the trunk and
cause it to open, the crack is closed with a composi-
tion of vegetable matter and earth to prevent maraud-
ers entering. Having dined sufficiently on one tree
the gluttons make their way through an underground
tunnel to their next banquet.
“A.S.H.”: Here’s my experience. Had
some half-grown pigs running in a paddock over the
Tarwin river in Victoria. I brought them over
to the styes, but they got out and made for the old
haunts across the river. Some swam straight across,
but a couple went more downstream, and their
throats were cut or torn with the forefeet, and bled
profusely. The others were only slightly torn. I
know for certain of one pig which cut its throat so
badly while swimming that it bled to death.
* * * #
“Te Pana” : I renewed acquaintance with
Apia (Samoa) recently. If the old, gin-soaking
beachcomber could do the same the juniper-berry
juice would be as apid in his mouth. Times on
the “beach” have changed. The smell of benzine
from motor-cars kills the perfume of the frangi-
panni, the drink dispensaries are as modern as
the pub at the corner of George-street, and Miss
Losa Samoa swaggers on the promenade all-te-same
white sister. The Maoriland garrison controls the
town, and the boys are a tough-looking crowd.
They are a bit grubby, and the bulk of their razors
have rusted. Mosquito bites, dust, perspiration and
thirst are at the old address, but the notes of a
bugle supersede the boom of the “lali,” and khaki
dominates the color scheme. One of the few relics
of the past is the boneheap of the German gunboat
Adler, cast ashore in a breeze at the time Westport
(M.L.) coal enabled the Calliope to go out and give
the fuel a world-wide advertisement.
“Biddy B.”: Here’s a strange death for a
horse. We had a paddock of 1200 acres, and wanted
a dam scooped. A trial shaft 6x2x12 had been
sunk to see if the ground would hold. There were
12 horses running in the paddock, amongst them a
valuable saddle nag. At night he spotted the fresh
earth thrown up out of the shaft, went to roll on
it, and slipped into the hole back first. As his head
had to double up so that he could comfortably fit in,
his neck was broken. It was a borrowed horse, so, of
course, he didn’t know that we wanted the shaft.
“Cleveland” : Talk about sheep-tallying and
bag-sewing reminds me of the time I was loading
wheat at Portland, Oregon. The wharves are built
high above the shipping to allow of the grain bags
finding their way into the ship’s hold by gravitation.
I was told off to tally, and although there were
three shoots working most of the time, every truck-
load of three bags was weighed and checked by one
man. This sounds easy; but listen. The ark in
question staggered away from the shoots with 52,000
bags stowed in her innards, all of which were in-
sinuated into her in 27£ working hours. Also, this
was bagged as it was shipped, and the sewing was
done by four men, which means near enough to 473
bags per hour per man for me! I tried to see how
it was done, and got a cast in my eye in the
process.

“P.P.M.” : The man who was. given a rally
in the Abo. pages recently for sewing 225 sacks a
day was only a beginner at the pastime. If he were
sewing in some of the factories at Footscray or
Yarraville and did not sew and ticket 225 sacks in
under tour hours he would be fired as incompetent.
What is considered a good day’s work is an average
of 80 sacks an hour; if lewt. bags, 100 an hour.
Conditions, two lugs, six stitches and a tag between.
lam a sewer at Cockbell’s. Am good, but not fast. My
best is 655 sacks. On another occasion the boss asked
me to sew a load for a waiting lorry , while the men
w’ere having their dinner. I sewed 72 sacks and had
half of them loaded in 45 minutes. Another day for
a wager I sewed 13 sacks and was on the lug of the
fourteenth in'five minutes. And there are sewers
about here that could lose me. To put up a record
I reckon a fast man in good nick should average two
sacks a minute throughout the day.
“Barb”: “McNorth’s” (B. 23/3/T6)
directions for the “bushman ’ whm mislays himself
are reliable sometimes, and in level forest country.
But in hilly country and scrub the timber grows and
leans all way’s ; the wind blows ditto. If a man has
not taken notice of the lie of the country or of
conspicuous trees during his march, and requires the
points of the compass to guide him back, let him
start a good fire, camp and wait for the sun.
If he won’t or can’t do that, let him “blaze” eare-
fuHy as he cruises round to pick up his tracks, so
thgt he may return to the spot where he left his
smoky fire if unsuccessful. Then he may at least have
a chance of being discovered by a search party.
Some men have an instinctive sense of direction, but
they are few, and deservedly celebrated where they
are known. A man I knew could find his way’ any-
where. You couldn’t “bush” him. I asked him
once: “How does Tintown lie from here?” “Oh,” he
replied, “about sunset.” Then he hesitated. “No,”
he said, “a little bit east of that!”
“Snell”: I went through Port Melbourne
with a friend one afternoon recently to call on a
transport. In a small street a large woman darted
out and seized a grubby small boy playing with
several other grubby small boys in the gutter. She
spanked him with Amazonian vigor, admonishing
him at the same time with a flow that would have
made a railway-ganger blush. “For pity’s sake,
don't knock the boy’s head quite off! ” expostulated
my friend. “Y’mind y’r own business,” said the
lady. “I won’t have ’im in the street pickin’
up bad langwidge!”
Bulloo : I* or the best all-round timber
in the bush my nomination is beefwood. Out here in
the sou’-west corner of Queensland it is used for most
things, from firewood to furniture. Certainlv burn-
ing is not its best point, unless the mosquitoes are
busy, but for yards—especially stub yards—fence
posts, window frames, sashes, doors, tables and forms
it takes a lot of beating. Its appearance when dressed
is not its least attraction.

“Caliacl” : It was in the Cloudy Bay dis-
trict, Papua, tha't this drama was staged. A party
of murderers was wanted, and as the police were
travelling through the scrub they heard a noise as of
a pig moving. A native Hop went to investigate,
and suddenly came upon one of the sons of Cain
facing him. Both rifles went up and the men stood
looking along the barrels at each other in perfect
silence until the brown murderer’s nerve failed him
and he dropped his weapon and ran. The policeman’s
gun teas empty.

“Mrs. Lance Rawson”: Apropos flying
foxes as a table dainty, I have often used them, and
during the fruit and honey season they are not bad,
but the strong smell of the wings disgusts most people
who would otherwise try the flesh. My plan was to
throw the fox on to a hot fire and burn off the wings,
then skin the body or scrape it clean, cut into pieces
and soak in vinegar and water or salt and water
for a few hours. Then, if pumpkins are “in,” cut
a slice off the stalk end of a good-sized one, scoop
out the seeds, etc., and fill in with fox, an onion
chopped up, salt, pepper, a little rice or macaroni,
and about a cupful of water. Put the pumpkin’s lid
on and cook —in the ashes for preference. If the foxes
are young and fat you’d hardly know them from
sucking-pig. Most bushmen exclaim at the idea of
eating iguana, yet there are few bush dainties equal
to the tail-end of a young “go” curried. As a
matter of fact, very few bush-dwellers are aware of
the many edible creatures there are around them.
One has to be meat-hungry to find them out, and
then learn how to cook them. I once dined a
Queensland Judge and his suite on iguana curry.
It not only received his Honor's approval, but he
asked for more next day. Certainly, I did not give
it a name other than “bush game.”

FAITHLESSNESS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Most people knew that Paddy Burke was mashed
Upon the buxom barmaid at The Mast.
There, ev’ry week, his fresh-got notes he cashed ;
And, when she left, his sky was overcast;
Now anyone who likes to drink a beer
His tale of woman’s faithlessness may hear.
“She’s gone away, and never left for me
No bloomin’ word! Me love ’as gone for nix!
I useter spend each ev’nin’ after tea
Till closing-time, an’ come erlong at six
Each morning, just to see ’er in the bar.
An’ yet she’s gone. That shows what women are!
“Devotion—that was me! When I would speak
She’d turn ’er nose up at me, honor bright!
And yet I’d spend me wages, week by week,
On booze to chat ’er! An’, ’ere, ev’ry night,
Full as an egg I got, just fer ’er sake.
And now she’s vanished. Women take the cake!”
N.S.W.

‘Tick”: “Flinderino” (B. 23/3/TG) asks
if snakes possess the poxx'er of fascination. They do
xx-hen a small bird or animal is their victim. I
twice saw snake mesmerism exerted, once by a huge
tiger upon a fantail, and once by a black five-footer
on a half-grown rabbit. The bird, with fluttering,
drooped wings and every aspect of extreme terror,
and moving as though drawn in jerks by a string,
approached the scaley horror, which lay motionless
with head slightly raised. The rabbit, though near
a burrow, made no effort to escape, but squealed
incessantly and darted from side to side, its move-
ments apparently concerting xvith a gentle sideways
motion of the snake’s head. In each ease, as I
xvaded into the argument, the spell broke, and the
entranced hastily made room for the subsequent
proceedings. “Flinderino” asserts that most animals
treat snakes with indifference. That’s not my
experience. I have seen sheep, xvhen packed in a
lane, scramble over each other to avoid a craxvler in
their midst, and I have also seen a tied-up horse
tremble and sweat when a snake crawled near. One
animal, however, I observed that had an apparent
liking for snake. He was a bullock that stood calmly
chewing a three-footer xxhile it xvrithed and con-
torted under the discomforting process.

country is all open and very scrubby they took some
gathering. Old drovers have told me since that they
have met xvith the same experience. Whaffor?

“Nymboi”: “Bad Australian” is right (B.
23/3/’l6). Freshwater cod and eels do reside to-
gether, and I have often caught both on the Nym-
boida and Orara rix’ers, N.S.W. North Coast. About
15 years ago I xvas introduced to a tremendous cod
xvhich the binghis had just captured ; it turned the
scales at 661 b., and had a head like a miniature
xvhale’s. Both the abox'e-noted streams are, unfor-
tunately, becoming gradually short of cod. I have
known Ihe time xx'hen one could journey up the Nym-
boida and almost invariably succeed in landing at
least 20 beautiful fish within a fexv hours. White

tucker.

“W.C.M.”: A certain cure for wounds in
horses is Stockholm-tar. A nag of mine galloped
into a barb-wire fence. When I disentangled him
I xvent for the gun, but was stopped by the local
blacksmith, xvho plastered the cuts xvith tar. They
xvere pretty bad cuts, the upper part of each foreleg
being opened to the hone, and there were -tears on
the chest and hind legs. The horse was turned out
on the grass and tarred each day for a week. Six
weeks later I rode the neddy 30 miles, and after-
wards sold him at a profit. The only marks left
by the xvire were slight scars on the legs. I’ve
tried tar several times since for horses and dogs,
and, as everyone knows, its use is universal in the
rhea ling-shed, and always with the same result.

“Moann ”: Years back opossums were
shipped across the Tasman to Maoriland and at once
started to thrive. They were rigidly protected, and
recently there xx’as a lot of talk about the future of
Loan Land’s fur industry owing to a discovery that
the colder climate of the pawned islands has had the
. effect of increasing the length and- weight of
’possum’s overcoat. Almost simultaneously xvith this
glad news a howl went up from the fruit-farmers of
the West Coast and Nelson because thousands of the
animals were raiding gardens and orchards. These
people craved immediate permission to murder ihe
’possums, and a worried official in Wellington is
trying to decide whether to preserve the fur industry
or protect the fruitgrowers.

“Billy W.” : “Hydros” (B. 6/4/’3G) wants
to know how Corriedales are bred. Ensor has 20,000
of them roaming over his White Rock estate, out of
Itangiora (M.L.), and he started in the ’Bo’s with a
Lincoln-Merino cross. The wool was pretty poor,
though, and he scrapped the Lincolns for English
Leicesters. Wholesale culling and the careful mating
of the progeny have enabled him to build up a very
useful, even type.

“Cict”: A most uncomfortable aspect is
shown to the world at large by the common Jexv-
lizard who has been caught napping by a horde of
meat ants. I strayed the other day against one that
had been thus attacked. The defendant disported
himself freely in a reckless and wholly unusual kind
of way. He threw himself up recklessly against the
atmosphere. Likewise he fervently scraped himself
on all handy materials, but to no purpose. Then at
last he played his trump-card, and won. Bolting for
a mass of loose sand he delved his way thereunder,
and the xxdngless tormentors, deprived of their game,
sat in an aimless manner on the several grains of
earth therearound, with wonderment inscribed on
their different .countenances. Shortly after, they
left the spot for fresh pastures, and after about a
quarter of an hour the fugitive emerged, inspected
his wrongs and damages, dried iris eyes on a conveni-
ent clod, and ambled homewards just as the supper-
hell rang.

les= the mong. decided to board at the stables. For

“K.Q.”: Let me into this argument re
pigs cutting their throats when swimming. The
last fresh in the Hunter River brought doxxrn and
precipitated on Stockton (N.S.W.) beach the remains
of an ancient hog with distinct scratches on each

“E.S.S.”: There is nothing so pugnacious,
so ready and bold in defence, as a jumper. The bull-
ant is more retiring, but interfere with him and he
will attack with ferocity. The jumpers do not wait
for a declaration of hostilities. They will follow an
intruder several yards from the nest, leaping oxer
grass and sticks in their eagerness to overtake him.
The bull-ant is fierce and determined in attack, but
he does not sacrifice himself in a mad frenzy like the
other. “The day when the bull-ant stung father”
is a date that is long fixed in the mind of the bush
youngster. The jumper’s sting, thougli causing
intense pain and raising a white lump, does not
impart quite the same tingling, burning, paralysing
sensation as that of the bigger ant.
Tick” : The Abo. who denies that pigs
cut their throats when swimming is only half-mast
high in veracity. In fresh water a pig generally
swims so low aft that he catches his jaws and throat
with his forepaws until badly scored and bleeding.
In salt water the increased buoyancy assists to a
better swim. At Bermagui (N.S.W,) I once saw a
log pig go overboard. It cut its throat badly before
landing, though it put up a swim of only 200 yards.
But when the s.s. Bega dived with us off Cuttagee
beach, and 100 pigs were left without lifebelts, one
was sighted from the deck of the Coomonderry, near
Montague Island, 16 hours later. It had traversed 18
miles dead against the current and was still battling
strongly in a heavy sea.

“Boro-Belle”: At Mackay a fine sample
of a crocogator’s jaw, about 2ft. in length, with
perfect set of white, glistening teeth, adorns a fane'
smith’s window. The owner on one occasion refused
£4O for it. That was before the war. At Halifax
on the Herbert River, as many as eight stuffed
’gators occupy the space along' the walls usually
taken up by framed whisky advts. The largest
specimen is 25ft. and the smallest a couple of feet
Some fine samples of the Proserpine River species
are to be seen adorning the walls of the Adelaide
Steamship Co.’s offices at Mackay. Crocodile shoot-
ing was a popular sport some years ago, when the
Proserpine sugar used to be brought to Mackay for
transhipment south, before the railway line to Bowen
was completed. The hides were tanned with man-
grove bark on the spot, and though some are j2* or
15 .>’ ears old,’they are in a splendid state of preser-
vation. At one of the pubs a stuffed croc., about 4ft.
long, was hoisted near the ceiling, in a glass case
It was only there a week when a cool-eyed drunk
threw a decanter and brought the sleeping lizard on
to the bar counter, where it now serves as a match-
striker.

‘Tick”: The evolutions of bird flocks,
about which “Nivel” seeks' information (B.
23/3/’] 6), are all a matter of “follow my leader."
Sheep and rushing cattle wheel and ring in the
same way. So also do soldier crabs and schools of
fish. If “Nivel” has observed how closely masses of
flying birds will miss telegraph wires or a wire-
netting fence into which the leaders have almost
dashed before rising, he may judge of the sudden-
ness with which a bird can adapt its flight to a
change of direction.
* * * # ,
“S. S. Severn”: A relation of Neptune’s
elephant came up from the deep seas lately on -an
island in Papuan waters. I didn’t see it, but a man
who doesn’t pour into himself more than the usual
amount said it was about 30ft. long and covered
with long, curly wool like a sheep. The marine
jumbuck was dead when washed up on the beach.
The adjacent niggers saw in the beast the fulfilment
of traditional ill-luck. Somebody should take out an
injunction against tradition to prevent its coming
home to roost.

“Musketoon”: The fox now takes mint-
sauce with his iamb. Arthur Cox, orehardist of Mus-
wellbrook (N.S.W.), tells me that one night recently
he baited some peaches with cyanide, and next
morning found a dead fox. Then he used strychnine,
worked up on the peaches, and his haul was four
foxes. When did Rufus turn vegetarian?
A MASSIVE RESISTER.
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
A youth of 19, weighing lOst. 81b., with a chest-
measurement of 48in., presented himself for enlist-
ment, but was rejected on the ground of being al>nor-
mally fat.
The doctor slowly shook his head ;
The youth stood ponderous and passive.
“No; you won't do, my lad,” he said,
For nineteen you are much too massive.”
Bui, said the youth, “you passed a man
As big as I am, if not bigger.”
“Ah, yes,” the medico began,
“I must admit he’d ample figure.
“He landed at Gallipoli,
And, though a most persistent trier,
He could not fit the trench, you see—-
And then the fat was in the fire.”

J.A.V.S.

nOST Motorists know that the strength of a
cover is dependent solely on the strength and
quality of the canvas used in building up the
inside casing of a tyre, but few realise what a
difference there is in the many grades of canvas used
by tyre makers—a difference not only of quality but in
price. You know that if a casing fails, the tyre fails.
Now, to reduce this possibility to a minimum, we use
in the manufacture of Dunlop Covers from 5 to 7 plies
of the most expensive and strongest sea island cotton
canvas made. An inch strip of one ply only of this
canvas has a breaking strain of 335 lbs., so you can
imagine what the strength of a 5 to 7 ply Dunlop casing
amounts to. The result is a resilient and remarkably
strong casing, capable of withstanding the jolts and
jars incidental to driving thousands of miles on our
roads. We could make a cheaper casing, and save
considerable money thereby, but our name and
reputation goes wit a every Dunlop Cover. We offer
you the advantage that such high quality ensures.
This is one reason why you should always specify
“Dunlops”—and there are others!

THE RIGHT CONSTRUCTION.
OOD MATERIALS are useless unless the right mode of construction is
followed. Like everything else, there is a cheap way and a proper
way of making Motor Covers. The Dunlop way is not a cheap process,
but is one that aims at giving motorists good results. In the first place, the
casings are accurately built up by a remarkable piece of machinery that ensures
every ply of canvas being properly stretched on the core, and rolled down
smoothly, leaving no possible chance for a loose ply or a wrinkle. 1 his is a very
important factor in tyre strength. The method in vogue in most factories is to
stretch canvas by hand, resulting in unevenly built up casings that will soonei or
later show a weakness. We eliminate the human element and produce tyres that
are absolutely uniform. The next stage is the fitting of-a covei ing over the casing,
and semi-vulcanising same in moulds under heavy pressure. The second important
“Dunlop” feature is the wearing tread, this is built up of 8 to 10 strips ot high-
grade Rubber compound, which are shaped into one homogeneous mass by heavy
pressure and then semi-vulcanised. The tread is then wrapped on to the casing
and again vulcanised. This process means more labour and more expense, but it
ensures your getting a cover that is correctly vulcanised m all parts, and one that
will give the maximum of wear. Therefore —Sir—in the Dunlop you get the right

“HEARNE’S”
Died in London, Mrs. Henry Pettitt, pro-
fessionally known as Annette Read, and for
long years before the English public. Among
other notable actors she had played with
were G. V. Brooke, J. L. Toole and Smith,
the colored tragedian. Henry, her husband,
ran a prolific melodrama mill 25 years ago
and ground out ‘’The World,” “Woman’s
Revenge,” “Taken from Life,” “Sailor’s
Knot,” “A Million of Money” and a barrel
of others.
“Peter D.”: Have seen no mention of a
singularly beautiful performance given by
George Titheradge. It was in “Grierson’s
Way,” by H. V. Esmond, produced in ’97 at
the Haymarket Theatre, London, for a series
of matinees. It never attained the dignity of
an evening “bill,” though Heaven alone
knows why. Titheradge played Grierson,
supported by Lena Ashwell (very fine),
Fred Terry as the villain (an abominably-
stagey performance), and the author as a
lialf-demented player of the violin, a cripple
(painfully realistic). It was a strong,
thoughtful play, with Titheradge at his very
best.
“Adelaide”: Hazel Morris’s bad and dan-
gerous habit of guying members of the
audience started a newspaper correspondence
in Adelaide. And it was not press-agent
work either, for it only began when she was
gone. There are two kinds of men in every
audience: those who rather enjoy having
the limelight turned on them, and those
who loathe it. The Morris woman’s Yankee
trick of coming right down and kissing a
victim on his bald spot, or tangoing along
the aisle with another, more or less unwill-
ing, drives the sensitive ones frantic; and
some day an exasperated man will certainly
ask a Court to give him about a thousand
pounds to settle his feelings.
“0.K.” remembers many circuses:—
The passing crazes, varying fashions and stable
customs in acrobatics and gymnastics are interesting
to the student of theatrical business. The crazes in
all cases present dangerousness in an ugly form; the
established customs are essentially graceful. Flying
trapeze work, invented more than half a century ago
by a Frenchman named Lestard, was an attraction
for all time, of course. A thing of beauty is a joy
for ever. The well-calculated system of swinging-
trapezes was practised and developed until it lost its
“thrill'’ for the public; but the beauty of the aerial
spring from bar to bar —the vault or somersault—
could never fade. It remains a permanent feature in
gymnastics. Similar work performed with fixed
horizontal-bars has its recurring vogues but is l® s
adaptable to added feats of gymnastics —the novelties
that give commercial value to a turn. For some
few years there was an epidemic of “rope sliding
by women acrobats, who came down slowly, hea
foremost with one leg twined around the rope (or
vice versa), whilst an attendant turned it to set t e
figure gracefully revolving. This went out o
fashion, because the element of danger was lac ing
and there was little poetry of motion in it. e
poetry was in the poses, and the performance " as
“living statuary” rather than gymnastics. Hea to
head balancing, made wonderful by the undernea
man walking up and down a spiral staircase, wi
inverted “brother"’ stretched perpendicularly ao'
him, had as much vogue as the feats of arre
jumping, which were ingeniously dangerous, u e
less pleasing to the eye than head-balancing '®P a
Meanwhile the “human pyramid” form of acio a 1 >
with its jumping and somersaulting from one
shoulders to another’s, went on as usual. 18 .
of work —the Craggs Family introduced it o
tralia —is most graceful and almost charming
expertness. So it never can become °' l ’ o f. 6 ’, re .
the rapid, knockabout, ground-tumbling " 1C
quires crude comedy effects to renew the era
it. In place of three circus clowns we go a•
ago an invariable trio of ground-tumblers K
as grotesque old men, doing unlovely deer s o
in ragged, baggy garments. And how
is the chair-and-table-balancing style .
ment! A man balances himself on the topmo 0 j
of a shaky pyramid of furniture and then
it, falling ungracefully to the stage, or
carefully down without upsetting any turn l
feat requires no picturesque exercise o
agility by the human form divine. It has
in it. It is the craze of the passing hour.
