Business, Robbery, etc.
The warning yell of G. A. Morehouse: —
In May last I agreed to purchase a cottage at
Lawson (N.S.W.). I signed the contract, paid the
usual deposit, and as the seller had the place insured
for £5OO I did not bother to reinsure, thinking, as
thousands of others do, that the existing policy kept
the place covered until the transfer had been signed.
Before the transfer had been completed a bushfire
burnt the place out, and the insurance people refused
our claim, pleading no liability. Now, if the signing
of the contract killed the policy, why did the com-
pany quietly accept the renewal premium in Septem-
ber, four months after the signing of the contract?
The renewal receipt which I have before me plainly
states it is to renew the policy until August, 1916
and it isn’t August yet. If any Bulletin readers
have had a similar experience or are interested I
would like to hear from them, with a view to co-
operation in getting the law amended. As it stands,
buyers are committed to the purchase as soon as the
contract is signed ; but if the house is burnt down
between the signing of the contract and the comple-
tion of the transfer, they have no redress.
“Quidam”: Judging by the progress- of
events in the country districts of N. S.
Wales the prospects of cheap meat in the
near future are not brilliant. Almost every
second man is working, has worked, or is
thinking of working a deal in meat on the
hoof, and in most cases it refers to a cow.
The lady has to be an exceedingly good
milker nowadays to escape being delivered
over to the nearest butcher for execution,
and in most cases directly even a good
milker goes dry she departs to the block, too.
If the same sort of thing is going on all
over Australia —and I suppose it is—the
prophets who predict enormous prosperity
for Australia when the war is over, ex-
pecting that she is going to sell meat at
high prices to half the world, had better
get busy showing how a country which has
slaughtered most of its female cattle is
going to throw even the poor dog a bone.

“Billjim’s Sister”: Inquiring for Austra-
lian-made cashmere stockings recently at a
large Melbourne drapery, I was handed a
pair stamped “Made in England.” On point-
ing this out to the duchess behind the
counter, I was told “We used to stock Aus-
tralian, but we’re sold out.” Evidently they
were too tired to order another supply. How-
ever, I have that pleased feeling of standing
in “Made in Australia” hosiery, as the local
store is a good patriot, although a very small
one—perhaps on that account.
* * * *
The championship at explaining how
water came to be present in milk he was
selling is held by a milkman at Wellington
(M.L.). When bailed up by an inspector
he pointed out that it had been raining that
morning, that water had collected round the
rim of his hat, and that when he bent down
to ladle out milk the other moisture ran off
into the can.
* * * *
“Torrens” : The short but keen drought of
1914-15 is getting in its after effects in the
matter of brandy. Australia uses on an
average 425,000 gallons a year, of which
150,000 are homegrown. Europe has had
several other things to think of since August,
1914, besides grape-growing, yet it wants the
spirit more than ever, for hospital use; and,
as brandy has to mature for at least two
years, the poor last-but-one crop in Australia
is going to make itself felt shortly. So the
wineries have been paying up to to a ton
for distillation grapes in South Aus tins
season —a most unusual price: nearly a
penny per pound.
“Burnley” : I wish somebody in the know
would give me the hang of things as regards
matches. Inquiries at the big Melbourne
factory justify the statement (B. 16/6/
that 20 million good pipe-lights are being
turned out daily, and that Queensland hoop-
pine and maple are being used with satis-
faction. Yet here is my experience. On
March 2 I stepped into a city grocers and
asked for two dozen matches. He handed
me the local articles at 3d. per dozen. On
March 10 I asked the same man for two
dozen more. He charged the same
but would only sell me one dozen, though he
assured me I could have another dozen next
dav if 1 called. (Just here I remembered
Mrs. Burnley’s similar experience with
sugar, which suddenly became plentiful as
soon as the price was raised to 3|d.) On
March 27 1 asked a suburban grocer for
matches. He planked down a packet of
Japanese and demanded 6d. After recovei-
ing from that shock I investigated four
consecutive grocers. All proffered Jap
matches, but at sd, while two had British-
made at ssd. Not one of the lot had a
Melbourne-made match for sale._ Consider-
ing the sudden rise from 3d. to and the
deadly persistence of the Jap matches, I
want to know what’s the game.
“Hawker”: As to enamelware mugs, etc.
(B. 16/3/TG), let us get down to details.
“Cliff Street” says an Adelaide firm makes
them. If so, what does it do with them?
Also, at what price does it sell them? For
months now such mugs have been unobtain-
able at the wholesale houses in Melbourne.
A small lot came along lately (English),
and were being sold wholesale at Bs. for
1-pts. and 9s. 6d. for f-pts.—exactly double
pre-war prices—when the Defence Depart-
ment said it wanted them all for the camps.
One house has a few still for sale (f-pts. at
95.), but they are Jap. and won’t compare
with English enamel. Now if the Ade-
laide firm has any on offer —and it ought to
be able to turn them out at less than the
present prices of imported—why doesn’t it
get them into the Melbourne warehouses?

“Uncertificated”: It’s not often you get
the -final kick from a circular, but that
experience was mine last week. I had
come to the last ragged end of my resources.
My boots were burst, my trousers bagging
at the knees, my credit down in the mud and
general ruin impending. And in the blackest
hour of my despair arrived a smiling post-
man laden with a circular from a safe-
deposit company, illustrated with prison-like
pictures of barred vaults and filled with
entreaties to me to go and lock all
my spare valuables and cash away
in its steel and concrete stomach, where
they and it would be safe for ever.
If the manager of the accursed place had
called upon me personally three riiinutes
after I had received his advertisement, I
would have taken great delight in strangling
him like a gentleman in a specially-bought
pair of white cotton gloves.
* * * *
John Currie, of Lara, in the Western Dis-
trict of Victoria, is the timber authority of
the Lismore Plains. In 1872 there were
only four trees in his neighborhood. Now on
four Currie stations there are 1200 acres
planted with mostly-Australian timber. On
Titanga Patrick Lang has 500 acres; the
Omans have 100 acres, and at Gnarpurt
James Manifold owns 800 acres of valuable
growth of various kinds and ages. Alto-
gether, according to a recent calculation, a
million and a quarter trees are now growing
on the Plains, and already they are worth
over fSO.OOO. It is a helpful object-lesson in
a neglected branch of Australian industry.
* * * *
“S. & Co.”: Can you give us a suitable
name for our knitted bedsocks? We used
to have a big turnover in this line. Last
year we called them footwarmers, and didn’t
sell a dozen pairs.
THE APPLE-EATERS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Thanks to the generous efforts of the press and the
kindly co-operation of the people, apples are now
being eaten by all classes in extraordinary quantities,
and it is probable that the great bulk of our surplus
crop will be disposed of. —Victorian orchardist.
The place where once I took my lunch,
’Twas then a peaceable retreat,
Is rendered awful by the scrunch
Of those who endless apples eat;
It seems that all repose is fled —
My wife eats apples in her bed!
In theatres the people sit,
And eat and eat, and never cease ;
In stalls and gallery and pit
There is no hope of earthly peace.
The player’s tale is drowned beneath
The tireless grind of loyal teeth.
The barber chew's an apple w'here
I have my curls curtailed, and e’en
In church they swamp the parson’s prayer
With apple-eating. People mean
To eat three hundred thousand tons—
Thank God it wasn’t onions !
VIC TORI A.
EDDYSON.
SETTLING-UP.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
They say if I
Should settle down,
I’d find that Care
No more would frown;
But 'tis this sorrow
Fills my eup—
I have no means
To settle up !
N.S.W.
H. M.
MARY AUSTRALIA’S LAMB.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Mary had a little lamb;
Its fleece shone like the sun;
She cut the stuff from off its back
And sold it to the Hun.
The weather it got cool one day,
So Mary went to town;
But owing to the war they say
She bought no winter gown.
The clever Meat Ring bought the lamb
For foreign use, I hear,
And Mary dined on Yankee beans
As chops were far too dear.
Once Mary had a little lamb....
The story, I’m afraid,
Shows Mary hasn’t speed enough
To collar German trade.
N.s.w.
UNCLE BOB.
AUSTRALIAN MUSIC.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Special Australian music has been arranged for th
choristers, organ and band.
—Cable in reference to Anzac celebration.
Australian music! How is this thing done?
What magic novel melodies are flowing?
What harvests of the tuning-fork are won?
What strange horns and trombones are they blow-
ing?
What pipes, what timbrels, what wild ecstasy
Have Anzac stalwarts brought from oversea?
Somewhere a concertina, bleating long,
Sets feet amid the eucalyptus moving,
Or follows some unending dirge of song,
To wombats and the ring-tailed ’possum proving
The vast extent of the parental joy
That sheltered round “the Wild Colonial Boy.”
Is this Australian music? God forbid!
Some harmony of wilder, grander merit—
In flowery places of the deep bush hid—
The children of that bush must needs inherit.
Shall London hear borne on her fogs along
Some sun-sweet sweep of continental song?
Australian music! Who and what and how?
This thing eludes me. I have never met it.
Bleat of the sheep and mooing of the cow!
A mighty theme is ours, but who shall set it?
Instead, I fear the tortured English air
Will squirm to tootlings of “Australia Fair.”
N.S.W.
GEORGE STREET.

The Motor Car has become an indispensable part of the economic life of the
Commonwealth. Originally an article used for pleasure only, the Motor Car has
advanced to a position in the business world unsurpassed in importance by any other
commercial or industrial feature in Australia.
It is of untold importance to the Farmer, the Station Owner and the Orchardist,
playing an invaluable part in the transport of produce, fruit, fodder, supplies, etc.
The Doctor through its agency saves valuable lives, the Salesman reaches territory
impossible of access, without its aid. The Business Man finds it an integral part of
his business, in the hack country the Mails are carried by Car, Motor Ambulances are
equipped for War Service, whilst Storekeepers, Butchers, Bakers, Dairymen and a
host of other tradespeople keep pace with their deliveries only by employing the
Motor Car.
In short, there is hardly a business activity in Australia in which the Motor Car does
not play a liighly-important part. In this development of commercial and industrial
conditions the Ford has led the way and performed the greatest work. It has been
calculated that 90 per cent, of the Ford Cars in Australia are directly or indirectly
used in connection with business activities.

Sufferers from constipation know from
experience that after temporary relief has
been obtained by the use of a purgative, the
condition often becomes worse. Most strong
purgatives have a tendency to cause consti-
pation after their immediate effects are over.
Pinkettes, the new laxative, are a real help
in correcting the irregularity. They do not
wear out in their effects, and taken accord-
ing to directions, the patient will find that
they soon help to create a healthy regularity.
They are a safe family medicine because they
contain no habit creating drug; they are
daintily got up ; are easily given to children
and they act thoroughly without causing
sickness and griping. Write for a free trial

FIFTIETH ANNUAL REPORT OF THE DIRECTORS TO THE
SHAREHOLDERS.
It is with pleasure the Directors submit herewith for the information of Shareholders the Balance-
Sheet of the Bank as on 27th March, 1916, also Statements of Profit and Loss and the Reserve Fund
on that date.
The Balance of Profit and boss is .. .. .. .. .. .. ■■ £62,996 lo 0
which it is proposed to appropriate as follows: —
To Dividend at the rate of 8 per cent, per annum
20,000 0 0
leaving a balance of.. .. •• •• .. •• •• •• •• £42,996 lo 0
to be carried forward.
An Interim Dividend at the rate of S per cent, per annum was paid in November last.
Since the last Report agencies have been opened at Muljarra, Port Rickaby and Wvnarka, and the
Wilkawatt agency has been reopened. . .
It is with much regret we record the death in December last of Mr. James Harvey, who occupied
a seat at the Board for 23 years—during the last two as Chairman of Directors.
In February last Mr. FI. C. E. Muecke resigned his seat at the Board, after 21 years valuable ser-
vice as a director.
The Chairmanship of Directors was filled by the election of Mr. Arthur Waterhouse, and tne two
vacancies on the directorate were filled respectively by the election of Messrs. Howard W. Llojd and
Arthur G. Rymill. . fVI „
It is noteworthy that the 11th December last was the 50th anniversary ot the date on which tne
bank opened its doors for business.
Attention is called to a new’ item in the Balance-sheet, viz., Government Wheat Advances Account,
£602,843 —this necessarily affects Cash Balances.

AN UNTAMED TERRITORY. The
Northern Territory of Australia. By
ELSIE R. MASSON. With Illus-
trations and Maps. Extra Crown
Bvo. 6s.
Nature: “The most perplexing Australian
problem is that of the Northern Territory, and
all interested in the present efforts to solve
it will welcome Miss Masson’s interesting
sketches of that vast land of dilemmas. The
book deals with various phases of life and
work in the Northern Territory, and records
the impression of a keen and sympathetic ob-
server who had unusual opportunities for insight
into its present condition and prospects. .. .
Miss Masson’s sketches should prove a contribu-
tion of permanent value to its literature, as an
instructive picture of the country during the
most critical stage in its development.”

BOOK. By J. H. MORGAN, late
Home Office Commissioner with the
British Expeditionary Force. Extra
crown Bvo, ss. net.
The Globe : “One who has nothing but praise
for this admirable book, which records the
impressions of a trained observer, whose official
duties took him behind the scenes to a greater
extent than has been the case with most writers
of war books.” ____

651 THE SHEPHERD OF THE
NORTH. By RICHARD AUMERLE
~ MATTER.
*** The hero of this book is a Roman Catholic
Bishop of singularly fine character. The story
of his work among the people of the Adirondack
country, and particularly of the part that he
plays in the fight that is waged against an
encroaching railroad, is intensely dramatic,
4nd>'comes to a thrilling climax with a forest
re, described in a most vivid and powerful
manner.
652 THE RUDDER. A Novel with
several Heroes. By MARY S.
WATTS, Author of The Rise of
Jennie Cushing, etc.
*** Mrs. Watts’s new volume contains the
history of an intelligent, well-bred girl, who
touches life at many points. The story is
told with the charm of manner, the know-
ledge of human nature, and the humor that
are always to be found in books by this
author, and it is consequently of very great
interest.

POLITICAL
POINTS
A NATIONAL CALAMITY.
“Luck seems to follow that hoy.”
"What a pity hick isn’t a recruiting ser-
geant!”
Chadband and McStiggins managed to get
in a word or two for poor old I key when a
“fake” Totalizator Bill was thrown at the
N. S. Wales Assembly. Chadband’s main
argument was that the effect of intro-
ducing the totalisator is to make Ikey more
prosperous, and that “in no place where
the totalisator has been installed has the
bookmaker disappeared.” I key’s principal
objection to the machine is that it will force
him to get work. Which suggests that
McStiggins reckons he knows more about
the game than the children of Israel. And
Israel, to do him justice, is too astute to
call McStiggins a poor old fool.
The passage of the Eight Hours Bill
through the N.S.W. Parliament, after many
difficulties with the Uppish House, makes a
more considerable change than many people
may suppose, because out of 1050 occupa-
tions regulated by awards only 727 have
already secured the 48 hours week. Now,
directly the Act is proclaimed, the man who
delivers the milk, the clerk who has been
in the habit of working unpaid overtime,
and scores of others will start watching the
clock with renewed interest.
“Mr. William Hughes, anyone can see, is
a man who will go far.” Yes, we have seen
Australian democratic leaders go far in
London ere this —quite to the Devil, in fact.
Westralia’s lately-imposed Profit Tax is
giving the State’s one great industry a
punch on the nose, because a stupid Depart-
ment insists that money spent on develop-
ment work is profit. Of course it isn't
any more than the money which a draper
spends on advertising or sign-writing or
which a grocer spends in sending round for
orders is profit. No doubt taxes must be
raised ; but it is suicidal to penalise people
for spending money in the attempt to
develop the great natural resources that we
are told are so much in need of development.
Jensen, Minister for the Navy, has been
explaining that under conscription “the
poor and the middle classes would suffer.”
Perhaps he will now explain (1) how much
they have gained by not having conscrip-
tion; (2) how much the French poor and
middle-class have lost in consequence of the
nation being ready to meet the invader with
a bayonet; and (3) how much better off in
human life and cash John Bull is on account
of being still not quite ready after 18
months of war. When he has done this,
Jensen might proceed to explain what a
fine thing it was for the poor and middle-
class men to die at Lonesome Pine because
the voluntary system hadn’t provided
enough to back them up. And then, all that
done, the Government might appropriately
tie Jensen up in the back yard.
“Grizzled Darling” explains how Peacock
economises;—
How is this for circumlocution ■or plain idiocy?
A Victorian State Department frequently corresponds
with another one on the floor immediately beneath.
To do so it solemnly licks a stamp and puts it on the
envelope, which it sometimes marks “URGENT,”
and stealthily conveys the letter downstairs past, or
through, the office it is addressed to, and out to the
street pillar-box. Some time that day or night the
P.O. motor whirls it along another stage, and it
passes through half-a-dozen pairs of hands in the
G.P.O. before finally wending its way back, a day
or two later, to the starting point—or, to be
strictly accurate, to within I7|ft. of it as the smoke
flics.
To the argument of Bung that closing up
the pubs at six o’clock is an interference
with the liberty of people who work until
that hour, and “therefore inconsistent with
democracy,” Cold Tea puts it forward that
this is an attempt to give a new defini-
tion to democracy—“the right to get drunk.”
It all hints at the controversial bogs that
people are going to get into later on when
their blood is really up. There is nothing
inconsistent with democracy in shutting up
pubs, or fruit shops or picture shows, or
I. WAV. orators or any other thing, person
or place which the democrat thinks ought to
be shut; riioreover, if it were decided by
popular vote to shut up every wowser in the
land. Bung would give three cheers for
such a vindication of democratic principles.
He would also forget to say anything about
the liberty of the individual. So far as
getting drunk is concerned, democracy does
most emphatically cherish the right to deter-
mine whether it will go on the jag or not.
What is inconsistent with democratic gov-
ernment is to conspire to prevent the demo-
crat deciding whether the democracy, in addi-
tion to consuming wine and beer, shall also
itself manufacture these liquids and sell
them.
Even Hughes has been talking in London
about the “millions to be spent in putting
people on the land after the war” —and
every land shark and monopolist in Aus-
tralia licked his lips as he read it. The
conversion of boom land values into perma-
nent national debt represents something
mightily appetising to Fat; but if in addi-
tion to a few other things which have to
be shouldered labor acquires that pup—well,
it will find itself with an animal on the
premises that can be relied upon to rob the
kitchen three times a day for all eternity.
There is a- movement within the N. S.
Wales Labor party to get rid of the glue
around the axle; which indicates that all
the members are not demoralised. But that
stalwart champion of Liberalism, Sydney
Telegraph, sees great danger in this, and
wildly calls upon the “moderate elements”
in the Labor movement to resist being
dropped out, because unless they can hang-
on Labor will become a mere Ishmaelite.
This is very fiue. The elements Labor has
to get rid of are the loanmongers, muddlers
and spongers who conceive that the aim of
the Labor movement is to keep them in a
job. And it is just this “moderate” sec-
tion that Liberalism wants to see undis-
turbed, clinging like Old Men of the Sea
around Labor’s neck. Liberalism knows
that, burdened with that disastrous load.
Labor is already growing ashamed of itself.
It is not a party which will fight for its
platform and principles that the great
dailies want to argue the point with. To
them a Parliamentary party which pro-
claims its own degradation is a much more
agreeable opponent.
Billy Hughes knows quite well how to
smash the Kaiser and capture all the trade
6t Germany. But Billy Hughes’s Govern-
ment doesn’t know how to settle a strike
of half-a-dozen cutters who are hanging up
tin- work of making uniforms for the troops
at the Federal clothing factory. You see,
the Kaiser hasn’t got a vote in West Sydney.
A CHIP OFF THE OLD BLOCK.
HANS: “Gome and Way Zeppelins at our
blace, Max. We god a new baby.”
In exporting as well as in importing the
foreign-trade habit gets in its unpatriotic
work. Notwithstanding the huge wheat yield
the Victorian flourmills are half idle, while
bran and pollard are becoming scarcer daily.
The beauty of exporting wheat in place of
flour is that you not only send away certain
valuable offals for which you practically get
nothing, but you simultaneously hamper the
dairy-farming industry and deprive the soil
of the country of certain much-needed essen-
tials. Also you give the foreigner a lot of
work that he has no time to do just now.
Yet the Federal Government still goes on
claiming that it is out to encourage Aus-
tralian industry in every form.
Heaven only knows where the insanity of
our party politics is going to lead us. The
other day, as one lot of Government work-
men went out after building a new and
costly block of shops near the Sydney rail-
way station, another lot of Government
workmen came in to demolish the premises,
most of which had never been occupied. The
appalling part of the business was that the
building was completed although it was
known at the time that the land it occupied
might be required for the city railway-—the
railway which is being pushed on with at this
time simply and solely because t' e Govern-
ment is under an obligation to provide the
impeccable Norton Griffiths syndicate with
a certain amount of work every year.
W. 11. Lee, the new apprentice Premier
of Tasmania, is a maker of waggons and
ploughs, and has been seven years in Par-
liament. lie is Chief Secretary, and
though quite unqualified has also taken
on the Education Dept. Lee is a pillar of
Cold Tea and a rigid 6 o’clocker. His
second in command is Lewis, a belted knight
who has been 24 years in Parliament and
Premier several times; but he is so
hopelessly conservative that in 1912 his own
party shunted him in favor of the late A. E.
Solomon. Lewis is Treasurer, as he has
been before; also he is to handle Mines,
things about which he knows nothing. He
is a supporter of Rung and a 10 o’clocker.
V. B. Propsting, a schoolmaster turned
lawyer, and a man of much ability but little
backbone, who was once Premier, but has
been buried in the House of Dodery for
years, is Attorney-General and also Minister
for Railways. Lastly, J. B. Ilavs, a farmer
who once chased gold in W.A., is Minister
for Lands, Works and Agriculture after a
three years’ run in Parliament. Shields,
M.L.C., a man of brains and abilitv and
the possessor of a spine, was offered the
Attorney-Generalship, but declined it,
though he accepted office without a port-
folio The new Ministry is hooked for a
passage ’ for its mf kiority consists of
A hitsitt and several ex-Ministers, such as
Burgess, Ilean. Mulcahy and Payne, who
all ought to have been asked in.

THE RED TAPE WORM.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Thousands of pounds a year are being saved
through economies approved by Senator Pearce. The
issue of red-tape, ink, pencils, string and sealing-
wax is watched to prevent waste. — Melb. Herald.
“The time has come,” the statesman said,
“For taking stock of things—-
Of pencils, tape and sealing-wax,
And all small reckonings;
For cutting down expenditure,
And tightening the strings.”
It was the Red Tape Worm that heard
Tlie ukase of the boss
To cut his vast gyrations short,
And it was very cross—•
“For am not I,” it asked with truth,
“A high and mighty Joss?
“For years I have securely held
The service by the wrists;
My bonds controlled Base Records, and
The Casualty Lists,
And Military Orders tied
With many turns and twists.
“Nay, further, were it not for me
The Censor had been lost;
For him 1 bound men’s hands, what time
He chloroformed the host,
And ’neath his anaesthetic touch
Tlie truth gave up the ghost.
“ ’Twas I that braked efficiency,
And strapped the gag secure
Between Administration’s teeth
To make its slowness sure;
And anything approaching haste
I did my best to cure.
“And now arises one who dares
My services to spurn ;
But time is short, and tape is long,
As all reformers learn ;
His die is cast, for at the last
The Red Tape Worm will turn.”
VICTORIA.
THE PARASITE.


SPORTING NOVELS
Readers of Sporting Stories have been well catered
for by Australian writers. In the famous
Bookstall ’ ’ Series of Australian Books
the . most successful library published in Australia, there are more than a
score of sporting novels, whose sales closely approach the half-million mark.
The latest addition:
“UNDER A CLOUD,”
By ARTHUR WRIGHT.
The best novel yet written by this
popular Australian Author. The
story of a remarkably successful
jockey, whose career is ruined in his
hour of triumph. But that is only
the beginning of a new and mysterious
career that leads the reader into all
sorts of unexpected situations.
ARTRUR^J^a
WmGMT
‘IN THE LAST STRIDE” (by Arthur
Wright) deals with incidents in several sports
—football, boxing and horseracing—and an all-
round athlete is the hero of the story. In
addition to the sporting interest, there are
several exciting episodes (including an opium-
smuggling adventure), and there is a strong
love interest.
‘EROS! EROS WINS!” (by F. Agar)
proves quite as exciting as its title indicates.
It is a brightly-written story which success-
fully exposes much turf villainy.
‘RUNG IN” (by Arthur Wright) carries a
sub-title which, to a certain extent, explains
the book: “A Taste of the Turf, Interwoven
with a Murder Mystery and a Love Story.”
It is a sparkling novel, possessing great
dramatic strength.
‘A ROGUE’S LUCK” (by Arthur Wright)
does not require any further recommendation
than that it is by the Australian author
whom both press and public acclaim as a
serious rival of Nat Gould. In this book
Mr. Arthur Wright has provided an excep-
tionally strong and clever story which has
proved one of the most popular of his books.
“WOLAROI’S CUP” (by Ambrose Pratt)
presents a picture of a Melbourne Cup-winner
in the making, and a splendid descriptive
account of the methods adopted by owners,
trainers and jockeys is given. The story
possesses a strong love interest, and is a clever
handling of an entirely new plot.
“GAMBLERS’ GOLD” (by Arthur Wright)
is a good, thrilling, sensational story in
which the course of true love is impeded by
the effects of an old-time tragedy, but which
is again set on its happy way by means of
some fine sporting incidents.
“IN RACING SILK” (by Gerald R. Bald-
win) is an interesting tale of the turf, inter-
mingled with an absorbing love story. An
exposition of the shady ways of an unscrupu-
lous bookmaker is a feature of the book.
“THE MARE WITH THE SILVER
HOOF” (by Bob Allen) is, of course, a
story of the turf —a story with an exceed-
ingly clever and interesting plot, by a writer
whose turf techinque is perfect. “Easily the
best racing novel that has come under our
notice,” says the Stock and Station
Journal.
Price: One Shilling Each; Posted 1/1.
All Bookstalls and Booksellers in the Commonwealth.

SPORTING
NOTIONS
The running in the Autumn Stakes and
St. Leger at the A.J.C. meeting was a wholly-unreli-
able guide as to what was to follow —during the
first two days, anyhow. In the open w.f.a. event
(ljj-mile), Carlita looked the best of the bunch on
paper. But she didn’t look particularly good to the
eye, though naturally that didn’t prevent her starting
favorite, because among the crowd who go a-racing
the number who take the trouble to inspect the
animal carrying their cash is relatively small. Giru
had a solid 'following. Reputation was at 4’s, Green
Cap at 10’s, and Amata, who was being given a
longer run than usual, at 12’s. Half a mile from
home Lewis had to ride Green Cap, and following
Giru into the straight he got his head in front at
the distance, to the great joy of punters who had
been backing him for the Cup. But what seemed
like a good thing didn’t come off; Reputation, arriv-
ing with a long, strong challenge, cut down the
leaders at every stride and won by a clear length.
Green Cap faded away in the last pinch and finished
behind Giru, Amata and Carlita. Incidentally, Scout-
master, winner of the last Metropolitan, was about
the tenth to finish. Reputation’s performance was a
strikingly good one, and brought him into great
demand for the big event on Monday—-until it was
discovered that one of his legs had filled and he had
been given a holiday.
Patrobas, Cetigne, Kandos and Common Law made
up the St. Leger field. Cetigne, starting at 7 to 4
on, looked a moral, for the Melbourne Cup winner
was clearly a long way from being himself. It
turned out a poor race to watch. After a mile had
been covered at a slow pace Cetigne was taken to the
front ahead of Common Law, and sharpening up the
speed reached the straight just clear of Kandos. Up
to the distance Cetigne appeared to be drawing away,
but there Kandos came again, and after an all-in
tussle appropriated the bacon by a long neck from
the Derby winner. Cetigne may have been made too
much use of on the passage ; but it is very doubtful
whether there is much in this excuse. What seems
a quite reasonable explanation is that Kandos won
on his merits. To the writer, Cetigne did not appear
to stay too well. On the day it seemed a fairly
safe conclusion that the Cup was going to be worth
seeing.
It W’as the 51st Doncaster Handicap, and for
Woorak (9st. 121 b.) there was money in hatsful. It
was piled on the brilliant deceiver down to 3 to 1,
and there were heaps more for “Baron” Brown’s
flighty-headed Quairneta, who by her win in the
Rosehill Flying had sprung into popular favor.
Though this was only her second appearance in public
4’s were considered fair odds in a field of 24, in-
cluding some of the best milers in the Common-
wealth. Always a slick beginner, Woorak was
auicklv in the Van, upsides with Rosedell. What a
beautiful galloper he is! Sweeping round the bend
the white-faced wonder ran ahead, and thousands
of punters started feeling if their tickets were safe.
At "the turn it looked as if the Epsom was being
repeated, with Woorak coming home alone. The
well-backed Lord Nagar (Bst. 21b.) and Pilbarra
(Bst. 31b.), who had been well forward up till this
stage, had now faded away, but Brattle (Bst. 101 b.)
and Eurobin (Bst. 131 b.) were hanging on to argue
the point, and at the distance were asking the flying
chestnut how much longer he could last. This is a
question Woorak never likes, and though he stalled
off Brattle, the other, galloping with great resolu-
tion, wore him down. Thus the Doncaster was
pulled off by Eurobin after a desperate, gruelling
finish. There were not many feeling for tickets on
him, and from the land of Israel there broke a
sound like unto a cataract of cash disappearing into
deep, cavernous receptacles. Quairneta had got
tangled up in the field, and stayed there. .The mile
was run in “393.”
The whole tribe of professional tipsters, punters,
touts, trainers, breeding specialists and all the rest
of them were uppercut and outed when Eurobin
came to light in the Doncaster. Here was a horse
with an exceptional winning record, which had been
heavily backed for the Newmarket Handicap, and had
been working lately at Randwick right under the
public nose. When stripped before the race he looked
very' well. Yet scarcely anyone gave him a dog’s
chance. He drifted away to the post at any old
price—30, 40, 50 to 1 according to the Ikey con-
sulted—while the public wildly threw good, reliable
currency on a pony with a welter-weight. All of
which once more proves that for the average man to
try to pick the winner of a handicap is equivalent
to exporting gold to Judea, and that what racing
men don’t know about their business would fill a
large-sized book. On form and condition Eurobin
stood out as one of the best horses in training, as
this paper last week took the opportunity of remark-
ing. He won because he galloped like a champion,
lie was a rank outsider mainly because the public
has long, pendulous ears.
For the Cup on Monday the democracy would
have nothing but Green Cap (7st. 71b.) —why
Heaven only knows, for the price on offer was just
plain murder. There was a field of 26, the horse
apart from his win at the Hawkesbury had to be
taken very largely on trust, his running in the Autumn
Stakes had not been at all impressive, yet Ikey had to
work hard writing tickets at two to one —with 25
others going for the bag! Certainly he carried a
stone and a half below w.f.a., but the odds were
enough to scare a Chinaman. Kandos (Bst. 21b.)
and Dollar Dictator (6st. 71b.) were backed at
eights, Cetigne and Giru at 12’s, and the rest
petered out at 20’s, 40’s and 50’s. And once more
the public w r ent down with the familiar old thud
and recent running was turned inside out and upside
down. After about seven furlongs had been left
behind Prince Bardolph (7st. 31b.), a despised out-
sider, hopped past Foil and Giru into the lead, and
clapping on the pace showed the way round the back,
Green Cap being at this point about seventh, while
neither Kandos nor Cetigne had been sighted. Was
it to be last year’s performance, when Scotch Artil-
lery led from end to end? The crowd W'atched in
silence, and MeShortodds breathed heavily through
his nose. At the bend Lewis brought the favorite
up under pressure, and was on terms with Prince
Bardolph as they swung into , the straight. “Green
Cap,” “Green Cap. easy!” was roared by thousands.
But they roared too soon. . The pace had been a
killer, and Prince Bardolph, coming away from the
tiring field, Won by a good two lengths. He out-
stayed and out-galloped everything, and when the
clock stopped it said “24|” —the fastest two miles
ever run in the Commonwealth. Scoutmaster was
third. Cetigne was nowhere.
* * * *
This paper did its best to warn punters
against allowing Eurobin to run loose last Saturday.
Since the son of Andria won his race at Flemington
on New Year’s Day The Bulletin has published
several laudatory remarks upon him. Eurobin is a
high-class horse that might have won anything before
his merits were discovered to the handicappers. He’d
have got into a Melbourne Cup at ist. and
made a certainty of it, always presuming that he
wasn’t subjected to a “severe preparation. ’ Geldings
don’t want it.
* * * *
Riding horses these days is a profitable
business. Young Foulsham, through piloting Luro-
bin and Kandos in Doncaster and St. Leger, pocketed
£l7B. There is a human story attached to the
Doncaster. Rank outsider and red-hot favorite fought
out the finish. Woorak is trained by Ike Foulsham ;
it was his nephew aboard Eurobin who cut down the
hope of the multitude. The Doncaster winner was
stabled at the Coach and Horses, Randwick, and
when the nag was being taken to the course the land-
lady called out to the lad, “Put half a crown on for
me!” “Better keep it in your purse, Missus,” he
called back. The purse would have been fatter by
£6 10s. if she hadn’t. But no matter what price a
horse starts at it’s a million to one on some lucky
bargee planking a bit on. After Eurobin arrived an
excited individual fled to the telegraph office and
with trembling hand managed to compose a wire
home to the missus declaring that he had won
£lOO. He told the envious clerk that he dreamt
about the result. Several Noses with bulging bags
bemoaned that they had laid £2OO to £4 about the
brute. They are never satisfied.
On Saturday and Monday the aggregate
punter fell into the soup right over his ears. Some
of the wagering was deadly. Proceedings opened
right enough with the Spire winning the Hurdles
at nine to two (he negotiated the two miles in
3min. 41 Isec. —an Australian record, and only -Jsec.
outside the world’s best) and then blue ruin brooded
over the landscape. Not another favorite got home
during the day. Long-priced outsiders bobbed up
with horrid regularity. In the Sires’ Produce Stakes
Miss Tressady was at two’s and five to two. She
finished absolutely last. The imported Sydian,
about whom there was much mystery in Vic. lately,
went out in the High-weight Handicap in fierce
demand at three’s in a crowd of 27, and at the end
there were 26 animals ahead of the wretched prad.
This, with the fall of Giru, Carlita, Woorak, Quair-
neta and Cetigne, left the public about enough
to pay its tram fare home. But it came back
with more cash on Monday and tackled Ikey again
and got jumped on from the start. Wolaroi was
the single favorite who arrived in time. Of the 12
races run on the two days the winners of 11 xvere
sired bv imported horses.
* * * *
After Monday there was no doubt about
the name of the most expeditious youngster of the
year. It is Wolaroi, who achieved the rich Cham-
pagne Stakes after a vigorous set-to with Portrina.
He carried 9st. 61b. and ran the six furlongs in
lmin. 13sec. The Newcastle-owned filly, Thana,
which won the still richer Sires’ Produce Stakes on
the opening day, got 21b. and a beating into third
place in the Champagne. But she is a rare good
filly, all the same. Wolaroi has now appropriated
more cash than any previous two-year-old had ever
raked together in Australia.
* * * *
The gilt attached to the Doncaster (£2106)
and the Cup ( £4342) went to Vic. Prince Bardolph
was purchased as a yearling in Sydney for 270 gns.
He is trained by Mark Whitty at Flemington.
Eurobin hails from Gippsland, and is leased by his
trainer, J. Whitebourne. Both animals are geldings.
A horse that has been much in the lime-
light on Melbourne suburban courses lately is Short
Rain. At Mentone, carrying Bst. 51b., he won the
third-division handicap after being backed from 20’s
to 8-1. Following Monday, at Moonee Valiev, he
started second favorite in the April Purse, and just
got there from the crack 14.2 pony Carwelkin. Next,
at Sandown Park, he met Carwelkin on 181 b. worse
terms, and won again. Another Aurofodina in the
making perhaps.
* * * *
“Spreader”: A champion racehorse has to
gallop for his reputation. A champion sire in most
cases is a rank opportunist. First-class wives will
make a name for any good horse, but a moke like
Positano would have been a dead certainty to miss
getting classic or first-class handicap winners from
station mares. Sir Modred, bred in Maoriland in
1877, turned out later on to be the best all-round
sire in America. He opened his stud career in J. B.
Ilaggin’s stables, where success for him was predes-
tined. Carbine at Welbeck had another oppor-
tunity of the made-to-order sort. The best-proved
horses as sires and ornaments of the Australian
turf are Positano and Fisherman, though the latter
did all his cheque-earning as a racehorse in Eng-
land. The blood of Panic, originally imported to
Tasmania in ’6O, has proved the most far-reaching.
Of the born-to-blush-unseen contingent, Sunrise, who
stood in the Junee district for years, was never
mated with any really first-class mares, yet perpe-
trated a phenomenal number of winners till old age
gathered him in. AVhat might he have accomplished
at llobartville,. or St. Albans? As new and unex-
pected traits come to light all the “lines of blood”
and “characteristic” theories go by the board and
take their place on the back shelves in company
with Mendelisms and figure deductions. The Aus-
tralian thoroughbred is the compeer of his English
relatives in most things, barring perhaps the ability
to whirl over 1320 yards with a postage stamp on
deck —and that, after all, is the thing that matters
most in our present stage of lunacy.
* * * »
Some racing prads have the rottenest luck.
A nag named Limpet at present performing in
Maoriland has started nine times this season. She
won once, and in the next eight attempts ran into
a place that wasn’t first. Another, Prince Eddie,
has out of 11 starts scored one solitary win and
scrambled into 10 other places.
This war is really getting too absurd!
Anyhow, the S. Aus. Jockey Club evidently thinks so;
for it asked the military authorities whether they
couldn’t get off Morphettville racecourse before the
Adelaide Cup week comes on. The authorities re-
plied, “regretfully,” that they can’t possibly do it;
so the Adelaide Cup and Goodwood will be "run this
year, like the Birthday Cup and City Handicap, at
Victoria Park.
* * * *
Steeplechasing is booming in Wilson’s
country, where it has never before been popular.
At the United Hunt’s meeting, to start on May 13
at Belmont, a big handicap sw’eepstake of 21 miles is
to be run, the added money being 700 sovs., which
will make the event worth over 1000 sovs. to the
winner. The meeting is to extend over two days,
with 13 events, all across country or over sticks.
* * * #
The German Derby will, unless the Allies
get there first, be run at Hamburg on June 25, so
the Hun evidently reckons he won’t be eating racing
nags for a day or two.
* * * *
Tommy Horan, great batsman, delightful
cricket writer and genial soul, has finished his
innings. By Victorians of the late ’7o’s and early
’Bo’s he was looked upon almost as a prodigy, and
many old-timers still contend that Victoria has pro-
duced no greater batsman. Certain it is that Mel-
bourne saw no greater leg-hitter, and if there weie
only a few Horans in the game to-day the abomin-
able leg-theory business would soon be dead. Horan was
a run-getter, thumping up century scores against Eng-
land (one), N. 8. Wales (three), South Aus. (one),
and in many minor contests. In first-class and pen-
nant cricket he accumulated about 10,000 runs, and
made thousands more in minor contests.
The late Thomas Horan was one of the
galaxy of stars that made up the ’B2 Australian
XI, which was famous for beating, for the first time
on earth, a picked English XL This memorable
match was played at Lords, and Australia won by
seven runs. What a team it was! Blackham,
Bonnor, Boyle, McDonnell, Palmer, Horan (Vic.),
A Bannerman, Murdoch, Garrett, Jones, Massie,
Spofforth (N.S.W.), G. Giffen (S.A.). Horan came
back with a batting average of 2a, which was con-
sidered good in those days Most of the famous
team are still alive. Tom Garrett is Public trustee
in n. S. Wales, and Harry Massie is general manager
of the Commercial Banking Co. of Sydney. One of
the mast curious events in Horan’s career was the
disposal of Billy Murdoch in the inter-State match,
N. S. Wales v. Vic., played at Sydney in January,
1882. N.S.W., batting first, amassed 775, a huge
score. Murdoch was over 300 when, as a last resort,
Horan went on to bowl —for the first time in his
life. Billy glanced one of his deliveries to leg, and
Blackham, who was keeping sticks, chased the ball.
As he was wearily plodding back from the boundary
he yelled to Horan to go on bowling. Horan went
on, and there not being a wicketkeeper Murdoch
went for a slog. He mishit, and the ball went fly-
ing over his head to a spot usually without a fields-
man. Blackham strolling in-field happened to be in
the way, and the epoch-marking innings came to an
end. Horan developed into quite a decent change-
bowler afterwards.
* * *
“Myna”: Tommy Horan was noted for
several things, among them his appetite, the size of
his hat, and his memory. Old-time cricketers speak
with wonder of his gastronomic feats and his vast
hat was almost as well known in Melbourne streets
as that of the late lamented Ranfurly Knox. 1t was
usually worn well down on to the back of his head,
and in close contact with his ears. Tommy’s memory
frequently caused him to recite poetry and prose,
particularly such as had been perpetrated by one
Shakspeare. In his younger days he was verra
careful o’ the siller. It is related that on one of his
tours practically half his personal expenditure con-
sisted of the £6"or £7 a dress-suit cost him. Yet he
was thoroughbred Irish, born on the ould sod in ’55.
* * * *
Tommy Horan’s death leaves only Frank
Allan and Jack Blackham of the Victorian section
of Dave Gregory’s famous 1878 Australian XI.
Only three barrackers—W. Slight, W. Trump and
Dave Scott —cheered them off at Spencer-street via
Sydney, for England; and Scott is the only remnant
of that cheer still above ground. After a tour of
37 matches (18 of which were won) how the thou-
sands cheered when they came back again ! Horan
never got that incident out of his life. He w T as a
great cricketer, but at the end his greatest pride was
in those two boys of his who are helping to save
civilisation from Kultur.
* * * *
“Devilfish”: Away back about 3800
attempts to acclimatise salmon and salmon-trout in
Tasmania were begun. The salmon-trout have stocked
rivers all over the island, but though the efforts to
get the real salmon to feel at home
were successful for a while the fish
eventually disappeared. The theory
is that they went to sea and forgot
to come back; anyway, it is doubtful
if there is one left now in any Tas-
manian stream, though there is no
obvious reason why there should not
be. Every now and then someone
blow's in with a yarn of having
caught the real Simon Pure in the
Pieman or the Gordon or some other
West Coast stream, but these seem
to be big salmon-trout run to seed;
none of those submitted to experts
have been diagnosed as real salmon.
They certainly grow to an exalted
size; there is an authenticated case
of a 401 b. fish from the Pieman, and
I have heard a legend of a 60-pounder.
They don’t waste time fishing with
rods, reels and flies on the West
Coast; they use a net, a dynamite
plug or a gun. The anglers on the Pieman used to
stand on a convenient rock, and when the back of
a big fish ploughing his way up-stream showed
above the water they had at him with a bullet or a
charge of shot.
* * * *
The Moana on its last trip dropped at
Wellington (M.L.) an old friend —the Rev. M.
Molyneaux, the athletic parson who a few years
dgo brought out a team of English footballers.
Recently he has been a naval chaplain; but he wants
to fight alongside some of his old Maori antagonists,
and has come all this way to enlist.
* * * *
Rugby in Auckland (M.L.) appears likely
to be wiped out this season among boys.
Delegates of the various clubs recently decided to
recommend the Union that no player over 20 years
of age should be permitted to come into the competi-
tions. So deadly in earnest about it were the club
representatives that they refused to entertain a pro-
posal to exempt men over 20 who had offered to
enlist and had been turned down by the doctor.
* * * *
St. Peter’s College (Adelaide) broke its
long run of failure in sport at this year’s athletic
meeting, winning from Prince Alfred’s by 12 points.
The competition was keen all through, and no boy
won more than two events. R. M. Fowler cut the
hurdles record for this fixture down to 16 4-ssec.
Old-time successes give St. Peter’s 14 wins against
five in these annual contests.

The Yanks aren’t too proud to slay one
another, anyhow. During the recent season 16
players were killed while playing the weird brand of
murder known as American football.

Swimming records by women given in
the New York Police Gazette Annual, 1916, are
all at sea. Annette Kellerman is given as the
record-holder of a mile, with 32min. 44sec. This
is only 6min. 36sec. slower than Fanny Durack’s
time ! E. McKay’s 6min. 51sec. is alleged to be the
feminine record for 440yds. It isn’t. Fanny
Durack’s 6min. 3 2-ssec. is. The same girl’s 2min.
57sec. for 200yds. is ssec. behind Eanny’s time for
220yds. The 75yds. and lOOydSi records are placed
at 57 4-ssec7 and lmin. 12 3-ssec. The Australienne
long ago clipped them down to 49 4-ssec. and
lmin. 6sec.

“K.T.P.”: “0.K.” (B. 13/4/’l6) is wrong
when he says Donald Dinnie found nobody his equal
at hammer-throwing when he came to Aus-
tralia. On Melbourne Cricket Ground (26/l/’9l)
Hughie Kneebone beat Donald in projecting 561 b.
of ironmongery—Kneebone, 30ft. 6in. ; Dinnie, 25ft.
Bin. On the same day Kneebone beat the Scot at
throwing the hammer and tied with him at tossing
the caber. One of Kneebone’s feats was to put a
561 b. weight from a standing mark 20ft. 6in. Dinnie
could not equal this. Kneebone on several other
occasions defeated Dinnie at weight-putting and
hammer-throwing.
* * * *
When Albert Bird died lately at Bendigo
(Vie.), no one took any notice; yet in his time
the old man had been one of the greatest distance
runners the world has known. Born in ’46 at
Sheffield, he beat all the cracks of his day, and with
Hewitt and Topley was brought by George Coppin
to Melbourne, where he made his first appearance
on New Year’s Day, ’7O, in a mile handicap. In
this he gave starts up to 360 yards and won easily.
So good was he that the management had to make
relay matches for him. At Adelaide on one occa-
sion he ran 11 miles inside the hour, and raced nine
men 10 miles, the first running two and the others
a mile apiece. He oleaned up the whole-bunch at
every mile. Bird at one time put a cross on on*
of Victoria’s best-known bookmakers, when he mp«l
Bradshaw, of Ballarat, and Foster, of Maoriland over
five miles, each of his opponents having to run n
miles. Bird told the writer that he received £2(m
from one Ikey to lose and £4OO from another to
win, and win he did, beating Bradshaw at the half
distance and Foster at the finish. He was a little
man, sft. spn. in height, and Bst. 91b. in racinv
condition, but could stretch his pins over 7ft Bin-~
a freak stride for such a shortv. After his racine
career he went to the wall in quick time, and used
to hawk fruit and clean boots for a crust Old
sports who have seen peds come and go claim for Bird
that he was the prettiest distance runner they remem
ber. He got through thousands playing hazards.
Harry Stone, the American newsboy pug
who returned to Sydney a couple of weeks ago for
a season of bash, had a strenuous Eastertide. On
Saturday he collided with Herb McCoy at the Sta-
dium, and, after smoking a few cigars over Sunday
was ready to mix it with Tommy Uren on Monday
at the same establishment. Stone, isn’t a first-
flighter in Dallarland, but is fairly ’ dose up, and
since last seen in these parts has become rather
more expert in his own peculiar style of hit, duck
and jump. He is the swinging door all the time
and on well-oiled hinges, too. McCoy found him
more or less ungetatable, and over the greater part
of the journey was fooled and well outpointed. Some-
times the Victorian looks good enough to meet any-
thing. Saturday was not one of these occasions
But in Uren—taller, more vigorous and with a
heavier punch than McCoy—Stone found himself with
a different sort of job on hand, and spent most of
his time getting out of trouble. Uren carried the
fight on top of him throughout, and it was Stone
who didn’t win. The main result of all this un-
pleasantness is to create more uncertainty about the
relative greatness of Messrs. McCoy and Uren, which
can only be settled by more punch on the nose.
Because it packed up and rushed to press
a day early last week this page forgot to mention
that on the previous Saturday the colors of the
Lonsdale Belt holder, Llew Edwards, were lowered
for the second time in Australia at Melbourne. Bert
Spargo did it. The disturbance was odorless and inces-
sant. In the early stages Spargo took the bit in his
teeth, attacking both flanks and the centre and covering
up when Edwards started reprisals. In the ninth round
Llewellyn revived the spirit of ancient Cambria, and
began distributing bewilderment with short-range
left and right hooks to the gallery. Edwards’s
backers were already getting measured for new
suits, but after the fifteenth interval Spargo came
back with sufficient success to receive the referee's
fatherly pat. Then the crowd took a deep breath
and yelled a benediction.

How to Treat Your Hair and Complexion.
A Few Simple Beauty Hints.
By Mdlle. GABY DESLYS, the weil=known Parisian Actress.
YOU ask me for a few hints on tne
treatment of the hair and complexion.
Well, the less “treatment” you give
the skin the better. 1 do not
believe much in massage, but a
little cream to the face is neces-
sary to counteract the effects of
wind or sun. What cream
would I recommend? Well, I
advise you to use a little mer-
colised wax every night and
again in the morning, after
washing the face. Rub it
gently into the skin, then wipe
off any superfluous wax and
dust a little barri-agar over
the face. You will find that
this will be the only “treat-
ment” necessary and will keep
your face fresh and youthful-
looking for all your life. The mercolised
wax removes all the dead outer skin, so
that you have always a fair, fresh com-
plexion like a girl’s.
For the hair, the first and most important
m
Photo: Wrather & Buys.
thing is a good shampoo. Never u 8e anv
thing inferior to wash the hair with Get
some good stallax from your chemist and use
a teaspoonrul in a cup,of hot
water. Then rinse the hair
well and it will look bright and
glossy.
A tome is. necessary when the
hair is inclined to fall out more
than it should, and is always
good to use during the spring
and autumn. Then the hair
needs a little—What do you call
itV—stimulant, and for this 1
would advise you to get a packet
of boranium and mix it with
some bay rum ; dab this into the
roots and it will not only stop
the fall, but make your hair
grow long and thick. Give your
hair a good brushing every; night, and that
will be all that you need do.
Blackheads Fly Away.
Instantaneous Remedy for Blackheads, Greasy Skin and Enlarged Pores.
A practically instantaneous remedy for
blackheads, greasy skins and enlarged pores,
recently discovered, is now coming into
general use in the boudoir. It is very
simple, harmless and pleasant. Drop a
sty mol tablet, obtained at the chemist’s, in
a tumblerful of hot water. After the effer-
vescence has subsided bathe the face in the
liquid, using a small sponge or soft cloth.
In a few minutes dry the face and the offen-
sive blackheads will come right off on the
towel. Also the large oily pores immedi-
ately close up and efface themselves natu-
rally. _ The greasiness disappears and the
skin is left smooth, soft and cool. This
simple treatment is then repeated a few
times at intervals of four or five days, to
ensure the permanence of the result.
Grey Hair—Home Remedy.
An old-fashioned Recipe restores Youthful Appearance.
There are plenty of reasons why grey
hair is not desirable, and plenty of reasons
why hair dyes should not be used. But, on
the other hand, there is no reason why you
should have grey hair if you do not want
it. To turn the hair back to a natural color
is really a very simple matter. One has
only to get from the chemist two ounces of
concentrate of tammalite and mix it with
three ounces of bay rum. Apply to the
hair with a small sponge for a few nights
and the greyness will gradually disappear.
This liquid is not sticky or greasy and does
not injure the hair in any way. It has been
used for generations with most satisfactory
results by those who have known the
formula.
To Kill Roots of Superfluous Hair.
The most Effective Formula ever Discovered
Women annoyed with disfiguring growths
of superfluous hair wish to know not merely
how to temporarily remove the hair, but how
to kill the hair roots permanently. For
this purpose pure powdered pheminol may be
applied directly to the objectionable hair
growth. The recommended treatment is
designed not only to instantly remove the
hair but also to actually kill the roots so that
the growth will not return. About an ounce
of pheminol, obtainable from the chemist,
should be sufficient.
Good News for Fat People.
Something New in Obesity Cures.
A London chemist says: “The latest
method of reducing obesity certainly is far
more pleasant and convenient than all pre-
vious methods. It consists merely in eating
clynol berries. The fat person who wants
to reduce without the usual rigid diet, exer-
cise, sweating baths, etc., now puts a few
of these little brown berries in his or her
pocket and eats three or four each day.”
Clynol berries not only eliminate fat from
the body, but also correct the tendency,
which is usually constitutional, to create
fatty matter. No discomfort whatever is
caused by their action ; in fact, except for
the loss of superfluous fat and the feeling
of “fitness” so created, you would not be
aware that these little berries were doing
their work.
Local inquiry shows that clynol berries are
not very well known in this country, but
the demand is increasing: daily, and any
chemist can quickly procure them if specially
requested to do so.

ESTABLISHED OVER 30 YEARS.
“We have won for ourselves the reputation
of a white-livered people,” laments ex-
President Teddy Roosevelt. The Bulletin
won’t go so far as to say the American liver
is white, exactly—it’s just a neutral tint.
Three soldier brothers named Renton,
from near Drouin (Vic.), just about get
into the record class. The baby, aged 18, is
6ft. long and weighs 12 stone. One of his
brothers can’t* stand erect under 6ft. 4in..
and carries 15 stone around with him. The
eldest brother towers 6ft. 6in. A few more
Rentons would make the Kaiser think the
famous Guard of Frederick of Prussia had
risen from the dead and turned traitor to
lvultur.
Adolf Leselien, Adelaide’s old gymnastic
instructor who went out the other day at
80, belonged to a family whose name was
originally Leszczynski, which is Polish: the
Germanised form, was adopted when the
family settled in Holstein, then under Danish
rule. Young Leselien left for South Aus. at
21, and presently took up teaching. In
conjunction with the school he ran a gymna-
sium, and from that grew what for years
was the best-known physical-culture school
in Australia. The late Samuel Way, Chief
Justice, was one of his early pupils. Once
on a time he made a quaint effort to revive
cross-bow contests, and ran them for a while
near the old Maid and Magpie. But he let
the side-line die quietly when it had had its
season.
“Jack” Cochran, of N.S.W. Assembly, who
was recently told to stand down for the rest
of the session, has been one of the big disap-
pointments in the Labor ranks. When he
secured the selection for Darling Harbor he
enjoyed an amazing popularity, and was
regarded by unionists as a man of special
ability. His claims for inclusion in the
first Labor Government, although he was
then a new member, were reckoned to be con-
siderable, and many optimists tipped him
for the job of Minister for Labor. But he
has drifted until he is content to be, appar-
ently, just “the member for The Rocks,” in
which capacity he asks indignant questions
as to why a tenant in the resumed area who
owes 79 weeks’ rent has been given notice
to quit, instead of having the rent reduced?
“N. or M.”: I have found the oldest
memorial window on record. It adorns All
Saints’ Anglican Church, Hobart. The two
lights represent John the Baptist and James
respectively, with a broad stone mullion
between. At the bottom of the left light
may be read :
S. John Baptist
late of Simon Springs
Erected by his widow
Captious people skip the mullion and read
right across ; but it isn’t half as interesting
that way.
“Fax”: The shabby genteel woman who
has looked on better days must renounce her
old claim to have been “brought up as a
lady.” She was bounced out of it the other
Monday by 200 female unionists in the
Melbourne Eight Hours procession. Those
smart young daughters of Labor, wearing
white silk blouses with dark skirts, blue ties
and blue-banded white hats, followed a
bannerette inscribed “Pioneer Lady
Marchers, 1915.” There’s nothing to be
said against an assertion of lady-likeness—
except that it isn’t ladylike. Cultured
women nowadays speak of themselves as
such, and even the leisured sisters who have
political axes to grind are content to be a
'Women’s National League. They have prac-
tically swapped terms with ' the Ladv
Marchers.
“D.E.”: Westralian Governments, past
and present, have been more than considerate
to the capitalist who takes up land for
speculative purposes. In the. north-east
beyond Laverton there is an enormous tract
of fine sheep and cattle country. Feed is
plentiful and abundant, and good stock water
can be got by sinking 50ft. to 80ft. The
only drawback is the dingo, which could
easily be coped with. Yet this land is almost
unused; it is largely held by fatmen who
conform to no regulations as to stocking and
fencing, and who pay only a nominal rent
in the hope that one day they will get out at
a profit. Meantime the small man with
honest intentions can’t make a start unless
he goes to places remote from the railway
terminus. Westralia has given too much
attention to the man who proposed to grow
wheat (and in many cases can’t) and much
too little to the small man who would grow
stock if he had a chance. In that respect
it could learn a lot from Queensland.
A new one has been tried on an Adelaide
police court. Accused was not, he declared
in great detail, intoxicated in the street.
While doing his work he had got sulphuric
acid into his system, and had to drink a
mixture of methylated spirit and whisky to
counteract the effects. You couldn’t call the
action of a medical remedy intoxication
surely? Indecent .conduct (as also charged)
—well, perhaps. But drunkenness—never !
Btill, a heartless Bench said “25 bob” in the
same old way.
The Melbourne undertaker’s man is asking
for a dress allowance of £26 a year Either
this or that his livery of grief shall be pro-
vided by the boss. Lately, by a Wages
LSoard decision, a few shillings a week were
added to the wages of these toilers.
Promptly the master-ghoul decided to get
even. His helpers had always provided their
own habiliments of gloom; now the boss
determined that, instead of allowing them
to P lc k up an old topper here and a not-too-
shabby flogger there, the outfit must hence-
forth be a new one to start with. Although
nobody worth mentioning, outside of Gov-
ernment House and John Madden’s flat,
wears the frock-and-topper nowadays, there
are still enough wearers to show the devas-
tating effect that constant use has on the
dreadful outfit. Wherefore 30 shillings a
week doesn’t seem an extravagant estimate
for a whole year s supply, to be used seven
days a week in all weathers, and on Mel-
bourne’s dusty, dirty roads at that. An
easy, economical way out of the difficulty
would _be for the State Ministry to bring
in a little bill prohibiting frill at funerals
Several thousands of Australia’s finest citi-
zens have lately been buried without it in a
far country; and they sleep as soundly as
the most splendidly-planted of those buried
here. Besides, this is the time for economy *
isn’t it?


A Remarkable Offer!
“It is almost in the nature of a fine contri-
bution to the War Funds” was the remark
passed the other day by a friendly critic.
The offer is as follows: —lf desired, we
will grant to every purchaser of our
Pianos on time payment, a Life Policy,
which provides that if the hirer, with intent
to purchase, should die before the completion
of payments, the Piano is to become the
property of the person named in the Policy
—absolutely without further payment. Thus
the husband or son meets with a fatal accident,
or suddenly dies, leaving behind a Beale Piano
which is being purchased on instalment plan,
and his widow keeps the Piano without paying
any further instalment; a souvenir of her
husband’s wise forethought and of the generos-
ity of Beale & Co.
It will further enhance the value of your Beale Piano to remem-
ber that it is not made in Germany, where the piano-makers
are fighting against us, but in Australia, by your own com-
rades and under the humane conditions of Australian labour.
You can possess a Beale Piano to-day. If you do not care
to pay cash, we can arrange the easiest terms to suit you,
and will make a liberal allowance for the old instrument, if
' -
you have one.
Mr. OCTAVIUS C. BEALE, Founder and present Controlling Director
of this business, was born at Mt. Mellick, Queen’s County, Ireland,
of an old Irish family. The directors and managers throughout the
business are all descendents of British families for many generations.
There are no Germans employed in the business, and no supplies are
obtained from Germany.

IMPORTANT
Look to Your Teeth.
There are no teeth like those furnished by
Nature. For service, comfort and good
health artificial teeth cannot be com-
pared with a natural set.
Sensible people will accordingly try to pre-
serve their teeth as long as possible by
keeping them clean. Cold water and soap
and water are useful, but are somewhat
ineffectual. Rough powders, such as cuttle-
fish, crabseyes, crude chalks and charcoal
are too severe on the enamel.
EUDENTA TOOTH PASTE is scientifi-
cally prepared to clean the teeth, sweeten
the breath, harden the gums and disinfect
the mouth (thus preventing decay).
EUDENTA is therefore regarded as “the
perfection of dental luxuries.” 1/-tube every-
where. Post free 1/3. Free sample posted.

“Yigilans” slices a piece from the roof of
Australia: —
Bill Lawrie, who has sold out his stations in the
Northern Territory to Yestey Bros., is about as
tough a man as one would meet in any country in
the world. For 37 years he has roughed it in the
open on the Adelaide, Alligator and Roper rivers,
with occasional trips to the East and South. For
nearly three decades he has hunted buffalo and
cattle and slept on the ground, and now, though over
70, looks as fit as Verbrugghen’s violin. The pro-
perties acquired by Vestey Bros, comprise the
Adelaide River, most of the Alligator and some of the
Roper. This country is in close proximity to Dar-
win, and is the best portion of Arnheim Land. Here
are to be found buffalo, Brahmin cattle, Timor ponies
and brumbies running wild, besides goats and pigs.
The swamp lands have been likened to Saigon, and
pronounced suitable for rice-growing. There are
strips of jungle containing good soil; but large
tracts of open downs would make excellent paddy-
fields with systematic drainage. Cotton grows wild
all over the country. It would appear that Yestey
Bros, and the Union Co. (practically the same Con-
cern) will soon hold a mortgage on the Territory.
This crowd, in conjunction with Bovril Ltd., holds
the Ord, Margaret and Sturt rivers (Westralia) ;
nearly all the Victoria River; Hodgson Downs and
the Elsey, on the Roper; the Alligator and Adel-
aide country ; and portion of the Katherine River—an
enormous tract of country. Apparently under the
Gilruth regime monopoly rules the roost. Vestey
Bros, will not develop the North. The meatworks
will no doubt give a little employment, but the
small landholder whom the country most needs will
not get a look-in. Lawrie says farming is a hopeless
proposition, as the soil is mostly poor; but all cattle-
men say that.

IN BANANA LAND
“Bill” : Ipswich is the highest-rated muni-
cipality in Queensland, local ratepayers being
hit just as heavily as the Act permits. Now
the city has decided that in future footpaths
must be swept with a hair broom instead
of the usual millet brush, because someone
has discovered that millet wears out footpaths
too rapidly. 1 suppose we’ll have to do it.
If the Sphinx had been dusted every morn-
ing from the time it was unveiled or
“opened.” there would only be about a pound
of it left by now. And the footpaths of
Ipswich are just as important as any monu-
ment.
“D.” : Dan Jones, of Brisbane, who swells
like a gobbler with indignation at the leth-
argy of officialdom when you mention cotton
in Queensland, has found some time to look
into the fruit-fly’s habits. He has proved
that eitronella aided by sticky paper is a
lure to the destroyer. The idea no doubt
was got by the attraction which ordinary
kerosene oil has for the same pest. They
simply plunge into the liquid. But Daniel
says eitronella is ever so much more sudden.
1 1 might be worth trying this for sheep
flies. Of all the insect pests in Australia this
one. has- worked most destruction, though
during the past couple of years he seems to
have gone somewhere on leave.
The Queensland Bar is licking its lips.
The Government lately established an Insur-
ance Department and aimed at giving it a
monopoly of. the accident business. There
was the usual conflict between the two
Houses, with the result that the measure iu
one place seems to give private offices the
right to go ahead and in another tells em-
ployers That they must turn into the public
shop. Goodwyn, who left the Ocean office
and became associated with Earle Herman,
has been appointed manager of the State
Department; and he went ahead on the
-assumption that he was the only pebble on
the beach. The other offices thereupon took
advice ; and now the finest lot of Costs the
Queensland Bar has known for years is
being got ready for the banquet. The whole
trouble arises out of the absurd system of
having one House which has authority from
the people to say “Yes,” and another House
which, though it doesn’t represent anybody,
has the right to say “No.” Either House
could probably produce a decent measure;
but when the two snarl over one bone the
result is just about as satisfactory as a ser-
mon would be if the Devil preached it from
notes supplied by the prophet Jeremiah.
"Itan”: If the millions of acres of first-
class agricultural land in the Taroom dis-
trict do not soon get a railway to make their
development possible, the whole area will go
over to the prickly-pear curse’s account.
Indeed, the porcupine is already sticking out
that way and moving forward at high speed,
per emus and cattle. This alarming pest
is really an Empire problem, since there will
soon be great demand for land by men now
in the armies. Apparently our own officials
and politicians are too busy with other mat-
ters to get solidly to grips with the soil-
devouring devil. One would think that the
loss Of 30 million acres would spur some sort
of action into these dead horses to save clean
country. Apparently they’re so dead that
nothing will spur them.
Another seandal is being stirred up in
Brisbane. In despair of getting justice any
other way the workers at the Blind Institute
lately paid their fees to qualify for member-
ship. and this year rolled up to the annual
meeting. They censured the committee and
called on the Government to intervene, and
to show their determination re-elected the
president, who had been discharged pre-
viously by the management. Their earn-
ings, they allege, run to about 28s. per
week, and they tell such a story of petty
tyranny that the Government can’t well
help taking notice.
John Gill, carrier of Cuunamulla. is
entered for the casual Australian competi-
tion. John married Minnie in 1899, and they
lived happily out West for 16 years, being
blessed meanwhile with a family of five. Last
New Year’s Day—or it may have been a
twelve-month earlier, Alinnie decided to begin
life afresh, so she strolled over to another
carrier’s house, 200 yards away, and made
a new start as Charlie Goulding’s missus.
John felt slighted, but stayed at home with
the children. He says he saw Alinnie working
about Goulding’s place often, yet he never
threw it in her face. But no man is
master of Himself, and John, in a fit of
impatience, asked Pope Cooper. C.J., for a
decree nisi. His surprised Honor handed
it over with a bewildered look aud some
vague remarks about something or other.
A LAST BARGAIN.
FIRST SHIPWRECKED PASSENGER: “ There's no hope. We shall sink in a moment!”
THE OTHER ONE: “ Here , vait a minute, qrick. I'll sell yer me vatch ’alf-price — you'll
never yet another chance.”
The sugar cocky cannot get his
Tommy Tanna or his Ah Cow, so he is
taking on foreigners and turning down the
Australian or English Jimbill. At Innisfail
whole gangs have been engaged to replace
the men who have done the cutting since
Tanna’s repatriation. The thing is such a
scandal that even the local recruiting com-
mittee is objecting; for it points out that
men who had said “Yes” are now declining
to go to the Front because of the local bosses
freezing on to foreigners. These men say
that when they return they will find fellows
who can’t speak seven words of English
permanently installed, and they will have to
whistle for a job themselves. The A.W.U.
committee is putting in a kick, and the
recruiting committee intends to see what
Senator Pearce has to say about it. No one
objects to these foreigners getting a job, but
the local Billjim can't stand a policy of
preference.
Tom Glassey, first “official” Labor man in
Australia, and first leader of the Queensland
Labor party, nowadays takes a hand in help-
ing Aberdare Colliery to pay dividends.
The Government proposes to consider
the Alt. Coolon mineral field, discov-
ered two years ago, but thus far undeveloped.
Only scratching has been done, but there are
vague rumors about samples of coarse gold
being taken into Charters Towers. I In-
field is 150 miles from Bowen. Clermont and
Ravenswood. It is said that the mineral belt
is pretty extensive, and that theie aie wol
fram shows attracting attention. Queens-
land badly wants a new mining held. Char-
ters Towers is very ill, and Gympie is giving
up quartz crushing for dairying. It is
astonishing with what tenacity people hang
on to a mining field with a past. Even the
Government departments cannot get out of
the habit of regarding a field as a perma-
nency. though the undertaker may be ham-
mering at. the door. The Agricultural De-
partment is watching the good old Towers
field starve to death without making the
slightest effort to see if the wide stretches of
second-class country around it cannot be
put to profitable use. Surely the land is
fit for something? The trouble is that the
Agricultural Department is about the most
unenterprising institution on the face of
God's earth.
‘‘Globe”: The Yankee talks a lot about
his long-distance ’phones; for a change,
listen to us. Here’s a telegraph-line in Aus-
tralia which takes beating. At night Bris-
bane sends press to Cairns. Only 1000-odd
miles if. you can get straight through cer-
tainly ; but there are times you can’t. One
night the usual storm had burst the coastal
line in several places, so we rigged lines this
way. Mt. Surprise, about 200 miles west of
Cairns, joined us to the Townsville line.
From Townsville to Cloncurry was the next
section, a mere SOO miles or so, due west.
Then south to Tambo, Muttaburra and
Roma. The rest was easy, through Too-
woomba to Brisbane. That made about
3000 the lot. and through rougher country
than the U.S.A. knows, a good part being
tropical jungle. The line worked well, and
the people read the news the next morning
with no knowledge of the remarkable track
it came along.
Billy Hughes.

The races to-day drew a big crowd. The
King’s local agent spent most of the after-
noon in the members’ stand, but Capt. and
Mrs. Cosens, the latter in peach-colored

“Carmen,” flickered by J. C. Williamson

AUSTRALIA JANE.
Lancelot Guy Rawson, for the last 16 or
17 years Crown Lands Ranger at Rockhamp-
ton, is in camp at Enoggera. He is a son
of the late Lance Rawson, connected with
the pioneering days of Bananaland as one of
the Rawson brothers, who owned The Hol-
low, at Port Alaekay. They were sons of
Chas. Stansfield Rawson, formerly in the
East India Co.’s service, and, incidentally,
cousins of Governor Rawson (N.S.W.).
The mother was a Collinson —a family full
of admirals and generals. A large number
of relatives, on both sides, have gone to the
Front, and several have been killed. Another,
son of Charlie Rawson, one of the Hollow
brothers, is in camp in Sydney. Altogether,
old Viking Rawson’s relatives make a good
showing in this struggle.
“Barcoo” : Jimmy Ahfoo has had a
funeral. Fifteen years ago Jimmy was in-
contestably the best-known man in Queens-
land. not excepting politicians. Miner at
Croydon, storekeeper in Springsure, and pub-
lican at every stopping place on the Central
lino as it poked its way to Longreach, wag
and “white” man. no one had anything but
good to say of him. Born in Canton Jj
years ago, and resident in this State for do
years, lie reared a family of II stalwart
Queenslanders. They were mostly musicians:
at Longreach there was a string orchestra
composed of eight members of the family
At that time the live business head of the
firm was the eldest daughter, a vocalw
quite out of the ordinary. At present unit
one runs a large and successful picture hi-
euit” in West Queensland.
