Willis Little, an M.L.C. who represents
the stouter section of the North-East Pro-
vince, has decided that Benalla is too far
from the city for a sick man, and has re-
signed. He was elected a dozen years ago
and shared the representation of his province
with Otto Sachse, now Chairman of Com-
mittees.
G.-G. Ferguson is visiting Tassy shortly
to give the Speck’s hydro-electric enterprise
a flying start. The event comes off on May
6. and by that time manager Butters reckons
he will be able to deliver power at Hobart
to all and sundry. His main trouble just
now is that he has so many people after
power that he reckons he may have to quad-
ruple his 9000 h.p. installation right away.
South Australia’s Mines Department re-
ports that the State had its record produc-
tion of minerals in 1915. Considering that
S. Aus., in all its expanse, cannot find true
coal, or a regular field of either gold or
silver, it did well to just top the million
pounds sterling last year. Copper has
always been the State’s strong point, and
with a big war demand at war-time prices,
the red metal accounted for well over half
the total, with ironstone-flux and ore (for
the Broken Hill Prop.) making over another
quarter. Salt —which is hardly “mined,”
being scraped off the surface of the dry
lakes —was a bad third with £BO,OOO, and
everything else was an also-ran. Gold
totalled under £26,000. The best S.A. has
ever done in that line was £76,000. And yet
there is the color everywhere. A man
can knock out tucker in almost any of the
gullies close to Adelaide; but the big deposit,
if ever found, seems likely to be up toward
the centre of the continent. Meanwhile,
copper is being produced to a value un-
equalled since the boom of 10 years ago and
the bigger one of half a century back.
“Try Fluke’s” story of Maoriland’s latest
“prophet” :
The trouble of the long-haired Rua, who has just
been carried off to quod after a shooting perform-
ance in M.L., was that he was no soldier—no second
Te Kooti. In the wild bush country of the
Urewera, where the acres mostly run up to the
moon, and the trails twist up the narrow beds of
rocky creeks like the tracks of a bolting politician,
an army corps could be ambuscaded. I have ridden
those trails and trudged the ranges with some of the
bare-legged old Hauhaus who live there, and can
only marvel at the extraordinary good luck that
attended the M.L. constabulary’s expedition. The
police didn’t worry about strategy or flanking parties
or rear attacks; they simply floundered in at the
front door, found the ridiculous medicine-man
having his curly tresses combed, and in the confused
gun-play that followed shot two of his tribe dead,
while constable O’Hara woodened out another wid
a stick. Rua halted between two minds too long.
Any other brown brother with a military eye could
have cut up the force pretty well anywhere on the
single-file bushtrack through the canons and up the
break-neck ridges. Now the M.L. citizen is wonder-
ing what the bill will be, and whether it wouldn’t
have been cheaper to have sent a plain John Hop
to get the runaway singlehanded instead of despatch-
ing ammunition-waggons and a caravan of pack-
horses and a restaurant and 60 brawny Johns on a
picnic. The Rua comedy started with an ordinary
tinpot police-court case. Justice Minister Herdman’s
quakings made it a costly imitation of a South
American insurrection.
“Cockaigne”: No, Henry Chaplin, British
M.P., fervent Protectionist and advocate of
close land settlement, who has just acquired
a peerage, is not as well-known hereabouts
as Cholly ; but if there had been any flicker-
onerators in the long ago they might have
got Henry in some extra-good films. In ’64 he
was engaged to marry Flo Paget, a daughter
of Lord Anglesey. On the day before
the morn on which they were to say “I will”
Henry drove Flo to the door of Marshall
and Snelgrove’s big London drapery, where
she said she wanted to go a-shopping. She
went inside, there met the Marquis of Has-
tings, scuttled away through a back door
and married him. The laugh w r as up to
Hastings. Three years later the favorite
for the Derbs 7 was the Marquis of Hastings’s
Earl, backed by him to win £lOO.OOO. Her-
mit, owmed by Henry Chaplin, was at 100 to
one, and his trainer. Machell, brought the
animal on to the course with its coat brushed
the wrong way. The race was run in a
snowstorm and Hermit won. Hastings
sadly patted the winner’s neck and then
walked slowly away—ruined, for his extrava-
gances had been gross. He died soon after.
Chaplin had the laugh that time. The
widowed Flo afterwards married Sir George
C-hetwynd, and in later years both were very
friendly with the man she jilted. Chaplin
did not marry till ’76, when he captured
Florence Leveson-Gower, daughter of the
wealthy Duke of Sutherland. She lived only
five years. As a young man Chaplin was
very rich, but latterly depreciation of his
Lincolnshire estates had made him what
Society calls Poor. Which means that he
isn’t bloated.
“Bulldozer’s” libellous story :
Late Chief Justice Way hadn’t an ounce of humor
in his dapper little body, but he caused more laughs
in court than any other S. Aus. Judge. Mostly, of
course, the laughs suffered a foul death, being stifled
at birth, owing to the joke being quite unintentional.
But many a time on the rising of the Court the tale
would be re-told in the robbing (I mean robing)
room, and then the Bar would let itself go. On one
occasion a K.C. (now harpooned by the Master
Fisherman) was steering the little Chief through a
beautifully-technical argument relating to contingent
remainders, when the Associate whispered to 'Sonor
that someone on the telephone wanted to know what
he had done in reference to a certain matter. From
the little man’s expression it was evident that
nothing had been done, and he was clearly puzzled
what to say in reply. He got out of his seat and
walked up and down the dais behind it pondering
deeply. The argument on contingent remainders
began to falter, and finally petered out. The un-
canny silence aroused the little Chief, and, looking
round, he said encouragingly: “Go on, Mr. Hammer-
head ; I am following you.” Mr. Hammerhead
bogged in again, his dorsal fin barely visible, and
the Judge immediately went on circuit again. At
length, after about five minutes’ perambulation, the
little Chief, with a snap of resolution, rapped out
to his Associate “Just ring off!” and resumed his
seat. Not a muscle on the collective face of the
brazen Bar moved, but the writer (who was collecting
data for his famous work, “The Encyclopaedia of Deep
Sea Denizens”) beat hastily for the open sea.

BUSINESS AS USUAL.
The Management of the “Australia”
Hotel are gratified to state that, not-
withstanding the upset caused by the
recent fire they are able to announce
“business as usual.” The service in
the Grand Dining Room, on the
Balcony, and in the Winter Garden
is being conducted in the same style
that has made the “Australia” Hotel
so popular with its patrons, while the
arrangements for Private Dinners and
Suppers are working with their accus-
tomed satisfaction.
Throughout the holiday season
arrangements for Afternoon Teas will,
as usual, be carried out in the Winter
Garden, which has been running con-
tinuously throughout.
J. URE SMITH, Manager.

BOOTS have come back again and no mistake.
But such dreams of boots, up-to-date,
dainty, delightful, fit accompaniment to the
flounced taffeta frocks of the day, which
combine smart modernity with old-world charm.
Paris and London both are now tripping to work
and play in short-fronted, high-heeled, suede-
topped boots like the Bostock style here illus-
It is a smart little boot, there is no doubt
about that, and every bit as comfortable as it is
smart. The beautifully fitting last on which it is
made sees to that, and lots of women, who have
never imagined they could wear a short-fronted
style, will be amazed to find how comfortable it
is, how jolly it makes their feet look.
Then for those who want something different,
who prefer shoes perhaps, there is a fine range of
styles to select from, all as up-to-date and charm-
ing as this model, and all safe to wear well and
feel as peculiarly one’s own as if they had been
made to measure.

The Edison Diamond
Disc Phonograph
is different and superior
to any other talking machine.
In nearly every musical sound —though heard as one note a
combination of several notes is really there. The different
relative numbers and intensities of this combination of notes
make the difference in Tone Quality. The lowest tone is called
the fundamental tone; the softer and sweeter tones are called the
overtones. If the overtones are absent from a note, the resulting
sound is thin and strident. For the first time in the history of
the Talking Machine the overtones can be recorded —and
accurately reproduced on the Edison Diamond Disc Phonograph.
The records are of an entirely new material —manufactured under
a secret process from a chemical compound of such great density
and hardness that the most minute sound-wave can be engraved
upon it, and no amount of wear will efface or distort it.
There are numerous factors which go to make the Edison Diamond
Disc the finest musical instrument the world knows; by hearing
it you can prove for yourself that it is a wonderful instrument
the only talking machine capable of pleasing the most exacting
music-lovers.
Illustrated catalog of all information post free on request.

The Inky Way.
J. J. Hall, till recently head reporter of
the Bendigo Advertiser, has taken on a
harder job as sec. of the newly-mooted Vic.
Farmers’ Union. Still, he has moved a bit
amongst the class he seeks to serve, so pro-
bably has sized the problem he is up
against—the prickly end of it being the
collection of the ten bob a year subscription.
* * * *
It is a fearful thing to try to be humorous
in front of a Police Court bench. The other
day The Bystander, a London illustrated
weekly, was “up” for publishing a cartoon
entitled “Reported Missing,” which depicted
a private soldier, wearing a sun-helmet, lying
under a tree, hugging a bottle of rum. The
prosecution claimed that the cartoon was a
gross libel on the forces, and likely to pre-
judice discipline, especially as it was drawn
by an officer. Furthermore, it tended to
prejudice recruiting. An indignant beak
fined the publishers £lOO, the editou£so, and
Lieut. C. G. Bernard, who drew the picture,
£25. Next time a London beak fines a sfel-
dier for being drunk, or even suggests that
a soldier can be drunk, this paper urges
The Bystander to call around and try to
collect £175 from it for grossly libelling the
King’s forces.
* * * *
Rifleman E. 11. Thompson, present address
the high seas; is a promising young Dunedin
(M.L.) artist, and publisher of The
Sketciier. He had produced five numbers
of his periodical with much success, and was
in the act of preparing No. 6, when he
decided to close the den and call on the Hun
to subscribe to a hastily-published bullet.
There’s going to be trouble for Sydney
Telegraph’s linotype presently. It may
play tricks on most people with impunity,
but when it talks of “the 16 pudges on the
N. S. Wales bench” it is liable to make ill-
feeling where ill-feeling really matters.
* * * *
Nelson (M.L.) Evening Mail celebrated
its jubilee the other day, having been
founded in 1866 by the late R. Lucas, father
of the present owner. The special issue to
mark the occasion was of more than usual
interest by reason of its accounts of the
famous Wairau massacre and the notorious
Maungatapu murders, which were big inci-
dents in the early days of Nelson. The
Mail, which began life as a humble sheet
produced on a hand press, is now a prosper-
ous and bulky journal that drops, from the
most modern machines. One unique point
about it is that the whole of its staff, with
the solitary exception of the linotype
engineer, started life on the paper as a boy.
* * * *
That keen-eyed humorist Robert Brothers
was concerned in a recent rescue from
drowning at Durban, S’Africa. He was
taking a Saturday-afternoon stroll with his
12-year-old son when they saw three girl-
bathers floundering in the Illovo River. One
of them was floating face downwards when
Brothers plunged in to help. But .he. isn’t
as good at swimming as he is at spinning a
yarn, and Australian literature was going
under when young Brothers swam into the
plot and assisted everybody to a happy
ending. # ,
“Norbell”: J. Dobbs, of the Morning
Bulletin, Rockhampton, has put .up a
record of half a century at the case with the
one employer, which is probably unbeaten on
the Australian Inky Way.
* * * *
From the Sydney report of a court-mar-
tial : —•
Sergeant Granter took it for granted that the grant
had been granted.
Well, grant that Granter expected the grant
to be granted, can It be granted that Granter
was entitled to anticipate the grant? That
is the question before the sub-editor.
“Itan”: The signed-article foolishness
reached the limit of absurdity during a recent
Queensland by-election campaign. One of
the principal reasons for its adoption was
that the appending of a signature to a para-
graph would ensure accuracy and truth. As
a matter of fact, there was never more wilful
lying at any election than at the one just
fought in Fortitude Valley, The responsi-
bility in some cases has been evaded by the
whole staff signing the articles; and in the
case of the Courier the editor’s name was
bracketed with the real writer’s, though he
at the time was out of the State. Not that
that mattered, of course. He no doubt left
his instructions. And probably they , didn’t
represent his personal views either,-but were
merely as near his views as the views of
his bosses would let him get.
THE SPECTATOR.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
With puckered brow I stand aside
And watch the passing show.
While strong-lipped knights, in lusty pride,
Into the lists all eager ride,
To answer blow with blow,
At Life’s fierce tournament I blink,
And smudge it o’er with printer's ink.
Men’s yearnings, strivings, loves and hates
But furnish me with pars.
Perchance a fighter’s wounded, then
Straightway I rush with fountain-pen
To open up his scars;
And with an “X” I seek to show
The place where he received the blow.
Though men may fight and women love,
And kingdoms rise and fall,
And cities rust, and nations die,
In these affairs no part have I
Although I write of all.
While others in the whirlpool fight
And do —I merely sit and write !
My soul I bartered for a “nose,”
My blood is turned to ink,
And with a cold, myopic eye
I watch life’s stream go surging by—•
And thus upon the brink
I’ll crouch until the tomb is ripe,
And help supply the nation’s tripe!
VICTORIA.
HARRISON O.

In Exile.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A RIBAND of silver, the river spills
Between the willows that line its
banks;
The poplars stretch to the bosomy hills
In formal rows, and my tongue gives
thanks
For the splendid peace of it all. . . .and yet
The exile within me cannot forget!
Too primly millinered, too precise
The landscape seems ; and a sullen pain
Grapples my heart in an occult vise,
Rouses a yeasty fear in my brain
Lest the Fates, untoward, elect that I
See not my homeland before I die.
Though surely the mood will pass, I know,
Yet just as surely will it recur;
Wisdom cajoles me, “Should this be so,
So let it be and make no demur!”
But Wisdom and I we could never agree ;
Her slattern dugs have no milk for me.
\nd wistful and lonely and ill at ease
I stroll down the garden to where I know
(Ah, well I know them, these dear old
trees!)
A couple of fine old Blue Gums grow ;
p m ay be a whimsey of my own mind,
But to me these Blue Gums are mately kind.
I stroke their butts and I kiss their leaves
And I speak to them softly, so none can
hear,
We tell each other how exile grieves,
And somehow it seems less hard to bear;
They’re not the same as at home, but yet
I know, like me, that they don’t forget.
MAOKILAND.
BERNARD INGLEBY.
The Renegade of K3.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
THE aerial-alarm gong boomed out im-
peratively from the observation
tower.
The waiting Prussian lieutenant snatched
the hastily-written message from the field-
telephonist and rushed across to the mess-
tent of the K 3 Flying Corps.
“Five, Seven, Twelve and Sixteen —to
repel dirigibles,” he shouted, thrusting his
head into the opening.
Fifty yards away four armored Etrich
monoplanes were being hurriedly wheeled
around in readiness for the start. Three
minutes later Holbach, Graf, Yon Schenk
and Vassmer were aboard their machines,
strapped in and facing the easterly breeze.
Captain Euler was finishing his final
order: “....and at any cost they must not
touch the magazines.”
The four monoplanes with a space of
forty feet between them rose into the air in
a line. As they passed over No. 4 Aerial
Defence Battery, with its eight test-tubes
of death pointing skyward, Yassmer, who
was the least experienced of the four, shivered
involuntarily; for he remembered the bleed-
ing torso that had fallen beside him from the
zenith, a memento of the unsuccessful
French raid of the previous day. After re-
leasing the trailing antennae of his wireless
he concentrated his attention upon their
objective. Two Lebaudy dirigibles were
approaching from the south-east, about half
a mile from each other, and quite four thou-
sand feet up from the plain.
Vassmer's wireless receiver throbbed into
activity. “Extend,” came the order. “Five,
Twelve —right. Seven, Sixteen —left. Keep
out of line of fire until signalled.”
Vassmer swung his machine over to the
left, with Graf close behind him, both rising
steadily. An incendiary shell from No. 4
Battery burst over the nearer Lebaudy like
a splash of blazing oil, but was not effective.
Then both airships opened fire from their
forward turrets.
Six times Yassmer felt the air-furrows of
passing shells, and he glanced swiftly across
to the right. No. 5 monoplane suddenly
sagged in the middle; then two halves flut-
tered down in a three-thousand-feet drop.
The next instant Vassmer saw a blinding
flash out of the corner of his eye, and on
looking backward caught a glimpse of Graf’s
machine whirling earthward in fragments
like a shattered match-hox.
“Two!” he grunted, and clenched his
teeth.
“Twelve and Sixteen,” buzzed the wireless
—“attack!”
He swept around in a curve to the left
and banked-over in the turn so that he was
moving parallel to the nearer dirigible, but
two hundred feet overhead. A ribbon of
flame shot upward from the Maxim that was
mounted on the airship’s upper sheathing,
and the steel base of No. Sixteen’s cockpit
jerked and rattled violently. The airman
opened his bomb-release, dropped four Zol-
emnite bombs, and soared upwards.
A crumpled mass of metal sheathing tore
op past him like a flying rag, and his mono-
plane was almost overturned by the dis-
placed air. The airman stared over the side.
The dirigible was ablaze from amidships to
stern and sinking rapidly.
Three-quarters of a mile away to the west
Von Schenk was hovering above the other
french airship. A charge of grapeshot
ripped his main planes into shreds, and the
disabled monoplane—with its hundredweight
load of Zolemnite bombs —crashed down
upon the roof of the Lebaudy.
vassmer, hurrying westward to Yon
Schenk’s _ assistance, nearly lost control of
his machine—the explosion was so appalling
that he thought the world had been split by
a monstrous fireball.
He was abruptly awakened by the re-
newed buzzing of his wireless: “Sixteen, if
uninjured, examine coastline and report.”
He swerved to the east and rose high
above the crest of Needle-top Hill. As he
gazed seaward he saw that an aero-aquatic
duel was in progress not more than a mile
away. A large British seaplane was circling
at a height of fifteen hundred feet above a
submarine, whose conical steel battery stood
out of the water like a small grey pyramid.
As Yassmer approached, with his quick-firer
ready, the invisible loopholes encircling the
armored cone ejected spurts of fire. The
tail of the seaplane disappeared in a cloud
of yellow smoke, and the next moment the
fore portion crumpled up and fell. A great
blaze splashed outward as the head struck
the sea, and after the fumes had eddied up
from the surface the submarine headed
northward and dived.
Yassmer followed the coastline eastward
for eighteen miles, and after easing down to
half-speed commenced to call up the K 3 base.
He transmitted HQ for fully four minutes
before receiving any reply.
“English seaplane sunk by submarine,” he
signalled. “Coastline clear. No ships in
sight. Am twenty miles east from Needle-
Top. Your signals weak.”
“Station destroyed by shell,” came the
answer. “Am using portable set. Order.
French right flank advancing near canal.
Reconnoitre and return immediate.”
The airman acknowledged the order and
turned inland over the hills.
As he approached the extreme right of
the battle-front a dull rumbling commenced
to soak through the helmet into his ears.
Away over to the south-east he could see
floating masses of smoke rising high into
the air. He pulled out the frame of the
skeleton map from its rack and clasped it
over his route-chart, ready for working on.
The orderly firing of his motor became
suddenly erratic, and a disquieting sugges-
tion thrust itself upon him. He plumbed
his oil-tank with feverish haste ; then, with
a curse, began a compulsory volplane. He
quickly determined to land on the flat top of
a hill that lay to the right. He accomplished
the landing without mishap, dismounted, and,
after unbolting his small quick-firer and
standing it on the ground alongside his
machine, awaited developments.
On the plain below he could see the gleam-
ing, pencil like guns, whose steel belchings
could rend open a granite cliff. There they
stood, breathing smoke and erupting sudden
death like mechanical volcanoes.
Over to the north-east and moving in ex-
tended formation two French and one Brit-
ish regiment were converging upon the inter-
section of two roads. The airman watched
with a feeling of fascination.
All at once, and almost in the middle of
the British regiment, there appeared a long,
wavering flame that lengthened until the
approaching body of men was bisected by a
river of fire a quarter of a mile long—an oil-
mine, the trench for which had been dug
during the previous week. Into the divided
host the German battery projected a steel
and leaden storm from a background of
blazing spurts and eddying vapors.
Half a mile away, and raising great clouds
of yellow dust, an ammunition transport was
hurrying along to the firing-line.
As Vassmer was observing its rapid ap-
proach along the recently-constructed road a
black shadow flicked over the hill from the
rear, and a British Sopwith biplane swept
downward and outward over the plain.
A blue flash spat backward from the cock-
pit, and a mighty hammer seemed to strike
the wireless receiver that was clasped to his
left ear. For a few seconds he was deafened
by the influx of sounds from around him, the
hideous thunder of bursting shrapnel and
the crackling roar of machine-guns.
In a state of maddening impotence he
noticed that the vulturine flight of the Sop-
with was directed towards the motor-lorries,
and he wrenched the smashed wireless hel-
met from his head and flung it into the
bushes. The biplane turned and then passed
high above the lorries, moving in the same
direction. A rapid succession of black
objects dropped out of it, like a broken
string of beads.
The earth gaped wide in a monstrous
vomiting of fire. Great flakes of steel
splashed upwards like spray into the riven
air. Then Vassmer’s eardrums were almost
shattered by an awful devastating roar—a
blast from the Pit —that surged against him
from the plain in a mighty wave.
At five hundred feet above the smoking
rent in the ground the biplane was rolling
and heaving. A shell hurtled over the
machine, like a flying worm. For a moment
the swaying fabric reared up vertically and
then stood still. Out of her head shot a
vivid gi*een flame, and an expanding cloud
of smoke enveloped the framework. When
the cloud had dissipated, the remains of the
Sopwith were scattered in and around a
gaping hole.
The oil-trench was still burning fiercely,
though in many places the flames had been
quenched by the tortured living and the
roasting dead. Through the gaps in this
fiendish barrier the British and French in-
fantry were swarming like ants between the
cracks in a door—to face a battery of
machine-guns that sprayed leaden death upon
them out of steel nozzles.
By degrees the airman became aware of
an extraordinary feeling of mental and
physical exhilaration. He fancied that his
body was expanding to a fearful size.
Suddenly he noticed that a monoplane was
approaching from the south-east, and for a
time was in doubt....
Leaning back against a rock, with his
quick-firer ready for action, he waited
anxiously. The nearing airman, when about
five hundred yards from the stationary
machine, became suspicious, and attempted
a dangerous turn. The quick-firer rattled
and shook venomously, and a helmeted head
that recklessly appeared over the side of the
cockpit abruptly jerked backward.
In less than thirty seconds an uncon-
trolled monoplane crashed head forward
against the hillside and turned over, with its
tail pointing skyward. Yassmer scrambled
down the slope, and, after dragging the dead
pilot out of his seat, unscrewed the auxiliary
oil-tank.
Ten minutes later No. 16 was gliding over
the plain, and Vassmer’s automatism was

developing into blood-lusting madness. The
thunderings from the earth seemed to fade
into vague murmurings, and he swept above
the battling thousands like a giant striding
over an army of ants.
A projectile tore upwards through the left
wing of his monoplane with a whining roar,
and burst far away overhead.
Vassmer laughed into the quaking air,
laughed like an unchained fiend in the ecstasy
of destruction.
“Shoot, you vermin—shoot!” he screamed
over the side.
Ills body quivered incessantly and seemed
to exude a fiery perspiration, and his head
felt like a red-hot globe. He could see the
slim, demure little tubes that stood on
three legs and squirted leaden drops, and he
yelled a greeting to them. He shouted with
fierce outbursts of diabolical merriment as
great furrows were ripped through the
charging masses, furrows that were lanes of
blood and twisting limbs.
Every shell that burst harmlessly in the
air enraged him.
“Lower ! Lower !” he roared, and thrust
one arm upward with splayed-out fingers as
if to grasp the firmament and smash it down
upon them. For he knew that they were
the spawn of vileness and futility. Huge
clouds of grey vapor billowed around him.
The air was sweet in his nostrils —sweet
with the perfume of streaming blood and
burning flesh. It was the incense of the
slaughter-house—incense rising to Vassmer
(he Omnipotent, Vassmer the Hod of Battles,
Vassmer the Sadist of Sudden Death.
He leaned over and flung his bombs broad-
cast, as a Titan might have hurled the light-
nings.
A shell slit the whirling vapors and struck
the tail of his monoplane. The air-dis-
placement of the bursting charge smote his
back and head like an avalanche, and
machine and man fell together.
Four hours later a bruised and exhausted
man lay sleeping on a camp-stretcher. A
file of men and an officer entered the tent.
The officer walked across to the sleeper and
kicked him.
“Up, you dog!” he shouted.
Five minutes afterwards, a dazed man,
with his back to a gorgeous sunset, stood
facing a firing-party. The last sound that
Vassmer heard in this world was the word
"Fire!”
* * * *
In the K 3 mess-tent that night the con-
versation was fitful and every face was
frowning.
“Yes,” said Von Alten to his brother,
“saw him myself. Fired on poor Korner
from the hilltop and brought him down.
Six of his bombs dropped on our Head-
quarters, and all the others killed our own
men. He got General Rotermund and Major
Stiebel with the first two, and Von Ritzenau
died in the ambulance. The old folly—a
damned amateur given a commission ! A
pup clad in a lion’s skin !”
The Bavarian Flight-Commander at the
head of the table stood erect with his glass
uplifted.
“My children,” he said solemnly, “to the
three who died like men.” Then he spat
behind him. “For Vassmer —who died like
a swine!”
HAROLD LINDSAY.
The Stolen God.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“T DON’T know how it is.” said the
second mate thoughtfully, “but I
never yet heard of a captain who got
on with his chief engineer. The Old Man
always seems to have an idea that the man
down below is a potterin’ passenger, and
the engineer generally suffers from the
hauntin’ suspicion that the Old Man is a
sunburnt old figurehead. They’re never more
than polite to one another, but each attends
to his own job, and between them the Brit-
ish mercantile marine makes big d vkiends
for the people who kill time round London.
I often wonder whither I’ll hate my -.-hief
when I’m old and gettin’ near the end of my
tether.”
lie looked thoughtfully across to where
the stevedores were shipping murderous-
looking slings of steel rails out of a rusted
tramp to the chatter and wheeze of a fussy
steam winch. “That’s rotten stuff to
handle —I’m glad I haven’t got to do it.
Have you a match V” I had.
Presently his pipe was going again. “I
once sailed with a pair of crusty old devils
who hated one another like H—l. The Old
Man had no particular vices—unless it was
a healthy craze for neatness ; but the engi-
neer had a mania for collecting any sort of
bird or animal or reptile he could get hold
of and selling it at a profit in the next port
they touched at. He always had some sort
of his rubbish on board, and as often as not
it was something that smelt or bit or stung,
or that took up valuable room. The Old Man
used to nearly go mad ; but the engineer was
some sort of relation of the owners, so the
Old Man wasn’t game to exercise the full
authority he had as master of the ship. We
were trading between the Indian ports at
that time, and, as you know, most of them
are places where you can get snakes and
things cheap. The old Chief thoroughly
enjoyed himself for a while, for, as well as
justifying his infernal instinct for messing
about with animated horrors, he was keep-
ing the Old Man hopping with rage, and
making lots of money. I believe that old
waster would have sold his grandmother to
a zoo if he had been offered a price for her.
Worst of it was that he gradually became
known as a buyer amongst the rag-tag and
bob-tail natives of the ports, and w r e were
kept busy chasing them and their goods off
the ship when in harbor. It wasn’t condu-
cive to discipline.
“But the Old Man didn’t venture to kick
really hard until Mac went ashore at
some God-forsaken place and, in a moment
of mad enthusiasm, stole the sacred monkey
out of one of the filthy temples. Heaven
only knows how he managed he came
off in the afternoon in one of the native boats
with the little grey beast blinking under his
coat, and hurried down to his quarters to
hide it.
“They couldn’t have missed it for some
time, because no fuss was made until a
couple of hours later, when the Old Man
was taking it easy in his cane-lounge on the
bridge. Quite suddenly (it was a still, wind-
less evening) the dirty town across the bay
seemed to boil over. Tom-toms and things
droned and buzzed and boomed, and under-
neath it all we could hear the angry murmur
of a crowd. I tell you I felt glad we were
out in the stream, instead of Being tied up
to a wharf.
“‘What will they be up to nowT growled
the Old Man. ‘ls it another releegious
festival, think you?’ Poor old chap! he
hadn’t seen the Chief come aboard and didn t
know what was in store for him.
“‘I don’t think so, sir. Perhaps it s a
Avedding.’
“‘Mebbe—mebbe! But they must be
very popular people, I’m tliinkin’, if they re
making all that fuss over them.’
“The racket, instead of subsiding, and
staying up toAvn where it had started,
seemed to increase and come nearer, and
presently I saw a lot of niggers swarming
on to the beach. The Old Man picked up
his binoculars quite casually; but as soon
as he had the picnic focussed I saw him
stiffen. ‘They’re very excited, yon!’ he
muttered. Then he wheeled on me. ‘Has
Mr. McClyde been ashore this afternoon?’
“ ‘Yes. sir,’ I said. .
“ ‘An’ did he bring anything with him ..
“It was no good lying. ‘Yes, sir. I think
he had a monkey with him.’
“The old man fairly leapt out of his
chair. ‘A monkey! It sno ordinary mon-
key they’re makin’ that fuss about!....
Good God ! I believe it’s the sacred monkey
he’s got! Tell him to hide the d- —d thing at
once, and see me here on the bridge. Here,
and tell him to bring his gun with him.
And do you get mine and your own.’
“As I hurried down the ladder he picked
up his glasses again and levelled them at
the beach, Avhere balf the population seemed
to be falling into boats.
“I soon rooted out old McClyde Qvhom I
had disco\ T ered gloating o\ r er his prize), and
he got his gun and accompanied me back to
the bridge looking as guilty as a schoolboy.
“ ‘Where did you get the monkey?’ shouted
the Old Man, as soon as Ave came in sight.
“ ‘I took it from a dirty hole of a temple
because they wouldn’t sell it to me,’ growled
the Chief.
“ ‘Man, did ye not know the thing Avas
sacred V
“ ‘Oh, I didn’t think they’d mind !’
“‘Didn’t think they’d mind!’ shouted the
Old Man. ‘Look yonder!’ and he pointed
to as nasty a collection of lunatics as you
could wish to see. They were paddling like
mad for the ship—a Avhole procession of ’em,
and you could lniA’e heard the yells for
miles.
“‘I can’t make up my mind,’ says the
skipper presently. ‘I don’t know whether to
give the thing back to them or throw it into
the fires and tell them Ave never saw it!’
“ ‘No monkey of mine goes into the fires,’
growled the Chief.
“ ‘lt’s their monkey,’ says the skipper. ‘lff
they don’t get it back neither you nor I will
ever be safe in this hole again. You took
their god, Mr. McClyde.’
“‘lt’s my- god now!’
“ ‘l’m responsible for the safety of this
ship, Mr. McClyde. These niggers will be
alongside and aboard of us in another minute
or so. You’ll bring up that monkey, Mr.
McClyde.’
“Old McClyde grumbled something in his
beard AA r hich we couldn’t hear; but the Old
Man guessed it was a refusal, and it broke
him up. Before I could offer to go and get
the thing the poor old skipper Avas actually
shaking his fist in McClyde’s face. ‘Ye’ve
been bringin’ yer crawlin’ rubbish aboard my
ship for years, but up to iioav it’s been only
rubbish; an’, so far as I k- ow. it was your
own dirty property —b light and paid for.
But this time ye’ve stolen a monkey-god in
a port that yer owners want to use, an’ I’m
not goin' to stand by an’ see ye do it. Now,
Mr. McClyde, will ye go an’ get tha brute —-
an’ bring a bag an’ a’ here with you to put
it OA r erside in. Hurry, man, or they’ll be on
top of us all!’ And they were, too. Luck-
ily the accommodation-ladder had been
hauled up, and the ship being in light trim
she stood up like a house on the Avater. The
yelling savages clustered round us and stood
up cursing in their boats while we looked
down and fired a shot or two to discourage
them.
“ ‘What d’ye want?' howled the Old Man
when they were a bit out of breath, and it
Avas possible for a human being to make
himself heard.
“An ugly brute stood up in a canoe and
made a long, rambling, passionate speech
which seemed to be half-threats and half-pro-
mises. The only understandable point he
made was that the monkey-god must be
restored to them.
“ ‘Will ye go away if I give ye the mon-
key?’ yells the skipper.
“Yes, they’ll go away and pray for every-
body on board for ever !
“ ‘Bight, then !. . . .Over with that monkey,
Mr. McClyde!’ And down came a closed
bag on a line with something kicking and
wriggling inside it.
“You heard such a row as those
brutes kicked up when they saw their
treasure coming back to them. The High
Priest —or Avhoever he Avas —took the bag
with his own hands, and the other boats
crowded round as close as they could get.
But that Priest wasn’t taking any more
chances Avith his live joss; once he got it
safely in his arms he sat down in his dinghy
and told his men to pull for the shore—
which they did with all the other boats pelt-
ing in their wake. In a minute or so they
were all gone and half-way to the beach.
“ ‘And now, Mr. McClyde—now that
they’ve gone, and we’re safe,’ said the Old
Man turning to the Chief—‘l hope this’ll
be a lesson to ye.’
“ ‘lt will!’ says the Chief Avith a queer
smile. ‘lt will be a lesson to me and it will
be a lesson to them. That’s the cook’s cat
they’ve got in the bag.’ ”
